Eliminating State and Body Health
Sanusi refutes state and health as the distinguishing attribute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the distinguishing attribute cannot be a state (ḥāl) or health of the body, and must be an accident (ʿaraḍ).

Core Idea
A state cannot explain the distinction without infinite regress, and health remains even in involuntary movements.

Sanusi continues his investigation by examining two more potential candidates for the distinguishing attribute: the state (ḥāl) and the health of the body. He shows why both fail, narrowing the options to a specific type of accident.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic and translation carefully. Focus on the arguments against state and health.
Note the technical term ḥāl (state) which is a non-subsistent attribute in Ashʿarī thought.
2
Understand the argument against state
Sanusi argues that a state cannot subsist by itself; if it were the distinguishing factor, it would need another state, leading to infinite regress. This is a classic argument.
Infinite regress (tasalsul) is considered impossible in kalam.
3
Understand the argument against health
Health of the body is present even when someone else moves your hand involuntarily, yet the distinction still holds. Therefore health cannot be the cause.
This shows the attribute must be something that varies between voluntary and involuntary cases.
4
Conclusion: it must be an accident
Having eliminated state and health, Sanusi concludes that the attribute is an accident (ʿaraḍ), which subsists in the subject.
This is a crucial narrowing step in the proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
دليل إثبات القدرة الحادثة

وتقرير الدليل الذي أشرنا إليه لإثبات القدرة الحادثة : أنا نفرض حركتين متحدتين في الجهة والحيز، إلا أن إحداهما ضرورية والأخرى مكتسبة، فلا شك أنا نجد تفرقة ضرورية بين هاتين الحركتين، ويبطل رجوع التفرقة إلى نفس الحركتين؛ لتماثلهما، وإلى ذات المتحرك؛ لأن معقولها في الحالين واحد، فتعين أن ترجع التفرقة إلى صفة زائدة في المتحرك

ثم يبطل رجوعها إلى حال؛ لأن الحال لا تطرأ بمجردها على الجوهر؛ لأن الحال لا يصح أن تعقل على حيالها، وإلا لزم أن تتميز بحال أخرى تقوم بها، ثم حال حالها كذلك، ويلزم التسلسل. ويبطل رجوعها إلى صحة البنية؛ لأنها غير مفقودة في حال حركة الاضطرار؛ وهي حال كون غيره محركا يده مع وجدان التفرقة

فتعين أن تكون تلك الصفة عرضا

ثم لا يخلو: إما أن يكون مما يشترط في ثبوته الحياة أو لا، والثاني باطل؛ لأنه لا تعلق له بالحركة؛ كالألوان والطعوم والروائح، ولأنه مشترك بين الحركتين، والمشترك بين شيئين لا يفرق به بينهما، فتعين الأول؛ وهو ما يشترط في ثبوته الحياة.

ثم يبطل كونه علما أو حياة أو كلاما؛ لوجود الكل مع ثبوت الحركتين ونفيهما، ويبطل كونه إرادة؛ لوجود التفرقة بين الحركتين حال الذهول، فتعين أن يكون عرضا له نسبة وتعلق ما بالحركة؛ وهو الذي سميناه قدرة، وإن اختلفنا نحن والمعتزلة في أنها من الصفات المؤثرة أم لا، مع الاتفاق على أنها من الصفات المتعلقة

وتعبيرنا في أصل العقيدة بـ (حركة الاختيار) معناه: الحركة التي من شأنها أن يتعلق بها الاختيار، وإلا فالفعل المكتسب قد يقع بغير اختيار، وذلك حيث يقع مع الذهول والغفلة، ومع ذلك يحصل الفرق بينه وبين حركة الاضطرار، ولهذا لو عبر في الحركة الثانية بحركة الاكتساب بدلا عن حركة الاختيار.. لكان أحسن
Proof of the existence of the created power. And the explanation of the proof we referred to for establishing the created power: we posit two movements unified in direction and locus, except that one of them is necessary and the other is acquired. There is no doubt that we necessarily find a distinction (tafriqa) between these two movements. It is invalid that the distinction be attributed to the two movements themselves due to their similarity, and to the essence of the mover because its conceptual meaning in both cases is one. So it is determined that the distinction be attributed to an additional attribute in the mover. Then it is invalid that it be attributed to a state (ḥāl), because a state does not occur by itself upon the substance, because a state cannot be conceived by itself, otherwise it would necessarily require another state subsisting in it, and then that state would likewise have a state, leading to infinite regress. It is invalid that it be attributed to health of the body, because it is not absent in the case of the involuntary movement, namely, when someone else moves his hand along with his perception of the distinction. Then it becomes determined that that attribute is an accident (ʿaraḍ). Then it is not free: either it is something whose existence requires life or not. The second is false because it has no connection to movement, like colors, tastes, and smells, and because it is common between the two movements, and what is common between two things does not distinguish between them. So the first is determined, i.e., that whose existence requires life. Then it is invalid that it be knowledge, life, or speech, because all exist with both the affirmation and negation of the two movements. And it is invalid that it be will, because the distinction between the two movements exists in the state of heedlessness. So it becomes determined that it is an accident that has a relation and some connection to movement, and it is what we have called power (qudra), even though we and the Muʿtazila differ as to whether it is among the effective attributes or not, while agreeing that it is among the related attributes. And our expression in the original creed as 'movement of choice' (ḥarakat al-ikhtiyār) means the movement that by its nature choice attaches to. Otherwise, the acquired act may occur without choice, i.e., where it occurs with heedlessness and inattention, yet the distinction between it and the involuntary movement still obtains. For this reason, if one were to express the second movement as 'movement of acquisition' (ḥarakat al-iktisāb) instead of 'movement of choice,' it would be better.
Source pages p. 453

Commentary
This passage is a detailed rational proof (dalīl) for the existence of a created power (qudra ḥāditha) in the human agent, which is the basis for distinguishing voluntary (acquired) actions from involuntary (necessary) ones. The author, Sanusi, begins by setting up the problem: we consider two movements that are identical in direction and locus, but one is necessary (like the tremor of a hand) and the other is acquired (like a deliberate movement). We necessarily perceive a distinction between them. This distinction cannot be attributed to the movements themselves because they are similar; nor to the essence of the mover because his essence is conceived the same in both cases. Therefore, the distinction must be due to an additional attribute (ṣifa zāʾida) in the mover. Next, the author refutes the possibility that this attribute is a state (ḥāl). A state, in Ashʿarī ontology, cannot subsist by itself; if it needed another state to distinguish it, that would lead to infinite regress. Also, it cannot be the health of the body, because even in involuntary movement the body is healthy. Thus the attribute must be an accident (ʿaraḍ) — a non-substance entity. He then restricts possibilities: either the accident requires life for its existence or not. Those that do not require life, like colors, tastes, and smells, have no relation to movement and are common to both types, so they cannot serve as the distinguishing factor. Hence it must be an accident that requires life. Among such accidents, he eliminates knowledge, life, and speech because they can be present even when both movements are absent or present; they do not vary with movement. He also eliminates will (irāda) because the distinction between the two movements persists even in a state of heedlessness (dhuhūl) when there is no will. Therefore, the only remaining candidate is an accident that has a specific relation and connection to movement, which is what we call power (qudra). The author acknowledges a disagreement with the Muʿtazila about whether this power is effective (muʾaththira) in causing the movement, but both agree it is a related attribute (ṣifa mutaʿalliqa). Finally, Sanusi clarifies a terminological point: in his creedal work, he uses 'movement of choice' (ḥarakat al-ikhtiyār) to mean 'movement to which choice is naturally attached,' not that choice always accompanies it, because acquired acts can occur without choice (e.g., in heedlessness). He suggests that 'movement of acquisition' (ḥarakat al-iktisāb) might be a better term. This whole argument is a classic kalam proof for the reality of human capacity (kasb) as distinct from divine creation.

صحة البنية
تسلسل
عرض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The distinguishing attribute could be a state (ḥāl) or the health of the body.
Correction: Sanusi shows both are impossible: state leads to infinite regress, health is present in both cases.

Checkpoint
Why does Sanusi reject the idea that the attribute is a state?
A) States are not real attributes.
B) States would require another state leading to infinite regress.
C) States are not accidents.
D) States require life.

Key Takeaway
The distinguishing attribute cannot be a state or health, so it must be an accident.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you move your hand voluntarily, do you recognize that it is an accident that distinguishes this movement from an involuntary one? Does this affect how you perceive your own actions and their accountability before Allah? How can you use this distinction to become more mindful of your voluntary deeds?
?
The argument eliminates health and state as the basis for distinction. If health is not the cause, then your physical state does not determine moral responsibility. Are there areas of your life where you have used excuses like 'I'm not in the right state' or 'my health prevents me' to avoid actions you know you should do? What will you do about that starting today?
?
Sanusi's reasoning leads to an inevitable conclusion: the distinguishing attribute must be an accident. Do you truly submit to rational proofs in your faith, or do you sometimes resist conclusions that challenge your preconceptions? What does this teach you about the relationship between intellect and revelation?

Identifying the Attribute as Power
Sanusi identifies the attribute as power and comments on terminology.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi determines the distinguishing attribute must be a power (qudra) and clarifies the terminology of 'movement of choice'.

Core Idea
The attribute must be an accident requiring life, related to movement, and not knowledge, life, speech, or will—hence it is power.

Sanusi now narrows down the type of accident. He argues it must require life, and then systematically eliminates other mental attributes until only power remains. He also clarifies a terminological point about 'movement of choice'.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
This is the longest passage. Read it carefully, following the elimination steps.
Pay attention to the list of candidates: knowledge, life, speech, will.
2
Follow the elimination of candidates
First, eliminate accidents that do not require life (e.g., colors). Then, among life-requiring accidents, eliminate knowledge, life, speech (constant), and will (absent in heedlessness).
Note the argument against will: the distinction exists even in heedlessness (dhuhūl).
3
Understand the identification as power
The only remaining accident is one with a relation to movement, which is called power (qudra). Note the agreement with Muʿtazila on it being a related attribute.
Sanusi mentions a theological disagreement about effectiveness, but that is not central here.
4
Reflect on the terminological clarification
Sanusi corrects the phrase 'movement of choice' to 'movement of acquisition' because acquired acts can occur without conscious choice.
This shows precision in theological language.

Proof of the existence of the created power. And the explanation of the proof we referred to for establishing the created power: we posit two movements unified in direction and locus, except that one of them is necessary and the other is acquired. There is no doubt that we necessarily find a distinction (tafriqa) between these two movements. It is invalid that the distinction be attributed to the two movements themselves due to their similarity, and to the essence of the mover because its conceptual meaning in both cases is one. So it is determined that the distinction be attributed to an additional attribute in the mover. Then it is invalid that it be attributed to a state (ḥāl), because a state does not occur by itself upon the substance, because a state cannot be conceived by itself, otherwise it would necessarily require another state subsisting in it, and then that state would likewise have a state, leading to infinite regress. It is invalid that it be attributed to health of the body, because it is not absent in the case of the involuntary movement, namely, when someone else moves his hand along with his perception of the distinction. Then it becomes determined that that attribute is an accident (ʿaraḍ). Then it is not free: either it is something whose existence requires life or not. The second is false because it has no connection to movement, like colors, tastes, and smells, and because it is common between the two movements, and what is common between two things does not distinguish between them. So the first is determined, i.e., that whose existence requires life. Then it is invalid that it be knowledge, life, or speech, because all exist with both the affirmation and negation of the two movements. And it is invalid that it be will, because the distinction between the two movements exists in the state of heedlessness. So it becomes determined that it is an accident that has a relation and some connection to movement, and it is what we have called power (qudra), even though we and the Muʿtazila differ as to whether it is among the effective attributes or not, while agreeing that it is among the related attributes. And our expression in the original creed as 'movement of choice' (ḥarakat al-ikhtiyār) means the movement that by its nature choice attaches to. Otherwise, the acquired act may occur without choice, i.e., where it occurs with heedlessness and inattention, yet the distinction between it and the involuntary movement still obtains. For this reason, if one were to express the second movement as 'movement of acquisition' (ḥarakat al-iktisāb) instead of 'movement of choice,' it would be better.

Commentary
This passage is a detailed rational proof (dalīl) for the existence of a created power (qudra ḥāditha) in the human agent, which is the basis for distinguishing voluntary (acquired) actions from involuntary (necessary) ones. The author, Sanusi, begins by setting up the problem: we consider two movements that are identical in direction and locus, but one is necessary (like the tremor of a hand) and the other is acquired (like a deliberate movement). We necessarily perceive a distinction between them. This distinction cannot be attributed to the movements themselves because they are similar; nor to the essence of the mover because his essence is conceived the same in both cases. Therefore, the distinction must be due to an additional attribute (ṣifa zāʾida) in the mover. Next, the author refutes the possibility that this attribute is a state (ḥāl). A state, in Ashʿarī ontology, cannot subsist by itself; if it needed another state to distinguish it, that would lead to infinite regress. Also, it cannot be the health of the body, because even in involuntary movement the body is healthy. Thus the attribute must be an accident (ʿaraḍ) — a non-substance entity. He then restricts possibilities: either the accident requires life for its existence or not. Those that do not require life, like colors, tastes, and smells, have no relation to movement and are common to both types, so they cannot serve as the distinguishing factor. Hence it must be an accident that requires life. Among such accidents, he eliminates knowledge, life, and speech because they can be present even when both movements are absent or present; they do not vary with movement. He also eliminates will (irāda) because the distinction between the two movements persists even in a state of heedlessness (dhuhūl) when there is no will. Therefore, the only remaining candidate is an accident that has a specific relation and connection to movement, which is what we call power (qudra). The author acknowledges a disagreement with the Muʿtazila about whether this power is effective (muʾaththira) in causing the movement, but both agree it is a related attribute (ṣifa mutaʿalliqa). Finally, Sanusi clarifies a terminological point: in his creedal work, he uses 'movement of choice' (ḥarakat al-ikhtiyār) to mean 'movement to which choice is naturally attached,' not that choice always accompanies it, because acquired acts can occur without choice (e.g., in heedlessness). He suggests that 'movement of acquisition' (ḥarakat al-iktisāb) might be a better term. This whole argument is a classic kalam proof for the reality of human capacity (kasb) as distinct from divine creation.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The distinguishing attribute could be will or knowledge.
Correction: Sanusi eliminates will because the distinction exists even in heedlessness, and knowledge/life/speech because they are constant.

Checkpoint
Why does Sanusi eliminate will as the distinguishing attribute?
A) Will is not an accident.
B) Will requires life.
C) The distinction persists even in heedlessness.
D) Will is not related to movement.

Key Takeaway
The only plausible attribute that distinguishes voluntary from involuntary actions is a created power, an accident that requires life and is related to movement.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of an action you performed today that you consider 'voluntary'—was it truly chosen, or was it done in a state of heedlessness? How does Sanusi's distinction between 'movement of choice' and 'movement of acquisition' force you to re-evaluate the sincerity of your everyday actions, especially in worship? What specific step will you take tomorrow to ensure your actions are more deliberately chosen before Allah?
?
Sanusi eliminates will (irāda) as the distinguishing attribute because the distinction persists even without conscious will. Reflect on a moment when you sinned while 'not really paying attention'—were you still accountable? If the distinction between voluntary and involuntary actions remains even in heedlessness, what does that say about your responsibility for actions done in spiritual apathy? How will you combat heedlessness in your prayer starting today?
?
The passage notes that the attribute of power (qudra) is created and related to actions. If your every movement is powered by a created accident from Allah, how does that change your sense of dependence on Him? When you next move your hand to do a good deed, pause and acknowledge that the power to act is not your own. What is one specific moment tomorrow where you will consciously recall that your ability to move is a gift from Allah, not a product of your own self?

Rational Judgments

Refuting Wrong Interpretations of Kasb
Sanusi refutes three interpretations of the effect of acquired power and affirms the Sunni meaning of kasb.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three incorrect interpretations of kasb that Sanusi refutes and the correct Sunni meaning of the term.

Core Idea
Kasb means concurrent creation, not any effect from the servant's power.

Sanusi continues his discussion of kasb by refuting three false understandings of how the acquired power relates to the act. Each interpretation is examined and shown to be either corrupt or heretical. The passage then reaffirms the true Sunni position: kasb is merely the concurrent occurrence of the act with the power, without any causal influence from the power.

Guided Path
1
Read the full passage
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it. Then read the translation. Let the flow of Sanusi’s refutations sink in before dissecting them.
Notice that the passage begins by recalling a previous rejection of a statement from the Qadi and the Teacher.
2
Identify the three interpretations
The passage targets three distinct views: (1) the power has an effect but only by Allah’s will (attributed to Imām al-Ḥaramayn), (2) the power has independent effect (the Qadariyya view), and (3) any meaning that implies a real effect (the misguided understanding of kasb). Find each one and see how Sanusi refutes it.
3
Understand the refutation of the first interpretation
Sanusi says this interpretation is corrupt and branches from Qadariyya. He adds that even if a great scholar like Imām al-Ḥaramayn held it, his slip is not to be followed in fundamentals of religion.
This shows the gravity of the error: even a scholar’s mistake cannot be used as evidence in creed.
4
Understand the refutation of the second and third interpretations
The second interpretation (independent effect) is called the very doctrine of Qadariyya. The third is the correct meaning of kasb: no effect at all. Sanusi explains his grammatical choice to emphasize exclusivity.
The phrase 'about the attachment' is placed early to stress that kasb only means that.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السنة، وتقدَّمَ إنكارُ الشريف شارحِ الأسرار العقليَّةِ عن صدور هذا القولِ مِنَ القاضي والأستاذ وإنْ أرادَ: أَنَّها تؤثر في وجودِ المقدور، لكن بمشيئة الله تعالى لا على الاستقلال كما يُحكى عن إمام الحرمين في آخرِ أمرِه.. فقد تقدَّمَ أيضاً فساد هذا القولِ، وتشعُبُهُ مِنْ مذهب القدرية مجوس هذهِ الأمة، والظنُّ بالإمامِ رضيَ اللهُ عنهُ أنَّه لا يرضى بمثل هذا القول وعلى تقدير أن يكون صدرَ منه - ولا حول ولا قوَّةَ إلا بالله - فزلَّةُ العالم لا يجوزُ أنْ يُقلَّدَ فيها في الفروع، فكيف بالعقائدِ أصول الدين؟! وإنْ أرادَ: أَنَّ القدرة الحادثة خلقها الله تعالى للعبدِ ومَلَّكَ لَهُ أَنْ يفعل المقدور بها كيف شاء على سبيل الاستقلال.. فهذا عين مذهب القدرية مجوس هذهِ الأُمَّةِ وإنَّما مراد أهل السنَّةِ بالكسب: ما أشرتُ إليه في أصل العقيدة؛ فقولي: (عن تعلَّقِ) يتعلَّقُ بـ (عبَّرَ)، وإنَّما قدَّمْتُ هذا المجرور على عامله لإفادة الحصرِ؛ أي: لا معنى للكسب إلا هذا، لا أنَّ معناه أنَّ للقدرةِ الحادثة تأثيراً ما كما تعتقده الجهلة الضالُّونَ في معنى الكسب الذي هو مذهب أهل السنة
the [true way]; and the rejection by al-Sharīf, commentator of al-Asrār al-‘Aqliyya, of this statement from the Qadi and the Teacher has preceded. And if he meant: that it (the acquired power) has an effect in the existence of the predetermined thing, but by the will of Allah Most High, not independently—as is related of Imām al-Ḥaramayn at the end of his life—then the corruption of this statement has also preceded, and its branching from the doctrine of the Qadariyya, the Magians of this Ummah. And the assumption about the Imam (may Allah be pleased with him) is that he would not be pleased with such a statement. And assuming it issued from him—and there is no power nor strength except with Allah—then the slip of a scholar is not to be followed in subsidiary matters, how then in beliefs, the fundamentals of religion?! And if he meant: that the acquired power is created by Allah Most High for the servant and He made him master to do the predetermined thing with it however he wills, on the basis of independence—then this is precisely the doctrine of the Qadariyya, the Magians of this Ummah. And the intended meaning of Ahl al-Sunna by ‘acquisition’ (kasb) is only what I indicated in the original creed; so my saying: ‘about the attachment’ is attached to ‘he expressed’, and I only placed this genitive phrase before its governor to indicate exclusivity; that is, there is no meaning for kasb except this, not that its meaning is that the acquired power has some effect as the ignorant misguided ones believe in the meaning of kasb, which is the doctrine of Ahl al-Sunna.
Source pages p. 457

Commentary
This passage refutes three possible interpretations of the effect of the acquired power (qudra ḥāditha) on the predetermined act (maqdūr). The author first notes that the rejection of a certain statement by al-Sharīf, the commentator of al-Asrār al-‘Aqliyya, from the Qadi and the Teacher has already preceded. He then considers: (1) If one means that the acquired power has some effect in the existence of the act but only by Allah’s will, not independently—as is attributed to Imām al-Ḥaramayn at the end of his life—then this statement has already been shown to be corrupt and to branch from the doctrine of the Qadariyya, the Magians of this Ummah. Even if such a slip occurred from the Imam, it is not to be followed in subsidiary matters, let alone in fundamentals of religion. (2) If one means that Allah creates the acquired power for the servant and gives him mastery to perform the act with it independently, then this is precisely the doctrine of the Qadariyya. (3) The correct meaning intended by Ahl al-Sunna for ‘acquisition’ (kasb) is only what the author indicated in the original creed: that the act occurs concurrently with the acquired power without any effect from it. The author then explains his own phrasing: ‘about the attachment’ (عن تعلَّقِ) is attached to ‘he expressed’ (عبَّرَ), and he placed the genitive phrase before its governor to indicate exclusivity, i.e., kasb only means that, and not that the acquired power has any effect, as the ignorant misguided ones believe.

Key Terms
الكسب
القدرة الحادثة
المقدور
القدرية
الأستاذ
القاضي
إمام الحرمين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that Ahl al-Sunna's 'kasb' implies a real effect of the acquired power on the act.
Correction: Sanusi clarifies that kasb merely means the act occurs concurrently with the power, without any causal influence. Any meaning that assigns effect to the power is either corrupt or heretical.

Checkpoint
According to this passage, what does Sanusi say about the interpretation that the acquired power has an effect on the act by Allah's will, not independently?
A) It is the correct view of Ahl al-Sunna.
B) It is an interpretation attributed to Imām al-Ḥaramayn and is corrupt.
C) It is the view of the Jabriyya.
D) It is the same as the Mu'tazila view.

Key Takeaway
The correct Sunni kasb is that the act is created by Allah and occurs concurrently with the acquired power, without any effect from the power.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you achieve something good, do you ever secretly attribute part of the success to your own ability and effort, feeling a sense of pride that is not purely gratitude to Allah? How does the truth that your power has zero effect on the act challenge that feeling? Will you now repent from that hidden shirk?
?
Sanusi warns that even a great scholar's slip is not to be followed in beliefs. Are there any teachers, scholars, or influencers whose opinions you have accepted without checking against the Quran and Sunnah in matters of creed? What step will you take today to correct that?
?
If you truly internalized that your will and power are created by Allah and have no independent effect, what would change in how you make dua for your daily actions? Would you pray with more desperation and reliance, or would you become passive? Examine your heart: does your dua reveal a hidden belief in your own efficacy?

Two Objections Against Qadariyya: Possible Becoming Impossible
Sanusi enumerates two objections and explains the first.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two additional objections Sanusi raises against the Qadariyya, and the detailed reasoning of the first objection regarding the possible becoming impossible.

Core Idea
If a possible act becomes impossible for God's power, then possibility itself is contradicted.

Sanusi has already demonstrated that the Qadariyya position leads to the incapacity of God's eternal power. Now he introduces two further problems. The first argues that their view forces a possible thing to become impossible—a logical contradiction. Let's read the passage to see how this argument works.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the two objections
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the two problems Sanusi enumerates: 'awd al-mumkin mustaḥīlan and tarjīḥ al-marjūḥ.
The first objection is explained in detail; the second is not elaborated in this passage.
2
Understand the premises of the first objection
The argument relies on two key premises: (1) the act before originated power is possible, and (2) every possible is within God's power.
3
Follow the Qadariyya claim and the resulting contradiction
The Qadariyya say that when God creates an originated power for the servant, the act becomes impossible for God's eternal power. See how this leads to the conclusion that a possible has become impossible.
This is the contradiction Sanusi uses to refute the Qadariyya.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فيه محذورانِ آخرانِ زيادةً على ما لزمهم مِنْ عجز القدرةِ القديمة على ما سبق في دليل التمانعِ : أحدُهُما : لزوم عود الممكن مستحيلاً الثاني : ترجيح المرجوح
وتقرير الأول : أنْ نقولَ : فعلُ العبدِ قبلَ أَنْ تُخلَقَ لهُ القدرة الحادثة ممكن ، وكلُّ ممكن فهو مقدور للباري جلَّ وعلا ، فينتجُ : فعلُ العبدِ مقدورٌ للباري تعالى ؛ فإذا خلق الله سبحانه للعبد قدرةً حادثة . . قالَ القدريَّةُ : إِنَّهُ يزولُ حينَئِذٍ عن الفعلِ ما ثبتَ لَهُ مِنْ إمكانِ أَنْ يُوجِدَ بالقدرة القديمة ، وصارَ إذ ذاك مستحيل الوجودِ بها ، فقد لزمَ أَنَّ ما كانَ ممكناً باعتبار القدرةِ القديمةِ صارَ مستحيلاً بالنسبة إليها !
In it are two additional problems beyond what they necessarily incurred from the incapacity of the eternal power regarding what preceded in the proof of mutual exclusion: one of them: the necessity of the possible returning as impossible; the second: preponderating the less likely. And the explanation of the first: that we say: the act of the servant before the originated power is created for him is possible, and everything possible is within the power (maqdūr) of the Creator (al-Bārī), may He be glorified and exalted; so it follows: the act of the servant is within the power of the Creator, exalted be He. So when Allah, glorified be He, creates an originated power for the servant... the Qadariyya say: then there ceases from the act what was established for it of the possibility of being brought into existence by the eternal power, and it becomes at that moment impossible of existence through it. So it necessarily follows that what was possible by consideration of the eternal power has become impossible in relation to it!
Source pages p. 460

Commentary
This passage is a statement of two objections against the Qadariyya (who claim that human acts are not created by God) followed by an explanation of the first. The author begins by saying that besides the already established problem of the incapacity of the eternal power (from the earlier proof of mutual exclusion, dālīl al-tamānu‘), there are two additional difficulties. The first is the necessity of the possible becoming impossible (luzūm ‘awd al-mumkin mustaḥīlan), and the second is the preponderating of the less likely (tarjīḥ al-marjūḥ). Then he proceeds to elaborate the first: before a human acquires an originated power (qudra ḥāditha), his act is a pure possibility—it could be or not be. And every possible is within the power of God (maqdūr li’l-Bārī). Therefore, the act is initially within God's power. However, when God creates an originated power for the human, the Qadariyya claim that the act ceases to be possible for God's eternal power and becomes impossible for it. This implies that an act that was possible for God's power becomes impossible—a change from possibility to impossibility, which is a contradiction.

Key Terms
محذوران
عود الممكن مستحيلاً
ترجيح المرجوح
القدرة القديمة
القدرة الحادثة
ممكن
مقدور

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Qadariyya believe that when a human acquires an originated power, the act is no longer within God's power and becomes impossible for God.
Correction: Sanusi argues that this would mean a possible thing becomes impossible, which is a logical contradiction. The act remains within God's power at all times.

Checkpoint
According to the first objection, what happens to the servant's act when the originated power is created?
A) It becomes necessary
B) It remains possible for the eternal power
C) The Qadariyya claim it becomes impossible for the eternal power
D) It becomes impossible for both powers

Key Takeaway
The Qadariyya view leads to the contradiction that a possible act becomes impossible for God's eternal power.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you live as if your actions are truly independent of God's power, forgetting that before your ability existed, they were entirely within His domain? What would change if you internalized this?
?
When you succeed in an action, do you attribute it to your own power or to God's? The Qadariyya view assigns independence to human power—does your heart ever lean that way?
?
This lesson shows that limiting God's power leads to contradiction. In what subtle ways might you be limiting God's power in your daily life, by relying on your own strength rather than His?

Refuting the Accidental Impossibility Objection
Sanusi refutes an objection about accidental impossibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Sanusi rejects the claim that the impossibility of an act for God's power could be accidental, and how he demonstrates it must be intrinsic.

Core Idea
An accidental cause cannot excuse impossibility without a sound basis.

In this passage, Sanusi anticipates a counterargument from the Qadariyya: they might argue that the impossibility of an act for God's power is not intrinsic but accidental, caused by the originated power attaching to the act. Sanusi dismantles this by showing that no valid cause for such accidental impossibility exists, so it must be intrinsic according to their own reasoning. He then argues that the originated power is not fit to be a preventer.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the first part of the Arabic (لا يقال... إلى آخر) and its translation. Understand the Qadariyya's claim: the impossibility is accidental due to the involvement of the originated power.
Notice they admit potency in the act, but claim God cannot also bring it about.
2
Explore Sanusi's refutation
Read from 'لأنَّا نقول' onwards. See how Sanusi denies that a valid cause exists, then concludes the impossibility must be intrinsic. Then follows the argument that the originated power is not a valid preventer.
The key move: no cause for accidental impossibility means it's intrinsic.
3
Reflect on the rational principle
Consider the principle: if an accidental attribute has no discernible cause, it reverts to being intrinsic. Also note the logical structure: objection → denial of cause → stronger conclusion.
This refutation protects the first proof from being undermined.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لا يُقالُ : استحالته عارضة لسبب ؛ وهو تعلق القدرة الحادثة بهِ ، فاستحال أنْ يكونَ الفعلُ موجوداً بقدرتين ، والاستحالة العارضة لا تقدح في الإمكان الذاتي لأنَّا نقولُ : لم يظهر لهذهِ الاستحالة سبب يصح ، فتعيَّنَ على زعمهم أن تكونَ ذاتيَّة ؛ لأنَّ القدرة الحادثة التي جعلوها مانعةً مِنْ تعلق القدرة القديمة بالفعل لا تصلحُ أن تكونَ مانعةً مِنْ ذلكَ ، بل الذي يصلح عقلاً ونقلاً العكس .
It cannot be said: its impossibility is accidental due to a cause, which is the attachment of the originated power to it, so it is impossible that the act exist by two powers, and the accidental impossibility does not undermine the intrinsic possibility; because we say: no valid cause has appeared for this impossibility, so it necessarily follows, according to their claim, that it is intrinsic; because the originated power which they made a preventer from the attachment of the eternal power to the act is not fit to be a preventer of that, rather that which is fit, rationally and textually, is the opposite.
Source pages p. 460

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer structure within the refutation of the Qadariyya. The author anticipates a possible counterargument: one might say that the impossibility of the act for God's eternal power is accidental (ʿāriḍa) because it is caused by the attachment of the originated power to the act—it is impossible for one act to be produced by two agents simultaneously. Accidental impossibility does not negate the intrinsic possibility (al-imkān al-dhātī) of the act. The author responds by denying that the originated power serves as a valid cause for this impossibility. Since no sound cause is apparent, the impossibility must be intrinsic (dhātiyya) according to the Qadariyya's own claim. Then he argues that the originated power is not fit to prevent the eternal power from engaging with the act; rather, rationally and textually, the opposite is true—the eternal power is fit to prevent the originated power. This refutes the attempted objection.

Key Terms
استحالة عارضة
إمكان ذاتي
مانعة
عقلاً ونقلاً

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that accidental impossibility is a valid excuse, leaving the act intrinsically possible and thus not proof of God's exclusive agency.
Correction: Sanusi shows that without a valid cause for the accidental impossibility, it must be intrinsic; thus the act is impossible for God, proving that originated powers are not truly effective.

Checkpoint
Why does the author reject the claim that the impossibility is accidental?
A) Because accidental impossibility never occurs
B) Because the originated power is not a valid cause for prevention
C) Because intrinsic impossibility is stronger
D) Because the act is necessary

Key Takeaway
The originated power cannot prevent the eternal power; the impossibility of the act for God is intrinsic, not accidental.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you treat some created causes as if they can truly prevent God's will from being done in your life? Reflect on a situation where you felt a human limitation made something impossible—does this lesson challenge that feeling? How will you realign your reliance on God's absolute power starting today?
?
The objector claimed an accidental impossibility based on a weak cause. Are there excuses you make for your own failures that you attribute to 'circumstances' without examining their validity? What would it look like to honestly assess whether the cause is truly valid according to this teaching?
?
If the originated power cannot truly prevent God's power, what does that mean for your prayers and dua? Do you pray with full certainty that God's power is unlimited, or do you subconsciously think certain things are impossible for God? Commit to one thing you will pray for this week with complete confidence based on this lesson.

Why reward and punishment are not causally linked to human action
Ahl al-Sunna respond to the objection that human inability entails injustice in reward and punishment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Ahl al-Sunna deny that lack of human causal efficacy leads to injustice in reward and punishment, and how they reinterpret 'cause' as 'sign'.

Core Idea
Actions are signs, not causes, of reward and punishment.

This lesson examines a classic objection against the Ash'ari position on human acquisition. The opponent argues that if humans have no real effect in their actions, then rewarding or punishing them is unjust. Ahl al-Sunna respond by denying the logical entailment and clarifying that reward and punishment are not rationally tied to human causality. Instead, actions are merely signs (amārāt) that Allah uses to indicate one's ultimate state.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Understand the opponent's claim that human inability to affect actions entails that there should be no reward or punishment, just as there is none for colors or other attributes.
Note the analogy used: actions are like colors in having no effect.
2
Identify Ahl al-Sunna's response
They deny the entailment (man' al-mulāzama) outright. This is the core of their defense.
3
Examine their reasoning
They accept that actions have no effect, just like colors, but argue that this does not lead to the opponent's conclusion. Reward and punishment are not based on causal efficacy.
4
Grasp the key distinction
Reward and punishment are based on divine will. Actions are merely signs (amārāt) that Allah has placed as indicators of happiness or misery.
The term 'cause' (sabab) in such contexts is used loosely to mean 'sign'.
5
Review the conclusion
The objection fails because there is no rational connection between causal efficacy and reward/punishment. Allah does as He wills.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأعراضه ، بجامعِ أَنَّ الجميعَ لا أثر له فيه ، فكما أَنَّهُ لا يُتاب ولا يُعاقَبُ على وجودِ ألوانه ووجودِ ذاتِهِ ووجود سائر أجزاء العالم وأعراضه ؛ لكونه لا تأثيرَ لهُ في شيءٍ مِنْ ذلك .. كذلك يلزم ألا يُتابَ ولا يُعاقَبَ على شيءٍ مِنْ أعمالِهِ ؛ لأنَّهُ أيضاً لا تأثيرَ لهُ في شيءٍ منها أصلاً .
أجابَ أهلُ السنَّةِ رضي الله عنهم بمنع الملازمة
•
قولهم في بيانها : إِنَّ الفعل حينئذٍ يصيرُ كاللون وغيرِهِ ممَّا لا تأثير للقدرة الحادثة فيه أصلاً
قلنا : هو كذلك عندَنا مِنْ غيرِ فرقٍ
•
قولهم : فيلزمُ ألا يُثاب عليهِ ولا يُعاقَبُ كما لا يُثاب ولا يُعاقَبُ على الألوان ونحوها ... فعلاً
قلنا : لا ملازمة بين الثواب والعقاب وبين كون سببه للمُكلَّف ، كيف وقد علمتُم مِنْ مذهب خصومكم أنَّ الله تعالى أنْ يُعاقِبَ البريء ويعطي إنعامه للمذنب العاصي ، يفعل ما يشاء ؟! والأفعالُ الواقعة على يد العبدِ أمارات وضعها الشارع على السعادة والشقاوة ، ولو وضعَ غيرَها مِنَ الألوان والطعوم ونحوها أماراتٍ عليهما .. لكانَتْ صالحةً لذلك ، وليسَ للثواب والعقاب علَّةٌ عقليَّةٌ تقتضيهما ، وكلُّ ما أُطلِقَ عليهِ في الشرع أنَّه سبب لهما . . فإنَّما المراد بالسبب الأمارة ، ووقع التسامح بالتعبير عنها بالسبب ؛ إذ لا مشاحة في الألفاظ اللغويَّةِ إِذا فُهِمَتِ المقاصد منها
And His attributes, by the common factor that all of them have no effect in it. For just as He is not rewarded nor punished for the existence of His colors and the existence of His essence and the existence of other parts of the world and its attributes, because he has no effect in any of that... likewise it follows that he should not be rewarded nor punished for any of his actions, because he also has no effect in any of them at all. The Ahl al-Sunna, may Allah be pleased with them, responded by denying the entailment. Their statement in its explanation: Then the action becomes like color and other things in which the originated power has no effect at all. We said: It is so with us without any difference. Their statement: Then it follows that he is not rewarded for it nor punished, just as he is not rewarded nor punished for colors and the like... actually. We said: There is no entailment between reward and punishment and between it being a cause for the obligated [person]. How is it that you have learned from the doctrine of your opponents that Allah the Exalted may punish the innocent and grant His favor to the sinful disobedient? He does what He wills! The actions that occur at the hand of the servant are signs that the Lawgiver has established as indicators of happiness and misery. And if He had established other signs from colors, tastes, and the like as indicators of them, they would have been suitable for that. There is no rational cause for reward and punishment that necessitates them. And everything that is called in the Law a cause for them, the intended meaning of 'cause' is merely a sign. And there is leniency in expression by calling it a cause, for there is no objection in linguistic expressions when the intended meanings are understood.
Source pages p. 463

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer argument. The opponent argues that if humans have no effect in their actions, then similarly they should not be rewarded or punished for them, just as they are not rewarded for their physical attributes. Ahl al-Sunna respond by denying the entailment (man' al-mulāzama). They explain that the denial is twofold: first, they accept that human power has no effect, but that does not lead to the conclusion. Second, they argue that reward and punishment are not rationally necessitated by human causality; rather, they are based on divine will. Actions are merely signs (amārāt) that the Lawgiver has placed as indicators of happiness or misery. The terms 'cause' (sabab) in such contexts is used loosely to mean 'sign'. Thus, the objection fails.

عقاب
ملازمة
أمارة
سبب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That reward and punishment require causal efficacy of human power; otherwise, they are unjust.
Correction: Ahl al-Sunna hold that reward and punishment are not rationally tied to human causality; they are based on divine will, and actions are merely signs that Allah uses to indicate one's state.

Checkpoint
How do Ahl al-Sunna respond to the claim that if humans have no effect in actions, then reward and punishment are unjust?
A) They affirm that humans do have effect.
B) They deny the entailment and explain that actions are signs, not causes.
C) They accept the conclusion and say reward and punishment are arbitrary.
D) They argue that the objection applies only to the Qadariyya.

Key Takeaway
Ahl al-Sunna defend divine justice by denying that reward and punishment are causally tied to human action; actions are merely signs that Allah has placed as indicators of one's ultimate state.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you examine your own good deeds, do you secretly believe they 'earn' you Paradise? How does the teaching that they are mere signs change the way you view your worship—and your hope in Allah's mercy?
?
If your actions are signs, not causes, then what does your current state of obedience or disobedience actually 'signify' about your heart’s orientation toward Allah? Will you honestly read that sign today and let it drive you to repentance or gratitude?
?
Are you willing to accept that Allah may reward or punish as He wills, even when that contradicts your sense of fairness? Does your soul submit to His absolute authority over reward and punishment, or does it still demand a causal explanation that makes you feel in control?

The Servant is Compelled in a Mold of Choice
Sanusi states the compatibilist doctrine and defends it against the praise/blame objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Sanusi's compatibilist doctrine of human action and how he responds to the objection that determinism undermines moral praise and blame.

Core Idea
Humans are compelled yet accountable, a compatibilist mystery.

Sanusi confronts one of the most difficult questions in Islamic theology: if God controls everything, how can we be held responsible? In this passage, he presents his answer in a memorable phrase — 'the servant is compelled in a mold of choice' — and immediately addresses the objection that this makes praise and blame meaningless. As you read, notice how he uses both rational arguments and Quranic authority to defend his position.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Notice the bold opening statement and the objection that follows.
The passage is short but dense; take your time.
2
Identify the objection
The objectors ask: how can a person be praised or blamed for actions not truly his own? They cite Quran 4:165 to argue that determinism would give humans a valid argument against God on Judgment Day.
This objection is a classic challenge to any deterministic view.
3
Examine the response
Sanusi gives two responses: (1) The answer is 'from the meaning of what preceded' (likely an earlier argument), and (2) The objection is invalidated by the creation of motivation and ability and by God's eternal encompassing knowledge.
The second response implies that God's control over the causes of action and His foreknowledge nullify any supposed argument.
4
Understand the conclusion
Sanusi reaffirms that the truth is the servant is compelled in a mold of choice. Therefore, it is appropriate to assign praise and blame, assuming the rational discernment of good and evil.
The phrase 'mold of choice' suggests that our choices are real from our perspective but ultimately determined by God.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
العبد مجبور في قالب مختار
قالوا : كيفَ يُمدَحُ العبدُ أو يُذَمُّ على غيرِ ما فعل ؟! ويلزمُ أنْ يكونَ للعبادِ الحجَّةُ في الآخرة ، وقد قال تعالى : لِئَلَّا يَكُونَ لِلنَّاسِ عَلَى اللَّهِ حُجَّةُ بَعدَ الرُّسُلِ [النساء : ١٦٥] .
قلنا : من معنى ما قبله (١)
وأيضاً : يبطلُ بمسألة خلْقِ الداعي والقدرة ، وبعلمه القديم المحيط بكل شيء
والحقُّ : أنَّ العبد مجبورٌ في قالب مختارِ ، فَحَسُنَ فِيهِ رَعْيُ الأمرين على تقديرِ تسليم أصل التحسين والتقبيح
العقليين .
The servant is compelled in a mold of choice. They said: How is the servant praised or blamed for what he did not do? And it follows that the servants would have an argument against God in the Hereafter, and He said: 'So that people would have no argument against God after the messengers' [An-Nisa 165]. We said: From the meaning of what preceded. And also: It is invalidated by the issue of the creation of motivation and ability, and by His eternal knowledge encompassing everything. And the truth is: The servant is compelled in a mold of choice, so observing both matters is appropriate in it, upon the assumption of conceding the principle of rational discerning of good and evil.
Source pages p. 464

Commentary
This passage presents the doctrine of human action and responds to an objection. It begins with a heading stating that the servant is compelled in a mold of choice, indicating a middle position between compulsion and free will. Then an objection is raised: how can a person be praised or blamed for actions not truly his own? This would give humans a valid argument against God on the Day of Judgment, contradicting the verse from An-Nisa. The author gives a concise response: the answer is from the meaning of what preceded (likely referring to an earlier argument not included here). Additionally, the objection is invalidated by considering that God creates both the motivation (dā'ī) and the ability (qudra), and by God's eternal encompassing knowledge. Finally, the author affirms the truth: the servant is indeed compelled in a mold of choice. Therefore, it is appropriate to observe both praise and blame, assuming the acceptance of the principle of rational discerning of good and evil (taḥsīn wa taqbīḥ). The passage thus establishes a deterministic framework while preserving moral accountability.

قالب مختار
التحسين والتقبيح العقليين
الداعي
القدرة
العلم القديم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The phrase 'compelled in a mold of choice' is misunderstood as pure determinism, denying any human role in action.
Correction: It actually indicates a compatibilist view where actions are determined by God but are experienced as voluntary, preserving moral accountability.

Checkpoint
What is the author's doctrine regarding human action?
A) Humans create their own actions independently
B) Humans are completely compelled with no involvement
C) Humans are compelled in a mold of choice
D) Humans have no role in their actions

Key Takeaway
Sanusi affirms that human actions are determined by God, but moral accountability remains intact, encapsulated in the phrase 'compelled in a mold of choice'.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you make a choice today, do you truly feel it is yours, or do you sense a hand beyond steering you? How does the doctrine of 'compelled in a mold of choice' change how you view your own successes and failures?
?
If you believed that your every action is compelled by God, would you stop striving? Why does that thought disturb you? What does your reaction reveal about your trust in God's wisdom?
?
Can you name a specific sin you committed where you blamed yourself, yet felt God's decree was behind it? How do you reconcile the two? What would it mean to accept both your responsibility and God's control without excuse?

Praise and Blame Do Not Require Agency
Refutes the claim that praise/blame must be for actions created by the praised/blamed.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why praise and blame do not require the agent to have created the action, and how this refutes the Mu'tazilite position.

Core Idea
Praise and blame are based on qualities, not on who created them.

The Mu'tazilites argued that praise and blame must be for actions that the person themselves created. But this is not rationally necessary. Imam al-Sanusi refutes this by showing that we praise beauty, good character, and even inanimate objects for qualities they did not create. This lesson examines his argument and its implications for divine justice.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal translation. Let the author's rhythm come through.
The passage is a refutation of a specific Mu'tazilite argument.
2
Identify the central denial
Find where the author denies the necessary correlation (ملازمة) between praise/blame and the agent being the creator. Understand why this denial is important.
3
Examine the examples
List the examples the author gives: praise for beauty, good character, inanimate objects. Note that in none of these did the praised create the quality.
These examples disprove the Mu'tazilite claim.
4
Understand the definition of praise
Read the author's definition of praise as eulogizing something for its excellences, present or future. See how this definition does not require agency.
5
Reflect on theological significance
Consider how this supports the Ash'ari view that reward and punishment are based on God's decree and signs, not on human creation of actions.
This connects to broader discussions of kasb and divine justice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجواب على نهج ما سبق : أنَّهُ لا ملازمة عقلاً بينَ المدح والذم وبين كون سببهما مخترعاً للممدوح أو المذموم ، والاعتماد في الأحكام العقلية - سيَّما بالنسبة إليهِ جلَّ وعلا - على مُجرَّدِ عُرْفِ اصطلاحي لا ينضبط أمرُهُ . . مِنْ أدلَّ دليل على تناهي القوم في الغباوة ، وكونِ تملكتْ عقولهم ، ولم تتركها أنْ تنفُذُ لمراشدها ، على أنَّا لو لهم الاعتماد في هذهِ المسألةِ على العُرفِ . . لَمَا اقتضى أَنَّ سببَ المدح أو الدم لا بدَّ وأنْ يكونَ فعلاً للممدوح أو المذموم ، كيف وقد تقرر المدحُ بالجمالِ وحسن الخلقِ ونحو ذلكَ ممَّا لا كسبَ للممدوح فيه أصلاً كما تقرَّرَ الدم بأضدادِهِ ، وتقرر مدح الجمادات وذمها كالثياب والأبنية ونحوها باعتبارِ ما اتَّصفَتْ بهِ مِنَ الأوصافِ مَعَ أنَّها لم تفعلها ولم تشعر بها أصلاً ؟! وإذا كان معنى المدح : الثناء على الشيء بما اتَّصفَ بِهِ مِنَ المحاسن حالاً أو مآلاً ، والذمُّ ضدَّ ذلك .. حَسُنَ مِدْحُ مَنْ خَلَقَ اللهُ سبحانه لهم بمخض فضلِهِ وإحسانِهِ أمارات تدلُّ شرعاً على حصولِ الكمالاتِ الأخرويَّةِ لهم ، والمحاسن الجسمانية والروحانية ؛ التي هي ما لا عين رأتْ ، ولا أذن سمعَتْ ، ولا خطر على قلب بشر ، كما يحسنُ ذمُّ مَنِ اتَّصف بأضدادها ، ولا حول ولا قوة إلا بالله .
And the answer in the manner preceding: that there is no necessary correlation intellectually between praise and blame and the cause of them being created by the praised or blamed. And relying in intellectual judgments—especially concerning Him, exalted and majestic—on mere conventional custom that is not regulated is among the strongest evidence of the people's extreme stupidity, and that their intellects have been mastered and not left to attain their guidance. Moreover, if we were to rely on custom in this matter, it would not imply that the cause of praise or blame must necessarily be an act of the praised or blamed. How so, when praise for beauty, good nature, and the like, in which the praised has no acquisition whatsoever, is established, just as blame for their opposites is established? And praise of inanimate objects and blame of them, like clothes, buildings, and the like, is established based on the attributes they possess, even though they did not do them nor were aware of them at all? And if the meaning of praise is: eulogizing something for the excellences it possesses, in respect of a present or future state, and blame is the opposite of that... Then it is good to praise those for whom Allah, exalted, has created—by His pure favor and beneficence—signs that indicate, according to the Sacred Law, the attainment of otherworldly perfections for them, and the bodily and spiritual excellences that no eye has seen, no ear has heard, nor has it occurred to the human heart, just as it is good to blame those who possess their opposites. And there is no power or strength except with Allah.
Source pages p. 465

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Mu'tazilite argument that praise and blame must be for actions that the agent creates. The author denies any necessary intellectual correlation (ملازمة) between praise/blame and the agent being the creator of the praised or blamed matter. He criticizes reliance on mere conventional custom (عرف اصطلاحي). He gives examples: praising beauty, good character, and even inanimate objects, which are not created by them. Then he defines praise as eulogizing something for the excellences it possesses, whether present or future, and blame as its opposite. This supports the Ash'ari position that reward and punishment are based on God's decree and signs (أمارات) He creates, not on human creation of actions.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Praise and blame are only valid for actions that the person themselves created.
Correction: Praise can be for qualities and attributes that are not created by the person, such as beauty or good character, and even for inanimate objects.

Checkpoint
What is the author's main point in this passage?
A) Praise and blame are based on custom.
B) There is no necessary correlation between praise/blame and the agent being the creator.
C) All praise is for actions.
D) Only God deserves praise.

Key Takeaway
Praise and blame are grounded in the qualities of the thing praised, not in whether the agent created those qualities.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you praise someone for their intelligence, kindness, or beauty—do you inwardly acknowledge that these are gifts from Allah, or do you subtly attribute them to the person's own doing? How can you train your heart to see the Giver behind every quality?
?
The passage says it is good to praise people based on signs (amārāt) that Allah creates in them for future perfection. Examine your own life: are you living in a way that those around you see signs of Allah's favor and future hope in you? What quality do you need to cultivate so that your life becomes a beacon of divine mercy?
?
If praise is ultimately for excellences granted by Allah, then all your achievements and good traits are not truly yours. Does that thought humble you or trouble you? Will you today thank Allah for even the smallest good quality you possess, and use it for His sake?

The Slave is Compelled in a Mold of Choice
Sanusi explains how the slave is compelled yet rewarded, citing Quranic evidence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Sunni (Ash'ari) position on human action: that the slave is compelled by God yet appears to act freely, and how this justifies reward and punishment.

Core Idea
The slave is compelled by God but acts within a 'mold of choice' that preserves moral responsibility.

This passage from Sanusi addresses a central puzzle in Islamic theology: if God creates all actions, how can humans be held accountable? Sanusi presents the Ash'ari solution: God's custom creates in the slave the experience of choice, while the act is wholly from God. This lesson unpacks that argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage carefully, then the literal translation. Pay attention to the key phrase 'compelled in a mold of choice'.
2
Identify the central claim
Sanusi states that the truth (al-ḥaqq) is that the slave is compelled in a mold of choice. This is the core Ash'ari formula. Note how he presents it as an answer that harmonizes divine omnipotence with moral accountability.
3
Follow the explanation (wajh)
Sanusi explains that God's custom (ʿādah) is to create in the slave successive wills, powers, and acts in a way that the slave does not feel coerced. Whenever the slave resolves to act, God creates the act and the power for it.
This process applies to both obedience and sin.
4
Examine the Quranic evidence
Sanusi cites verses 18-20 of Surah al-Isra to show that God provides (imdād) based on the slave's will. This provision is termed tawfīq (success) or khidhlān (abandonment).
The verses speak of God providing for both those who desire this world and those who desire the Hereafter.
5
Understand the role of apparent agency
Sanusi concludes that the slave appears (ẓāhir) to be the originator of his action, so much so that imagination and fantasy do not doubt it. However, only Ahl al-Sunnah, with God's support, see the true reality that God is the sole creator.
This corrects the error of those who think the slave creates his own acts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما قولي : ( والحقُّ : أنَّ العبد مجبورٌ في قالب مختار ... ) إلى آخره : فهذا جواب آخرُ يحسنُ الثواب والعقاب على مذهب أهلِ السنَّةِ ، وإن وافقنا المعتزلة على قاعدة التحسين والتقبيح العقليين . ووجْهُ ذلكَ : أَنَّهُ سبحانَهُ لمَّا أجرى عادتهُ بإمداد العبد بالإرادات والقُدَرِ والمقدور على وجه التوالي ؛ بحيثُ لا يُحِنُّ [لا يحس؟] أَنَّهُ أُكرِهَ على الفعل أو أُلجِيَّ إليه ، ومهما صمَّمَ العبدُ عزمَهُ على فعل أمدَّهُ سبحانَهُ بخلْقِهِ ، وخلق القدرة عليهِ ، طاعةً كانَ ذلك الفعلُ أو معصيةً ، كما قال تعالى : من كَانَ يُرِيدُ الْعَاجِلَةَ ... [الإسراء : ١٨] الآيةَ ، وقال جل وعزّ : ﴿ وَمَنْ أَرَادَ الْآخِرَةَ ... [الإسراء : ١٩] الآيةَ ، ثم قال سبحانه إبْرَهما : كُلَّا تُمِدُّ هَؤُلَاءِ وَهَؤُلَاءِ مِنْ عَطَاءِ رَبِّكَ وَمَا كَانَ عَطَاهُ رَبِّكَ مَحْظُورًا) [الإسراء : ٢٠] ، فرتَّبَ الإمداد على الإرادة منهم إذا شاء ، وذلك الإمداد هو المُعبَّرُ عنه بالتوفيق والخذلانِ ، فصار العبد بحسَبِ الظاهرِ مُوجِدٌ لفعله ، حتى إنَّ الوهم والخيالَ لا يشكّانِ في ذلك ، وقد ضلَّ بهما كثيرٌ مِنَ الخَلْقِ ، ولولا أنَّ الله سبحانه أيَّدَ عقول أهل السنَّةِ فخرقوا حُجُبَ التوهُماتِ المظلمةِ ، وبرزوا إلى شموس المعرفة ، فأدركوا بها الأمر كيف هو . . لكانوا كغيرهم
As for my statement: 'And the truth is that the slave is compelled in a mold of choice...' to its end: this is another answer that makes reward and punishment good according to the school of Ahl al-Sunnah, even if we agree with the Mu'tazila on the basis of rational good and evil. And the face of that is that He, glorified, when He customarily provides the slave with wills, powers, and the empowered in a successive manner, such that he does not [feel?] that he was coerced into the action or compelled to it, and whenever the slave resolves his determination to do an act, He, glorified, provides him with His creating it, and creates the power for it, whether that act is obedience or disobedience, as the Most High said: 'Whoever desires the immediate...' [al-Isra 17:18] the verse, and He said, Mighty and Exalted: 'And whoever desires the Hereafter...' [al-Isra 17:19] the verse, then He, glorified, said to both: 'Each [group] We provide [for them] from the gift of your Lord, and the gift of your Lord is not restricted' [al-Isra 17:20]. So He made the provision dependent on the will from them when He wills, and that provision is what is called success and abandonment. So the slave became, according to the apparent, the originator of his action, so much so that imagination and fantasy do not doubt that, and many of creation have gone astray through the two, and were it not that Allah, glorified, supported the intellects of Ahl al-Sunnah and they tore through the dark veils of fanciful notions and emerged to the suns of knowledge, and thereby perceived the matter as it is, they would have been like others.
Source pages p. 468

Commentary
This passage is a definition and justification of the Sunni position on human action. The author begins by referencing his own statement that the slave is 'compelled in a mold of choice' (majbūr fī qālib mukhtār), which is a key Ash'ari formulation. He then presents this as an answer that renders reward and punishment rationally acceptable, even while agreeing with the Mu'tazila on the principle of rational good and evil (al-taḥsīn wa al-taqbīḥ al-ʿaqliyyayn). The explanation (wajh dhālika) proceeds: God's custom (ʿādah) is to provide the slave with successive wills, powers, and objects of power in such a way that the slave does not feel coerced. Whenever the slave firmly intends an act, God creates both the act and the power for it, whether it is obedience or sin. The author cites Quranic verses (al-Isra 17:18-20) to show that God's provision (imdād) follows the slave's will. This provision is termed tawfīq (success) or khidhlān (abandonment). Consequently, the slave appears (ẓāhir) to be the originator (mūjid) of his act, so much so that imagination and fantasy do not doubt it—leading many into error (they think the slave creates his own acts). Only Ahl al-Sunnah, whose intellects God supported, were able to pierce the veils of dark fanciful notions and reach the true reality (al-amr kayfa huwa). Without this, they would have been like others (i.e., those who deny divine omnipotence). Thus, the author establishes that the apparent human agency is real in a sense, but the true originating agent is God.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Admitting compulsion (jabr) nullifies moral responsibility.
Correction: This passage shows that the 'apparent' (ẓāhir) agency of the slave suffices for reward and punishment. The slave experiences choice and is held accountable, even though God is the true creator of the act.

Checkpoint
According to this passage, what is the basis for reward and punishment on the Sunni view?
A) The slave creates his own actions independently.
B) The slave appears to originate his actions, even though God creates them.
C) The slave is completely coerced with no sense of choice.
D) Reward and punishment are arbitrary and not based on actions.

Key Takeaway
According to Ahl al-Sunnah, the slave is compelled by God but experiences choice in the apparent realm, which provides a valid basis for reward and punishment.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you realize that even your desire to obey Allah is created by Him, does that make you more grateful for your acts of worship, or does it stir a feeling of helplessness? How can you turn that feeling into deeper reliance on Him?
?
The passage warns that imagination and fantasy can lead many to believe they are the true originators of their actions. Where in your daily routine do you subconsciously feel that you are the independent author of your deeds? What would change in your du'ā' if you truly believed that every step is His provision?
?
If God's provision of tawfīq or khidhlān depends on your will, are you earnestly asking Him for tawfīq, or do you take good deeds for granted? How can you actively seek His help while acknowledging that even that seeking is from Him?

Refuting the Claim That 'Lan' Implies Eternal Negation
Sanusi refutes the claim that 'lan' implies eternal negation using Quranic and logical arguments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Arabic word 'lan' does not always indicate eternal negation, and how Sanusi uses Quranic and logical arguments to refute that claim.

Core Idea
The word 'lan' can be time-bound; it does not automatically mean eternal negation.

Sanusi addresses a key objection raised by those who deny the possibility of seeing Allah. They argue that the word 'lan' in the Qur'an indicates eternal negation, but Sanusi demonstrates through clear examples that this is not the case. Let's examine his reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it, then read the translation. Notice the structure: an analogy, a Quranic verse, and a logical principle.
Pay attention to the flow of the argument.
2
Analyze the stone-and-food analogy
Sanusi uses this analogy to show that 'lan' can be used for a specific situation, not always eternally. Understand how the analogy sets up the key distinction.
The correct response differs based on the nature of the object.
3
Examine the Quranic counterexample
Sanusi cites the verse about the Jews (2:95) to prove that 'lan' does not always mean eternal, since they will eventually wish for death in the Fire.
This directly challenges the claim.
4
Understand the logical principle
Sanusi explains that the response must match the question, and time-bound negations remain time-bound. The logicians' example clarifies this.
This principle is crucial for interpreting 'lan tarani'.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
Connect the dots: 'lan tarani' applies only to this world, not the afterlife, because Moses asked for an immediate vision.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كان ممتنع الرؤية لقال : لا تصح رؤيتي ، أو لن تمكن ، أو لا أرى ، ونحو هذا ، ألا ترى أَنَّ مَنْ كانَ في كُمِّهِ حجرٌ ، فظَنَّهُ بعضُهم طعاماً ، فقال : أعطني هذا لاكله .. كانَ الجواب الصحيحُ له : إِنَّ هذا لا يُؤكَلُ ، أمَّا إذا كانَ طعاماً يصحُ أَكْلُهُ فحينئذٍ يصح أن يقول المجيبُ : إنَّكَ لن تأكله ، وهذا واضح . والجواب عن قولهم : إنَّ ( لن ( للتأبيد.. ممنوع ؛ لقوله في اليهودِ : وَلَن يَتَمَنَّوْهُ أَبَداً ﴾ [البقرة : ٩٥] ، وهم يتمنونه في النار . ثم إنَّ الآيةَ جواب لسؤالِ موسى عليه السلام ، وهو إِنَّما سألَ رؤيةً ناجزة في الدنيا ، فالجواب يعود إلى سلب رؤيتِهِ في الدنيا ؛ إذ الأصل في الجواب المطابقة ، ولأنَّ الجواب وقع هنا بنقيض السؤال ، وقد قيّد المسؤول بوقتِ معيَّن ، فالأصلُ أنَّ نقيضه يتقيَّدُ بِهِ ، ولهذا قالَ أهل المنطقِ : إنَّ نقيض الوقتيّةِ كقولك : زيدٌ مُتحرِّكُ الأصابع بالضرورة وقتَ الكتابة .. يُؤخَذُ فِيهِ ذلك الوقتُ بعينِهِ ، فيُقالُ في نقيض هذه القضية : زيد ليسَ مُتحرِّكَ الأصابع بالإمكان العام وقتَ الكتابة ، وإلى هذا المعنى أشرتُ بقولي : ( وقد يُتأَنَّس . . . ) إلى آخره
He [i.e., the one who denies the possibility of seeing Allah] would say: 'My seeing is not valid,' or 'You will not be able,' or 'I will not see,' and the like. Do you not see that if someone has a stone in his sleeve, and someone else thinks it is food, and says, 'Give me this to eat it,' the correct response to him is: 'This is not eaten.' But if it were food that can be eaten, then the responder may say: 'You will not eat it.' And this is clear. The answer to their statement that 'lan' indicates eternity... is [it is] rejected (mamnū'); because of His statement concerning the Jews: 'And they will never wish for it ever' [al-Baqarah 2:95], and they will wish for it in the Fire. Moreover, the verse is a response to the request of Moses, peace be upon him; he only requested an immediate vision in this world, so the response refers to negating his vision in this world; for the principle in a response is correspondence, and because the response here occurs as the contradictory of the request. And since the requested thing was qualified by a specific time, the principle is that its contradictory is also qualified by that time. That is why the logicians said: The contradictory of a time-bound proposition, like your saying: 'Zayd is necessarily moving his fingers at the time of writing'—the same time is taken into account in it, and it is said in the contradictory of this proposition: 'Zayd is not moving his fingers with general possibility at the time of writing.' And to this meaning I alluded with my saying: 'And it may be taken as an indication...' etc.
Source pages p. 496

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the claim that the word 'lan' (لن) indicates eternal negation, which was used by those who deny the possibility of seeing Allah in the afterlife. Sanusi begins by giving an analogy: if someone has a stone in his sleeve and another person thinks it is food and asks to eat it, the correct response is 'this is not eaten' because it is not food. But if it were food, the response could be 'you will not eat it' (using 'lan'). This shows that 'lan' can be used for negation in a specific time, not necessarily eternal. Then he directly counters the argument that 'lan' always indicates eternity. He provides a Quranic counterexample: Allah says about the Jews 'and they will never wish for it ever' (lan yatamannawhu abada), yet they will wish for it in the Fire. So 'lan' here does not mean eternal absolute negation. Moreover, the verse in response to Moses (asking to see Allah in this world) uses 'lan' regarding this world only, because the question was about an immediate vision. The principle is that a response corresponds to the question, and the contradictory of a time-bound statement is also time-bound. He illustrates with a logical example: the contradictory of 'Zayd is moving his fingers necessarily at the time of writing' is 'Zayd is not moving his fingers with general possibility at the time of writing' — the time qualification remains. Thus, 'lan tarani' applies only to this world.

Key Terms
لن
تأبيد
نقيض
وقتية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Arabic word 'lan' always indicates eternal negation.
Correction: Sanusi shows with Quranic examples and logical analysis that 'lan' can be restricted to a specific time frame, such as in the verse about the Jews and Moses' request.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi, what does the Quranic verse about the Jews ("And they will never wish for it ever") demonstrate about the word 'lan'?
A) It always implies eternal negation.
B) It can be used for temporal negation.
C) It is only used in negative contexts.
D) It is only used for future events.

Key Takeaway
The word 'lan' can indicate negation within a specific time frame, not necessarily eternal, as shown by Quranic examples and logical principles.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read the words of Allah, do you ever cling to a surface-level meaning without considering the context and principles of interpretation, even if it leads to a false image of your Lord? This lesson calls you to trust the scholars who weigh every word with precision. Will you humble yourself before their method, or rely on your own uninformed reading?
?
The Jews in the Quran were warned that they would never wish for death, yet they will wish for it in the Fire. Does your certainty about the unseen—like the reality of the Fire—move your heart to action today, or do you treat such truths as mere information?
?
Moses, a prophet, asked to see Allah, and the response was time-bound. You, too, may desire a direct vision now. But if Allah withholds it in this life for your own good, can you trust His wisdom and wait patiently for the vision He has promised to the believers in the Hereafter?

Soundness as a Positive Judgment: Objection and Refutation
First objection and its refutation: soundness as a positive judgment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Sanusi argues that soundness (sihha) must be a positive judgment, based on the logical principle that two negatives cannot be opposites.

Core Idea
Soundness is positive because its opposite is 'no soundness', and two negatives cannot be opposites.

Sanusi's argument that Allah will be seen in the afterlife involves several premises, one of which is that the soundness of seeing Allah is a positive judgment. An objector denies this, claiming soundness is not positive. How does Sanusi respond? This passage contains a neat logical refutation that hinges on the nature of contradiction.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Let the logical structure emerge.
Notice that the objection is stated very briefly.
2
Identify the key logical principle
The answer uses the idea that two negatives cannot be opposites. Make sure you understand what 'taqābul nafiyayn' means.
In logic, contradiction requires one positive and one negative.
3
Reflect on why this establishes positivity
Soundness is the contradictory of 'no soundness'. If 'no soundness' is negative and soundness were also negative, they could not be true opposites. Therefore, soundness must be positive.
This shows the argument is not merely verbal but rooted in logical necessity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأول : منع أنَّ الصحة حكم ثبوتي (١) وجوابه : أنَّ الصحَّةَ نقيضُ ( لا صحة ) المحمول على الممتنع ، فالصحة أمر ثبوتي ؛ لاستحالة تقابل نفيين (٢)
The first: The denial that soundness is a positive judgment (1). Its answer: Soundness is the contradictory of 'no soundness' predicated of the impossible. So soundness is a positive matter, because it is impossible for two negatives to be opposites (2).
Source pages p. 500

Commentary
This passage presents a specific objection in a longer argument about the proof for ru'ya. The objector denies that 'soundness' (sihha) is a positive judgment; they claim it is negative or merely relative. The answer argues that soundness is the opposite of 'no soundness,' which is a positive concept. Since the predicate 'no soundness' is applied to the impossible (mumtani'), the opposite must be positive. Moreover, two negatives cannot be true opposites (taqābul); one must be positive. Therefore, soundness is a positive judgment. This is a subtle logical point.

Key Terms
الصحة
حكم ثبوتي
نقيض
الممتنع
تقابل نفيين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that judgments like 'soundness' are purely conventional or relative, with no logical grounding.
Correction: The passage shows that soundness has logical grounding in the law of non-contradiction; its positivity is rationally necessary.

Checkpoint

B) Because it is the contradictory of 'no soundness', and two negatives cannot be opposites.
C) Because the objector conceded it.
D) Because it is derived from divine attributes.

Key Takeaway
Soundness is a positive judgment because it contradicts 'no soundness', and two negatives cannot be opposites.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The passage argues that two negatives cannot be opposites. In your spiritual life, do you define your faith by what you avoid (e.g., not drinking, not lying) rather than by what you actively pursue? What would it look like for you to live with a positive orientation toward obedience?
