The Universality Test for Soundness
Second objection: soundness does not require a ground, with refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second objection to the necessity of a ground for soundness and how the refutation uses a universality test to prove that soundness requires a ground.

Core Idea
Soundness needs a ground; otherwise it would apply to everything.

The objector tries to find a counterexample: the soundness of something being known. The response uses a powerful test—if soundness needed no ground, it would attach to all things. Since it does not, the ground is necessary.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the objector's concession and their counterexample. Note why they think 'being known' requires no ground.
The objector grants that soundness is a positive judgment but denies the need for a ground.
2
Analyze the refutation
The refutation uses a reductio: if no ground were needed, soundness would apply universally to existent and non-existent. Since it does not, a ground is necessary.
The key point is that soundness does not apply to impossible things, so there must be a specific cause.
3
Connect to kalam reasoning
Understand that this argument establishes that every positive judgment about reality requires a ground, which is a foundational principle in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني : سلَّمنا أنَّهُ حكم ثبوتيٌّ ، لكن لا نُسلّم توقفه على مُصحح ، وليس كلُّ حكم مفتقراً إلى مُصحح ؛ فإِنَّ صحَّةَ كونِ الشيء معلوماً حكم ، ولا يفتقر إلى مُصحح وجوابُهُ : أَنَّهُ لو لم يفتقر إلى مُصحح لعم تعلقهُ الموجود والمعدوم ، وحيثُ لم يعم اقتضى مُصححاً
The second: We grant that it is a positive judgment, but we do not grant that it depends on a ground (musahhih). And not every judgment is in need of a ground; for the soundness of something being known is a judgment, and it does not require a ground. Its answer: If it did not require a ground, its relation would be general to both the existent and the non-existent. And since it is not general, it necessitates a ground.
Source pages p. 500

Commentary
The objector concedes that soundness is positive but denies it needs a cause or ground (musahhih). They give an example: the judgment 'something is known' is sound but doesn't need a ground. The answer uses a reductio: if soundness didn't need a ground, it would apply equally to all things, existent and non-existent. Since it does not apply universally (e.g., some things are impossible), there must be a specific ground that qualifies something as sound. This shows the necessity of a musahhih.

Key Terms
مصحح
حكم
الموجود
المعدوم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The example of 'being known' proves that soundness does not require a ground.
Correction: The refutation shows that if soundness needed no ground, it would apply to all things equally. Since it does not, the example fails to prove the point.

Checkpoint
What is the counterexample given by the objector?
A) The soundness of something being known.
B) The soundness of existence.
C) The soundness of impossibility.
D) The soundness of divine attributes.

Key Takeaway
The universality test proves that soundness requires a specific ground, otherwise it would apply to all things.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You just learned that soundness requires a ground—otherwise it would apply universally. Think about your own acts of worship (e.g., prayer). If your prayer were sound based on mere routine without a genuine intention of seeking Allah, would it truly be sound? What is the specific ground that makes your prayer accepted? Are you performing it with that ground, or just going through the motions?
?
The objector claimed that 'being known' is a judgment without a ground. Reflect on your own knowledge of Allah. Do you treat your faith as something automatic—'I was born Muslim, so I know'—without seeking a ground? The refutation shows that if knowledge required no ground, then even false beliefs would be sound. Is your knowledge of Allah grounded in evidence and reflection, or merely inherited? What step will you take today to strengthen that ground?

The Objection: Soundness as Non-Positive Judgment and Its Refutation
First objection and its logical answer regarding soundness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the objection that soundness is not a positive judgment is refuted using the logical principle that two negations cannot be opposites.

Core Idea
Soundness must be positive because it opposes a negation, and two negations cannot be opposites.

A key argument in kalam is that soundness (sihha) is a positive attribute. An objector denies this, claiming it could be negative. The answer uses a logical argument: soundness is the opposite of 'no soundness', which describes the impossible. Since two negations cannot oppose each other, soundness must be positive. Let's examine this reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Note that it consists of two parts: the objection (منع) and the answer (جوابه).
The objection is very brief: it simply denies that soundness is positive.
2
Identify the key terms
Focus on the terms: 'soundness' (صحة), 'positive judgment' (حكم ثبوتي), 'the impossible' (الممتنع), and 'opposition of two negations' (تقابل نفيين). Understand what each means in this context.
'No soundness' (لا صحة) is a negation predicated of the impossible.
3
Understand the logical principle
The answer argues that soundness is the opposite of 'no soundness', and since the impossible is described by 'no soundness', soundness must be positive. The reason given is that two negations cannot oppose each other—they would be equivalent or not true opposites.
This principle is foundational: if X and Y are opposites, one must be positive.
4
Apply the conclusion
Recognize that the objector's claim would make soundness a negation, but the answer shows that would create an impossible logical situation. Therefore, soundness is indeed a positive judgment.
This refutation protects the original proof about seeing God.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأول : منع أنَّ الصحة حكم ثبوتي وجوابه : أنَّ الصحَّةَ نقيضُ ( لا صحة ) المحمول على الممتنع ، فالصحة أمر ثبوتي ؛ لاستحالة تقابل نفيين
First: The prevention that soundness is a positive judgment. And its answer: That soundness is the opposite of 'no soundness' which is predicated of the impossible, so soundness is a positive matter; because it is impossible for two negations to be opposed.
Source pages p. 500

Commentary
This passage presents the first objection to a proof concerning the soundness of seeing God. The objector denies that soundness (sihha) is a positive judgment (hukm thubuti), implying it could be a negative attribute. The answer refutes this by arguing that soundness is the opposite of 'no soundness', and since the impossible is described as 'no soundness', soundness itself must be a positive quality. This is because two negations cannot logically oppose each other; one must be positive. So the answer establishes soundness as a positive attribute.

Key Terms

حكم ثبوتي
ممتنع
تقابل نفيين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Soundness is a negative or relative concept, not positive.
Correction: Soundness must be positive because it is the opposite of a negation ('no soundness'), and two negations cannot logically oppose each other.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
Soundness is a positive attribute because it is the opposite of a negation, and two negations cannot be opposites.

The Second Objection: Does Every Judgment Need a Corrector?
Second objection denying need for a corrector and answer using generality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second objection to the proof of soundness and its answer, and why the objector's example does not refute the need for a corrector.

Core Idea
Soundness applies only to what exists — that very limitation proves it needs a corrector.

The opponent challenges the proof by saying not all judgments need a corrector. He offers the example of 'soundness of being known,' which seems to have no cause. But Sanusi shows that the very fact that soundness does not apply to non-existent things demonstrates that it must have a corrector that restricts it to existent things.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and example
Read the passage carefully. The objector concedes that soundness is a positive judgment but denies it needs a corrector. He gives the example of 'soundness of being known' as a judgment that requires no corrector.
Notice the objector is not denying the judgment itself, only its dependence on a cause.
2
Understand the answer's logic
Sanusi's answer uses the principle of generality: if soundness needed no corrector, it would apply equally to existing and non-existing things. But soundness does not apply to non-existing things, so there must be a corrector that restricts it.
The argument is conditional: if no corrector, then general; not general, therefore corrector.
3
Reflect on the implication
The objector's own example of 'soundness of being known' is actually a judgment that only applies to things that exist and are known. Its limited scope itself implies a corrector, so the objection fails.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني : سلَّمنا أنَّهُ حكم ثبوتيٌّ ، لكن لا نُسلّم توقفه على مُصحح ، وليس كلُّ حكم مفتقراً إلى مُصحح ; فإِنَّ صحَّةَ كونِ الشيء معلوماً حكم , ولا يفتقر إلى مُصحح وجوابُهُ : أَنَّهُ لو لم يفتقر إلى مُصحح لعم تعلقهُ الموجود والمعدوم , وحيثُ لم يعم اقتضى مُصححاً
Second: We concede that it is a positive judgment, but we do not concede its dependence on a corrector. Not every judgment requires a corrector; for the soundness of something being known is a judgment, and it does not require a corrector. And its answer: That if it did not require a corrector, its relation would be general to the existing and the non-existing, and since it is not general, it necessitates a corrector.
Source pages p. 500

Commentary
This passage presents the second objection to the proof. The objector concedes that soundness is a positive judgment but denies that it needs a cause or corrector (musahhih). He gives the example of the soundness of something being known—that is a judgment without a corrector. The answer argues that if the judgment of soundness did not require a corrector, it would apply equally to existing things and non-existing things. But since soundness does not apply to non-existing things (they are not sound), there must be a corrector that restricts it to existing things. Thus the need for a corrector is established.

Key Terms
مصحح
تعميم
موجود
معدوم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some judgments, like the soundness of something being known, seem to have no corrector.
Correction: But the fact that soundness does not apply to non-existing things shows that it does have a corrector that restricts it.

Checkpoint

B) Soundness of being known

Key Takeaway
The objector's own example backfires: the limited scope of soundness proves it depends on a corrector.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Look at your own certainties — do they apply equally to things that don't exist? If not, what is restricting them? That restriction is your corrector. Are you willing to trace it back?
?
If soundness required no corrector, it would apply to everything existing and non-existing. But you know it doesn't — dead things aren't 'sound' in the same way. That gap is a proof of a cause. What gap in your life (something you claim as sound but doesn't apply everywhere) is pointing you to a missing corrector? Will you follow that lead?

Corrector vs. Cause in Vision Debate
Fourth objection distinguishing corrector from cause.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fourth objection in the debate on the soundness of vision and the crucial distinction between a corrector and a cause.

Core Idea
A corrector is broader than a cause—it could be a condition.

This lesson presents the fourth objection raised against the argument that the soundness of vision is a caused judgment. The objector concedes causal explanation but questions why soundness must be a caused judgment, pointing out that the need for a corrector does not necessarily imply a cause—it could be a condition. We will explore this distinction and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Contextualize the objection
Understand that this objection accepts causal reasoning but disputes the specific claim that soundness is a caused judgment.
The objector says 'we concede the validity of causal explanation'.
2
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation carefully. Notice the concise, argumentative style.
3
Analyze the objection
Identify the core challenge: the opponent's argument only proves need for a corrector, not necessarily a cause.
The opponent's statement 'if it did not depend on a corrector...' is countered.
4
Examine the distinction
Understand the difference between 'illah (cause) and shart (condition) with the example of life being a condition for knowledge.
The example shows that a corrector can be a condition.
5
Note the incomplete answer
Recognize that the full response is not given here, so the lesson focuses on the objection and the key distinction.
This passage is only a fragment of a larger debate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرابع : سلَّمنا صحة التعليل، لكن لمَ قلتُم : إِنَّ صحَّةَ الرؤيةِ مِنَ الأحكام المعلَّلة ؟ وقولكم في جوابِهِ : إِنَّهُ لو لم يتوقف على مُصحح لعم حكمه المعدوم والموجود .. لا ينتج إلا أنَّه يتوقف على مُصحح ، وهو أعم مِنَ العلَّةِ ; إذ قد يكون شرطاً; فإنَّ الحياة شرط
Fourth: We concede the validity of causal explanation, but why do you say that the soundness of vision is among the caused judgments? And your statement in its answer: that if it did not depend on a corrector, its judgment would be general to the non-existing and existing ... only results in it depending on a corrector, and that is more general than the cause; for it may be a condition; for life is a condition.
Source pages p. 501

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth objection. The objector now concedes the validity of causal explanation but questions why the soundness of vision is considered a caused judgment. The answer given (presumably by the opponent) is that if it did not need a corrector, it would apply to non-existing and existing equally, which it does not, so it must depend on a corrector. But the objector points out that this only proves dependence on a corrector, not necessarily a cause; a corrector could be a condition (shart) rather than a cause. The example of life being a condition for knowledge is given. The answer seems cut off, so the full resolution is not present in this extract.

Equal Rulings and Different Causes: Objection and Answer
Sanusi presents the sixth objection and its answer regarding equal rulings having different causes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the sixth objection regarding equal rulings having different causes and why Sanusi rejects the analogy with color-ness.

Core Idea
Rational rulings are defined by their implications, not in themselves.

The objector tries to poke a hole in Sanusi's argument by pointing out that common properties like 'color-ness' are caused by specific color details. Sanusi responds by drawing a sharp distinction between physical properties and rational rulings. Let's see how he defends the integrity of the rational judgments.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
The objector concedes the ruling is common but denies that equal rulings cannot have different causes. He offers the example of 'color-ness' being caused by specific color properties.
Notice the structure: 'We grant X, but we do not grant Y.'
2
Identify the answer's key move
Sanusi distinguishes rational rulings (like 'being knowing') from physical properties. They are not distinguished in themselves but by their implications (موجباتها).
This is a crucial point about how we understand attributes of God.
3
Examine the counterexample
Sanusi shows that if 'being knowing' were caused by something contrary to knowledge, it would invert its meaning. This is impossible.
4
Understand the final point
Sanusi grants that 'color-ness' depends on specific colors, but points out that this would mean the more specific causes the more general, which is prohibited in the realm of rational judgments.
This is why the analogy fails.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السادس : سلَّمنا أنَّه مشترك ، ولكن لا نُسلّم امتناع تعليل الأحكام المتساوية بعلل مختلفةٍ ؛ فإنَّ اللونية مشترك ، ووجودها معلَّل بخصوصيَّاتِ الألوانِ . وجوابُهُ : أنَّ الأحكام العقليَّةَ كالعالميَّةِ والقادرية لا تتميز باعتبارِ ذاتِها ، وإنَّما تتميّز باعتبارِ مُوجِباتِها مِنَ العلم والقدرة ، فلو علَّلْنا العالميَّة بحقيقة تخالف العلم .. لزمَ قَلْبُ معقولها ، وذلك محال وأمَّا لزومُ اللونية لخصوصيَّاتِ الألوان فمُسلَّم ، والممنوع كونُ الأخص علة للأعم
The sixth [objection]: We grant that it is common [i.e., the ruling], but we do not grant the impossibility of explaining equal rulings by different causes; for 'color-ness' is common, and its existence is caused by specificities of colors. And its answer: That rational rulings like 'being knowing' and 'being powerful' are not distinguished in themselves, but rather are distinguished by their implications from knowledge and power. So if we explained 'being knowing' by a reality contrary to knowledge, it would entail inverting its intelligible meaning, and that is impossible. As for the dependence of 'color-ness' on specificities of colors, it is granted, and what is prohibited is that the more specific be a cause of the more general.
Source pages p. 502

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer pair (السادس). The objector concedes that the ruling (e.g., visibility) is common (shared between God and creatures) but denies that equal rulings cannot have different causes. He gives the example of 'color-ness' (اللونية) which is a common attribute but its existence in each color is caused by the specificities of individual colors. The answer distinguishes between rational rulings (الأحكام العقلية) like 'being knowing' (العالمية) and 'being powerful' (القادرية), which are not different in themselves but are distinguished by their implications (موجباتها) from knowledge and power. Therefore, if we were to explain 'being knowing' by a reality contrary to knowledge, we would invert its intelligible meaning, which is impossible. The answer concedes that 'color-ness' depends on specificities of colors, but this is a case where the more specific causes the more general, which is prohibited (الممنوع كون الأخص علة للأعم). Thus the analogy fails: in the case of rational rulings, the more specific cannot cause the more general.

Key Terms
مشترك
تعليل
الأحكام المتساوية
علل مختلفة
اللونية
الأحكام العقلية
العالمية
القادرية
موجبات
قلب معقول
الأخص
الأعم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that any common property can have different causes, so the objection disproves Sanusi's argument.
Correction: Sanusi shows that rational judgments are defined by their implications, so they cannot be caused by conflicting realities without destroying their meaning.

Checkpoint
Why does Sanusi reject the analogy between 'color-ness' and rational judgments like 'being knowing'?
A) Because rational judgments are not common properties.
B) Because rational judgments are distinguished by their implications, not in themselves.
C) Because color-ness is not a rational judgment.
D) Because the objector conceded too much.

Key Takeaway
Equal rulings for God and creation cannot have different causes because rational judgments are defined by their implications, not by themselves.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever assumed that because you and Allah both 'know' something, your knowledge and His are essentially the same? How does this lesson’s distinction between rational rulings and their implications challenge you to think differently about the uniqueness of Allah’s attributes? What will you do the next time you catch yourself comparing your knowledge to His?
?
If your actions are to be consistent with your beliefs, what would it mean for you to live in a way that the implications (موجبات) of your faith—like trust in Allah and obedience—are not contradicted by your choices? Give one specific area in your life where your practice does not match the implications of your faith. What step will you take today to align them?

Eighth Objection: Other Common Attributes
Sanusi presents the eighth objection and its answer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi defends the limitation of common attributes against the objection that possibility or composites could also be common.

Core Idea
A defender can rely on 'I investigated and did not find' until proven otherwise.

In this passage, Sanusi presents the eighth objection against the limitation of common attributes to existence and temporality. The objector argues that possibility (imkān) or a composite of possibility and something else could also be common. Sanusi then gives the answer from a Tlemceni commentator, showing how the defender's position is upheld.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Notice how the objector concedes existence is common but argues that possibility or its composite could also be common. This is a strong objection (man' qawī).
2
Understand the response
The Tlemceni commentator explains that the defender's claim 'I investigated and did not find' is sufficient until the opponent provides a counterexample. Then the defender invalidates possibility as a common cause by noting that not every possible thing is seen.
The response has two parts: the sufficiency of investigation and the invalidation of possibility.
3
Reflect on the dialectical method
This exchange shows how kalam arguments proceed: limitation (ḥaṣr), objection, and defense. The burden shifts to the objector to produce a concrete counterexample.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثامنُ : سلَّمنا أنَّ مفهوم الوجودِ مشترك ، لكن لا تُسلَّم أن لا مشترك سوى الوجودِ والحدوثِ ، وحصركم منخرم بالإمكان أو بالمركب منه ومِنْ غيرِه. وهذا منع قوي ، والاعتماد على عدم الوجدانِ لا يفيد العلم ، ولا يمكن إبطال التعليل بالإمكان أو بالمركَبِ منهُ ومِنْ غيرِهِ بأَنَّ الإمكان أمر عدمي ؛ فإنَّ الخصم يقولُ ذلك في صحة الرؤية ، ولا يمتنع تعليلُ العدمي بالعدمي . قلتُ : أَجابَ عنهُ بعضُ التِّلِمْسانيين في شرحه على عقيدة ابن الحاجب بأنْ قالَ : يكفي المستدل : « بحثتُ فلم أجد » ، ثم ظهور وصفِ صالح للتعليل بعد إبطالِ ما حُصِرَ مِنَ الأوصافِ لا يُوجِبُ انقطاعَهُ ، فيتعيَّنُ إبطاله ، ثم أبطل عليَّةَ الإمكان منفرداً : بعدمِ صحة رؤية كلٌّ ممكن، ومعَ غيره
Eighth: We grant that the concept of existence is common, but it is not granted that there is no common [attribute] other than existence and temporality, and your limitation is breached by possibility or by the composite of it and something else. This is a strong objection, and reliance on not finding [another attribute] does not yield knowledge, and it is not possible to invalidate the explanation by possibility or the composite of it and something else by [saying] that possibility is a privative thing; for the opponent says that concerning the validity of vision, and there is no objection to explaining a privative [effect] by a privative [cause]. I said: A certain Tlemceni answered in his commentary on Ibn al-Ḥājib's creed by saying: The arguer is satisfied with 'I have investigated and did not find'; then the appearance of a description fit for explanation after invalidating what was limited [by investigation] does not require his being refuted; rather, his [argument] must be invalidated. Then he invalidated the causality of possibility alone: by the fact that not every possible is validly seen, and with something else.
Source pages p. 504

Commentary
This passage is the eighth objection and its answer. The objector concedes that existence is common, but argues that the defender's limitation of common attributes to existence and temporality (ḥudūth) is flawed. There could be other common attributes, such as pure possibility (imkān) or a composite of possibility and something else. The objector says this is a strong objection (man' qawī). The defender cannot rely on simply not finding such attributes (عدم الوجدان) as a proof, nor can he argue that possibility is a privative attribute (amr 'adamī) because the opponent can also accept explaining a privative effect by a privative cause. The answer (from a Tlemceni commentator) is that the protagonist (mustadill) is justified in saying 'I investigated and did not find'—that remains sufficient until a counterexample is shown. Moreover, when a candidate description appears after the protagonist has already invalidated the limited set of possibilities, that does not automatically defeat his argument; rather the opponent must also invalidate the protagonist's argument. Then the protagonist goes on to invalidate possibility as a common cause by noting that not every possible thing is visible (some are non-existent). The last phrase 'ومع غيره' (and with something else) hints that the composite of possibility with another attribute is also invalidated, though the text is cut short. The lesson shows a typical kalam dialectical move: limitation (ḥaṣr), objection, and answer.

Key Terms
الإمكان
العدم
الحدوث
المنع
عدم الوجدان
التعليل بالعدمي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument 'I investigated and did not find' is too weak to prove anything.
Correction: In kalam, this is considered sufficient until a counterexample is provided.

Checkpoint
What alternative common attribute does the objector propose?
A) Necessity
B) Possibility or a composite containing possibility
C) Incorporeality
D) None

Key Takeaway
A defender's claim of exhaustive investigation is sufficient until the opponent produces a concrete alternative.

?
The objector here tried to shift the burden from providing evidence to criticizing the defender's method. In your own life, when you disagree with someone, do you focus on proving your own view or on poking holes in theirs? Which approach brings you closer to truth? Will you change how you engage in discussions from now on?
?
The defender's statement 'I have investigated and did not find' requires thorough and honest effort. Think of something you believe about God, yourself, or the world that you have not truly investigated. Are you willing to set aside the comfort of your current belief and honestly search for evidence until you can say 'I investigated and did not find'? What is stopping you from doing that starting today?

Temporality as a Relational Attribute
The commentator responds to the ninth objection about temporality's validity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why temporality (ḥudūth) cannot be a real attribute and how it remains a valid common factor without causing regress.

Core Idea
Temporality is a relational attribute, not a real one — else it would regress infinitely.

The ninth objection claims that temporality is not a valid attribute because it is only conceivable with non-existence. The commentator first refines this definition, then gives the decisive answer: temporality is merely a relational consideration (ṣifa iʿtibāriyya), not a real reality. This lesson unpacks that response and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to grasp the flow of the objection and answer.
Notice the objection concedes commonality but denies validity.
2
Understand the objection
The objector accepts only ḥudūth and wujūd as common but argues ḥudūth is not a valid attribute because it requires non-existence.
This is what the commentator responds to.
3
Follow the first response
The commentator denies that temporality requires non-existence. He defines it as existence qualified by precedingness (musbūqiyya), which is concurrent and a modality.
The attribute of something established is established.
4
Grasp the final answer
The commentator's ultimate answer: temporality is a relational attribute; if it were real, it would itself be temporal and cause infinite regress.
This preserves temporality as a valid common factor.
5
Reflect on the implication
Temporality can be used as a cause without being a real inherent attribute — it is a relation considered by the mind.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
التاسع : سلَّمنا أن لا مشترك سوى الحدوث والوجودِ ، لكن لا نُسلّم سقوط الحدوثِ عن درجة الاعتبار
قولُه : ( لا يُعقَلُ إلا بشِرْكَةِ مِنَ العدم ) قلنا : لا تُسلّم ، بل الحدوث هو الوجود المقيَّدُ بمسبوقيَّةِ العدم ، والمسبوقية أمر مقارن للوجودِ وكيفية له ، وصفة الثابتِ ثابتة .
وجوابه : أنَّ الحدوث صفةٌ اعتباريَّةٌ ، لا حقيقة ثابتة ، وإلا لكانَتْ حادثة أيضاً ، ولزم التسلسل
The ninth: We concede that there is no common (musharak) except temporality (ḥudūth) and existence (wujūd), but we do not concede that temporality falls from the degree of consideration. His statement: 'It is not conceived except with partnership from non-existence.' We say: It is not conceded. Rather, temporality is existence qualified by being preceded by non-existence, and the precedingness (musbūqiyya) is something concurrent with existence and a modality (kayfiyya) for it, and the quality (ṣifa) of what is established (thābit) is established. And its answer: That temporality is a relational attribute (ṣifa iʿtibāriyya), not a real reality (ḥaqīqa thābita), otherwise it would be temporal also, and regress (tasalsul) would follow.
Source pages p. 505

Commentary
This passage presents the ninth objection and the commentator's response. The objector concedes that the only common factor between God and creatures is temporality (ḥudūth) and existence (wujūd), but argues that temporality should not be considered a valid attribute for causing visibility. The commentator first counters the objection's claim that temporality 'is not conceived except with partnership from non-existence' — meaning it requires non-existence. The commentator denies this, defining temporality as existence qualified by being preceded by non-existence (wujūd muqayyad bi-musbūqiyyat al-ʿadam). The precedingness (musbūqiyya) is concurrent with existence and is a modality (kayfiyya) for it; the attribute of something established is itself established. Then the commentator gives the final answer (jawābuhu): temporality is a relational attribute (ṣifa iʿtibāriyya), not a real reality (ḥaqīqa thābita). If it were a real attribute, it would itself be temporal and lead to an infinite regress (tasalsul). Thus, temporality is not a real inherent attribute that could serve as a cause.

Key Terms
الحدوث (al-ḥudūth)
الوجود (al-wujūd)
المسبوقية (al-musbūqiyya)
الصفة الاعتبارية (al-ṣifa al-iʿtibāriyya)
التسلسل (al-tasalsul)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think temporality (ḥudūth) is a real attribute like existence, making it problematic as a common factor.
Correction: The commentator clarifies that temporality is a relational attribute (ṣifa iʿtibāriyya), not a real reality, thus it does not lead to regress.

Checkpoint
According to the commentator, why can temporality not be a real attribute?
A) Because it is shared between God and creatures.
B) Because if it were real, it would itself be temporal and lead to infinite regress.
C) Because it is not conceived without non-existence.
D) Because it is a modality of existence.

Key Takeaway
Temporality (ḥudūth) is a relational attribute, not a real one, which allows it to serve as a common factor without leading to infinite regress.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you treat your own existence as something real and self-sufficient, or do you recognize it is merely a relational attribute tied to the non-existence that preceded it and the Creator who sustains it? How would your daily priorities change if you truly felt the weight of that relational dependency?
?
The objector thought temporality required 'partnership with non-existence' — a kind of mixture. Are there areas in your life where you subconsciously think your good deeds are 'mixed' with sin or your faith is 'mixed' with doubt, rather than seeing them as a single, pure relation to God? What step can you take tonight to clarify that relation?

The Tenth Objection: Existence as Cause vs. Conditions
The commentator refutes the objection that existence as a cause might require conditions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why a rational cause (al-ʿilla al-ʿaqliyya) must necessitate its effect by its very essence and cannot be dependent on conditions, as opposed to a mere condition (sharṭ).

Core Idea
A rational cause necessitates by essence; conditions only enable, they do not necessitate.

The objector concedes that existence is a common cause but argues that perhaps its necessitation is conditional. He draws an analogy with life, which makes certain judgments possible but does not necessitate them for God. The commentator clarifies that rational causes are fundamentally different from conditions—they necessitate their effects by their very nature. This distinction is key to understanding why existence must directly entail visibility.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Carefully read the objector's claim: existence may require conditions and absence of impediments. Note his analogy with life enabling pleasure/pain but not for God.
The objector is trying to cast doubt on the absolute necessitation.
2
Focus on the answer
The commentator responds that a rational cause (ʿilla ʿaqliyya) cannot admit conditions because it necessitates by its essence. Compare with knowledge and the property of being knowledgeable.
Notice the key distinction: rational cause vs. condition.
3
Reflect on the analogy
Understand why life is not a rational cause but a condition. Consider how this applies to the main argument about existence and visibility.
This clarifies that existence, as a rational cause, must directly cause visibility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
العاشر : سلَّمنا أنَّ الوجودَ علَّةٌ مشتركةٌ ، ولكنْ لمَ قلتُم : إِنَّهُ يقتضي ذلك مطلقاً ، وما المانعُ مِنْ توقُّف اقتضائِهِ على شرطٍ وانتفاء مانع ، والحكم مُتوقفٌ على ذلكَ ؟ ألا ترى أنَّ الحياةَ مُصححة لكثيرٍ مِنَ الأحكام ؛ كاللذة والألم وغير ذلك ، والبارى تعالى لا يصح وصفه بذلك ؟!
وجوابه : أنَّ العلَّةَ العقليَّةَ لا يصح فيها ذلك ؛ لأنَّها تقتضي حكمها لذاتها ، فلا يصح وجودها بدونِهِ ؛ كالعلم والعالمية ، والحياةُ في جميع ما ذكروه شرط لا علة
The tenth: We concede that existence is a common cause (ʿilla mushtaraka), but why did you say that it necessitates that absolutely? And what prevents its necessitation from being dependent upon a condition and the absence of an impediment, and the judgment is dependent upon that? Do you not see that life (ḥayāt) qualifies (tuṣaḥḥiḥ) many judgments, such as pleasure and pain and other things, yet the Creator (al-Bārī) is not validly described with that? And its answer: That a rational cause (al-ʿilla al-ʿaqliyya) does not admit that, because it necessitates its judgment by its essence, so its existence without it is not valid; like knowledge (ʿilm) and the property of being knowledgeable (al-ʿālimiyya). And life in all that they mentioned is a condition (sharṭ), not a cause (ʿilla).
Source pages p. 506

Commentary
This is the tenth objection and its answer. The objector concedes that existence is a common cause, but questions why it would absolutely necessitate the judgment (i.e., visibility). Perhaps its necessitation is conditional upon a condition and the absence of an impediment. He gives an analogy: life (ḥayāt) makes possible many attributes like pleasure and pain, but God cannot be described with them. So why can't existence similarly require conditions? The commentator answers that a rational cause (al-ʿilla al-ʿaqliyya) cannot be conditional; it necessitates its effect by its very essence. If existence were such a cause, its presence without the effect would be impossible, as in the case of knowledge (ʿilm) and the state of being knowledgeable (ʿālimiyya) — knowledge necessarily entails being knowledgeable. Life, in contrast, is merely a condition (sharṭ) for certain judgments, not a cause (ʿilla). Thus, existence cannot be compared to life; it is a rational cause and must necessitate directly.

Key Terms
العلة العقلية
الشرط
العلة
الحياة
العلم
العالمية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That existence as a cause might require additional conditions to necessitate its effect, similar to how life enables judgments.
Correction: A rational cause (ʿilla ʿaqliyya) necessitates its effect by its essence and cannot depend on conditions. Life is only a condition, not a cause, so the analogy fails.

Checkpoint
What is the key difference between a rational cause (ʿilla ʿaqliyya) and a condition (sharṭ) according to this passage?
A) A rational cause necessitates by its essence, while a condition is merely enabling.
B) A rational cause requires a condition to act.
C) A condition is stronger than a rational cause.
D) There is no difference.

Key Takeaway
A rational cause necessitates its effect by its very essence and cannot be dependent on conditions, unlike mere conditions which enable but do not necessitate.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The Tenth Objection: Existence as a Common Cause and the Distinction Between Cause and Condition
Objection and answer on the unconditionality of rational cause.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why a rational cause cannot be conditional and how to distinguish between a cause and a condition.

Core Idea
A rational cause necessitates its effect by its essence, unconditionally.

In this passage, Sanusi presents the tenth objection to the argument that existence must cause visibility. The objector suggests that existence's causation might depend on conditions, similar to how life enables pleasure but God is not subject to it. Sanusi's response clarifies a crucial distinction: a rational cause is unconditional, unlike a condition. Let's examine the objection and its answer.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the objector's concession that existence is a shared cause, but then questions why it must be unconditional.
The objector uses the analogy of life to argue that causation can depend on conditions.
2
Understand the analogy of life
Life enables judgments like pleasure and pain, but God is not subject to them. This shows that a cause may require conditions or absence of impediments.
The objector uses this to suggest existence might also require conditions.
3
Grasp the distinction between cause and condition
Sanusi answers by distinguishing a rational cause (al-ʿilla al-ʿaqliyya) from a condition. A rational, essential cause like knowledge necessitates its effect (being knowledgeable) by its own essence and cannot exist without it. Life, in the examples, is merely a condition, not a cause.
Thus the analogy fails; existence as a cause of visibility is a rational cause, unconditional.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
العاشر : سلَّمنا أنَّ الوجودَ علَّةٌ مشتركةٌ ، ولكنْ لمَ قلتُم : إِنَّهُ يقتضي ذلك مطلقاً ، وما المانعُ مِنْ توقُّف اقتضائِهِ على شرطٍ وانتفاء مانع ، والحكم مُتوقفٌ على ذلكَ ؟ ألا ترى أنَّ الحياةَ مُصححة لكثيرٍ مِنَ الأحكام ؛ كاللذة والألم وغير ذلك ، والبارى تعالى لا يصح وصفه بذلك ؟! وجوابه : أنَّ العلَّةَ العقليَّةَ لا يصح فيها ذلك ؛ لأنَّها تقتضي حكمها لذاتها ، فلا يصح وجودها بدونِهِ ؛ كالعلم والعالمية ، والحياةُ في جميع ما ذكروه شرط لا علة
The tenth: We grant that existence is a common cause, but why do you say that it requires [its effect] absolutely? What prevents its requirement from depending on a condition and absence of an impediment, and the judgment depends on that? Do you not see that life makes correct many judgments, such as pleasure and pain and others, while the Exalted Creator cannot be described by that? Its answer: that a rational cause cannot have that [dependence] because it requires its judgment by its own essence, so its existence without that judgment is not valid; like knowledge and being knowledgeable; life in all what they mentioned is a condition, not a cause.
Source pages p. 506

Commentary
This passage is the tenth objection (al-ʿāshir) and its answer. The objector concedes that existence is a shared cause (ʿilla mushtaraka) but questions why its causation must be unconditional. Could it not depend on a condition (sharṭ) and absence of impediment? The objector gives an analogy: life (al-ḥayāh) makes possible many judgments (like pleasure and pain) but God, who is living, is not subject to them. This shows that a cause may require conditions. The answer distinguishes between a rational or essential cause (al-ʿilla al-ʿaqliyya) and a condition. A rational cause, such as knowledge entailing being knowledgeable, necessitates its effect by its own essence; it cannot exist without that effect. Life is a condition (sharṭ), not a cause, in the examples given. Therefore, the analogy fails. Existence as a cause of visibility for God is a rational cause, not a conditional one.

Key Terms
العلة المشتركة (shared cause)
العلة العقلية (rational cause)
شرط (condition)
العلم والعالمية (knowledge and being knowledgeable)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That a cause can be conditional, requiring additional conditions or absence of impediments.
Correction: A rational cause necessitates its effect by its essence, unconditionally. Conditions do not necessitate; they only enable.

Checkpoint
How does Sanusi's response distinguish between a cause and a condition?
A) A cause is unconditional by essence, a condition does not necessitate
B) A cause is always from God, a condition from creatures
C) A cause requires its effect even if impeded
D) A condition is stronger than a cause

Key Takeaway
A rational cause is unconditional by essence, unlike a condition which does not necessitate its effect.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You affirm that God's existence is a rational cause for His being seen in the afterlife. Does your certainty about this truth produce in you a longing for that vision, or do you live as if the condition of your own life (like the objector's 'life') makes that vision uncertain? What would it mean for you to treat God's existence as an unconditional guarantee of your hope?

The objector used the example of life permitting pleasure/pain to suggest God's causation is conditional. Where in your own obedience have you made God's commands conditional on your comfort or circumstances, treating His decrees as mere possibilities rather than essential necessities? How can you shift from condition-based submission to essence-based surrender?
?
If knowledge is a rational cause that necessarily produces being knowledgeable, then your knowledge of God's unity (tawhid) should necessitate your actions. Examine your life: is your knowledge of tawhid actually causing your daily choices, or have you turned it into a condition that only sometimes affects you? What one step can you take today to let that knowledge become an unconditional cause in your heart?

The Bahshamiyya Objection and Sanusi's Answer
Sanusi presents and answers the Bahshamiyya's objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Bahshamiyya's objection that if vision only attaches to existence, it cannot perceive differences, and Sanusi's response that knowledge of existence precedes distinction.

Core Idea
Vision first grasps existence, then difference follows.

The Bahshamiyya, followers of Abu Hashim, challenge the possibility of seeing God by arguing that if vision only grasps existence, we would not perceive distinct things. Sanusi responds by explaining the natural order of perception: we first know existence, then distinction.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the Bahshamiyya's question carefully: if vision attaches to existence, how do we perceive differences?
This challenges the proof for seeing God.
2
Read Sanusi's answer
Notice that Sanusi says we first know existence, and then distinction follows.
This is a direct reply.
3
Reflect on the implication
Consider how this resolves the objection for the possibility of seeing God.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني عشر : أنَّ هذهِ الحجَّةَ تنتقصُ بالوجهين اللذين ذكرهما الإمامُ الفخرُ ، وقد تقدَّما وزادَتِ البهشميَّةُ سؤالاً ؛ وهو أنَّ الرؤية لو تعلَّقَتْ بالوجودِ لَمَا أدركنا اختلاف الأشياء . وجوابه : أنا إذا شاهدنا شيئاً علمنا وجودَه ، وتبعه العلم بتمييزه . وقال أبو هاشم : الرؤية تتعلق بالأخص ، ويتبعه العلم بالوجودِ الأعم ، قال : وما ذكرناه أدخلُ في قضية العقل ؛ فإنَّ العلم بالأخص يستلزم العلم بالأعم ، ولا ينعكس . قلنا : نحنُ لا ندَّعي أنَّ ذلكَ لازم للعقلاء إلا عادةً ، بل نقولُ : إِنْ عُلِمَ ذلكَ في بعض الأشياء فهو قضية عادية . وقولُ أبي هاشم : ( إنَّ الرؤية تتعلق بالأخص ، ثم يتبعها العلم بالوجودِ ) كيف يصح منهُ معَ زعمِهِ أنَّ أخص وصف الشيء حال نفسية . وقولُهُ : ( كما أنَّ الحال لا موجودةٌ ولا معدومةٌ فهي لا معلومةٌ ولا مجهولة ) ، وعنى بهِ : أَنَّها لا تُعلَمُ على حيالها ، وإذا لم تكن معلومة على حيالها فكيف تكون محسوسة وكلُّ محسوس معلوم ؟! وقوله : ( إِنَّا ننتقلُ مِنْ إدراكِ الأخصّ إلى إدراك الوجودِ الأعم ) لا يستقيم مع دعواهم : أنَّ الوجود عرضي يفارقُ ؛ فإنَّهم أثبتوا الماهياتِ مُتقرّرةً في العدم بدونِ الوجودِ ، والعلم بالأخص إنَّما يستلزم العلم بالأعم الذاتي أو لازمه ، لا العرضي المفارق . قلتُ : واقتصرنا في العقيدةِ على أحدِ هذه الاعتراضات ، وهو السابع منها ، وبالله التوفيق .
The twelfth [objection]: that this argument is diminished by the two aspects mentioned by Imam Fakhr, and they have preceded. And the Bahshamiyya added a question: that if vision were attached to existence, we would not perceive the difference of things. And its answer: when we witness something, we know its existence, and the knowledge of its distinction follows. And Abu Hashim said: vision attaches to the more specific, and the knowledge of the more general existence follows it. He said: what we mentioned is more fitting to the judgment of reason; for knowledge of the more specific necessitates knowledge of the more general, and does not reverse. We said: we do not claim that this is necessary for rational beings except by habit, but rather we say: if that is known in some things, it is a habitual judgment. And the saying of Abu Hashim: 'Vision attaches to the more specific, then the knowledge of existence follows it'—how can it be correct from him, given his claim that the more specific description of a thing is a state (ḥāl) that is mental? And his saying: 'Just as the state is neither existent nor non-existent, so it is neither known nor unknown'—by which he meant that it is not known in itself, and if it is not known in itself, how can it be sensed, and every sensed thing is known?! And his saying: 'We transfer from the perception of the more specific to the perception of the more general existence' is not consistent with their claim that existence is an accidental attribute that separates; for they have established the essences (māhiyyāt) as fixed in non-existence without existence, and knowledge of the more specific only necessitates knowledge of the essential more general or its necessary concomitant, not the accidental separable attribute. I said: and we confined ourselves in the creed to one of these objections, which is the seventh of them, and with God is success.
Source pages p. 507

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of objections raised against the proof for the possibility of seeing God. It begins by referencing the twelfth objection: that the argument is weakened by two points mentioned by Imam Fakhr al-Din al-Razi. Then it presents an additional question from the Bahshamiyya (followers of Abu Hashim): if vision were merely attached to existence, we would not perceive the distinct differences between things. The answer given is that when we see something, we first know its existence, and then knowledge of its distinct characteristics follows. Abu Hashim himself proposed that vision attaches to the more specific attribute (al-akhass), and knowledge of the more general existence follows. He argued that this is more rational because knowledge of the specific necessitates knowledge of the general, but not vice versa. The author (Sanusi) then responds that we do not claim this is a rational necessity but rather a habitual occurrence. He then criticizes Abu Hashim's position by pointing out an inconsistency: Abu Hashim claims that the most specific description of a thing is a 'state' (ḥāl) that is neither existent nor non-existent, and therefore neither known nor unknown in itself. If it is not known in itself, how can it be sensed? Moreover, Abu Hashim's claim that we transfer from perceiving the specific to perceiving the general existence does not hold because existence, in the Mu'tazilite view, is an accidental attribute that can be separated from the essence. Knowledge of the specific only necessitates knowledge of essential attributes or necessary concomitants, not accidental separable attributes. The passage concludes with the author noting that in his creed (al-ʿAqīda al-Sughrā), he only included one of these objections—the seventh—and relies on God for success.

Key Terms
الرؤية
الوجود

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that vision cannot grasp existence without also grasping specific attributes simultaneously.
Correction: Sanusi shows that vision first attains existence, and then through that we discern distinctions.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi, what is the first thing we know when we see something?
A) Its specific attributes
B) Its existence
C) Its distinction
D) Its name

Key Takeaway
Vision primarily grasps existence, and from that we derive knowledge of differences.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you observe the world around you, do you first acknowledge that each thing exists before you judge its worth or purpose? What changes in your attitude if you consciously prioritize existence over differentiation?
?
The Bahshamiyya feared that vision focused only on existence would miss the uniqueness of each thing. In your relationship with Allah, do you sometimes try to grasp His attributes immediately instead of first affirming His existence? How can you practice 'resting in existence' before demanding details?
?
Sanusi teaches that knowledge of distinction follows from knowledge of existence. Is there a person or situation in your life where you have been impatient to 'differentiate' and judge, without first simply acknowledging their existence before Allah? How will you apply the priority of existence this week?

Abu Hashim's Proposal and Sanusi's Response on Habit
Sanusi critiques Abu Hashim's alternative view.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Abu Hashim's claim that vision attaches to the specific attribute and Sanusi's distinction between rational necessity and habitual occurrence.

Core Idea
Sanusi argues that the link between specific and general knowledge is habitual, not logical.

Abu Hashim attempts to improve on the Bahshamiyya objection by proposing that vision attaches to the more specific attribute, from which knowledge of general existence follows. He claims this is more rational. Sanusi clarifies that while this inference is common, it is not a logical necessity but a habitual pattern.

Guided Path
1
Read Abu Hashim's proposal
Notice his claim: vision attaches to the specific, and knowledge of existence follows.
He says this is more fitting to reason.
2
Read Sanusi's critique
Sanusi says the inference is not necessary for all rational beings, only habitual.
This is a key distinction.
3
Reflect on the difference
Consider why this matters for theological debate: it weakens Abu Hashim's rational necessity claim.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

The Problem of the 'State' and Sensation
Sanusi exposes inconsistency in Abu Hashim's view of the state.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Sanusi's criticism of Abu Hashim's concept of the 'state' (ḥāl) as being unknowable and therefore not perceivable by vision.

Core Idea
If the specific attribute is a 'state' that is neither existent nor non-existent, it cannot be sensed.

Sanusi attacks Abu Hashim's position from within his own metaphysical framework. Abu Hashim claims that the most specific attribute of a thing is a 'state' — a mental entity that is neither existent nor non-existent. Sanusi points out that such a state cannot be known in itself, and therefore cannot be perceived by vision.

Guided Path
1
Read Sanusi's critique
He quotes Abu Hashim's definition of the state as neither existent nor non-existent, and therefore neither known nor unknown.
This is the key point.
2
Understand the concept of ḥāl
A state in Mu'tazilite thought is a mental attribute that is not independently real.
It is not directly knowable.
3
See why it cannot be sensed
Sanusi argues that if it is not known in itself, it cannot be sensed, because everything sensed is known.
This refutes Abu Hashim's claim that vision attaches to the specific.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني عشر : أنَّ هذهِ الحجَّةَ تنتقصُ بالوجهين اللذين ذكرهما الإمامُ الفخرُ ، وقد تقدَّما وزادَتِ البهشميَّةُ سؤالاً ؛ وهو أنَّ الرؤية لو تعلَّقَتْ بالوجودِ لَمَا أدركنا اختلاف الأشياء . وجوابه : أنا إذا شاهدنا شيئاً علمنا وجودَه ، وتبعه العلم بتمييزه . وقال أبو هاشم : الرؤية تتعلق بالأخص ، ويتبعه العلم بالوجودِ الأعم ، قال : وما ذكرناه أدخلُ في قضية العقل ؛ فإنَّ العلم بالأخص يستلزم العلم بالأعم ، ولا ينعكس . قلنا : نحنُ لا ندَّعي أنَّ ذلكَ لازم للعقلاء إلا عادةً ، بل نقولُ : إِنْ عُلِمَ ذلكَ في بعض الأشياء فهو قضية عادية . وقولُ أبي هاشم : ( إنَّ الرؤية تتعلق بالأخص ، ثم يتبعها العلم بالوجودِ ) كيف يصح منهُ معَ زعمِهِ أنَّ أخص وصف الشيء حال نفسية . وقولُهُ : ( كما أنَّ الحال لا موجودةٌ ولا معدومةٌ فهي لا معلومةٌ ولا مجهولة ) ، وعنى بهِ : أَنَّها لا تُعلَمُ على حيالها ، وإذا لم تكن معلومة على حيالها فكيف تكون محسوسة وكلُّ محسوس معلوم ؟! وقوله : ( إِنَّا ننتقلُ مِنْ إدراكِ الأخصّ إلى إدراك الوجودِ الأعم ) لا يستقيم مع دعواهم : أنَّ الوجود عرضي يفارقُ ؛ فإنَّهم أثبتوا الماهياتِ مُتقرّرةً في العدم بدونِ الوجودِ ، والعلم بالأخص إنَّما يستلزم العلم بالأعم الذاتي أو لازمه ، لا العرضي المفارق . قلتُ : واقتصرنا في العقيدةِ على أحدِ هذه الاعتراضات ، وهو السابع منها ، وبالله التوفيق .

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you hold any belief about God that you have never examined for internal consistency? After reading this passage, are you willing to scrutinize it as Sanusi scrutinized Abu Hashim's view?
?
If a 'state' is unknowable in itself, then claiming to know it would be a contradiction. Are there things about your faith that you claim to know while admitting they are beyond understanding? Can you honestly reconcile that?
?
Sanusi demands logical coherence. Is there an area of your life where you act inconsistently with what you claim to believe? Will you confront that inconsistency today?

Inconsistency with Accidental Existence
Sanusi refutes Abu Hashim using the Mu'tazilite distinction of accidental existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Sanusi's argument that Abu Hashim's view is inconsistent with the Mu'tazilite concept of existence as an accidental separable attribute.

Core Idea
Knowledge of the specific does not necessitate knowledge of an accidental existence.

Sanusi further critiques Abu Hashim by pointing out that his own school (the Mu'tazila) holds that existence is an accidental attribute (ʿaraḍ) that can be separated from the essence. Therefore, knowledge of the specific attribute (which is essential) cannot imply knowledge of existence (which is accidental).

Guided Path
1
Read Sanusi's critique
He says Abu Hashim's inference is inconsistent with their claim that existence is accidental and separable.
This is a logical argument.
2
Understand the distinction
In Mu'tazilite thought, essences (māhiyyāt) exist in non-existence without existence; existence is an accidental attribute.
Essential vs. accidental.
3
See the inconsistency
Knowledge of the specific (essential) only implies essential generalities or necessary concomitants, not accidental ones like existence.
Therefore, the inference fails.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني عشر : أنَّ هذهِ الحجَّةَ تنتقصُ بالوجهين اللذين ذكرهما الإمامُ الفخرُ ، وقد تقدَّما وزادَتِ البهشميَّةُ سؤالاً ؛ وهو أنَّ الرؤية لو تعلَّقَتْ بالوجودِ لَمَا أدركنا اختلاف الأشياء . وجوابه : أنا إذا شاهدنا شيئاً علمنا وجودَه ، وتبعه العلم بتمييزه . وقال أبو هاشم : الرؤية تتعلق بالأخص ، ويتبعه العلم بالوجودِ الأعم ، قال : وما ذكرناه أدخلُ في قضية العقل ؛ فإنَّ العلم بالأخص يستلزم العلم بالأعم ، ولا ينعكس . قلنا : نحنُ لا ندَّعي أنَّ ذلكَ لازم للعقلاء إلا عادةً ، بل نقولُ : إِنْ عُلِمَ ذلكَ في بعض الأشياء فهو قضية عادية . وقولُ أبي هاشم : ( إنَّ الرؤية تتعلق بالأخص ، ثم يتبعها العلم بالوجودِ ) كيف يصح منهُ معَ زعمِهِ أنَّ أخص وصف الشيء حال نفسية . وقولُهُ : ( كما أنَّ الحال لا موجودةٌ ولا معدومةٌ فهي لا معلومةٌ ولا مجهولة ) ، وعنى بهِ : أَنَّها لا تُعلَمُ على حيالها ، وإذا لم تكن معلومة على حيالها فكيف تكون محسوسة وكلُّ محسوس معلوم ؟! وقوله : ( إِنَّا ننتقلُ مِنْ إدراكِ الأخصّ إلى إدراك الوجودِ الأعم ) لا يستقيم مع دعواهم : أنَّ الوجود عرضي يفارقُ ؛ فإنَّهم أثبتوا الماهياتِ مُتقرّرةً في العدم بدونِ الوجودِ ، والعلم بالأخص إنَّما يستلزم العلم بالأعم الذاتي أو لازمه ، لا العرضي المفارق . قلتُ : واقتصرنا في العقيدةِ على أحدِ هذه الاعتراضات ، وهو السابع منها ، وبالله التوفيق .
The twelfth [objection]: that this argument is diminished by the two aspects mentioned by Imam Fakhr, and they have preceded. And the Bahshamiyya added a question: that if vision were attached to existence, we would not perceive the difference of things. And its answer: when we witness something, we know its existence, and the knowledge of its distinction follows. And Abu Hashim said: vision attaches to the more specific, and the knowledge of the more general existence follows it. He said: what we mentioned is more fitting to the judgment of reason; for knowledge of the more specific necessitates knowledge of the more general, and does not reverse. We said: we do not claim that this is necessary for rational beings except by habit, but rather we say: if that is known in some things, it is a habitual judgment. And the saying of Abu Hashim: 'Vision attaches to the more specific, then the knowledge of existence follows it'—how can it be correct from him, given his claim that the more specific description of a thing is a state (ḥāl) that is mental? And his saying: 'Just as the state is neither existent nor non-existent, so it is neither known nor unknown'—by which he meant that it is not known in itself, and if it is not known in itself, how can it be sensed, and every sensed thing is known?! And his saying: 'We transfer from the perception of the more specific to the perception of the more general existence' is not consistent with their claim that existence is an accidental attribute that separates; for they have established the essences (māhiyyāt) as fixed in non-existence without existence, and knowledge of the more specific only necessitates knowledge of the essential more general or its necessary concomitant, not the accidental separable attribute. I said: and we confined ourselves in the creed to one of these objections, which is the seventh of them, and with God is success.
Source pages p. 507

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of objections raised against the proof for the possibility of seeing God. It begins by referencing the twelfth objection: that the argument is weakened by two points mentioned by Imam Fakhr al-Din al-Razi. Then it presents an additional question from the Bahshamiyya (followers of Abu Hashim): if vision were merely attached to existence, we would not perceive the distinct differences between things. The answer given is that when we see something, we first know its existence, and then knowledge of its distinct characteristics follows. Abu Hashim himself proposed that vision attaches to the more specific attribute (al-akhass), and knowledge of the more general existence follows. He argued that this is more rational because knowledge of the specific necessitates knowledge of the general, but not vice versa. The author (Sanusi) then responds that we do not claim this is a rational necessity but rather a habitual occurrence. He then criticizes Abu Hashim's position by pointing out an inconsistency: Abu Hashim claims that the most specific description of a thing is a 'state' (ḥāl) that is neither existent nor non-existent, and therefore neither known nor unknown in itself. If it is not known in itself, how can it be sensed? Moreover, Abu Hashim's claim that we transfer from perceiving the specific to perceiving the general existence does not hold because existence, in the Mu'tazilite view, is an accidental attribute that can be separated from the essence. Knowledge of the specific only necessitates knowledge of essential attributes or necessary concomitants, not accidental separable attributes. The passage concludes with the author noting that in his creed (al-ʿAqīda al-Sughrā), he only included one of these objections—the seventh—and relies on God for success.

Key Terms
العرضي المفارق
الماهية
الذاتي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Existence is always implied by the essence.
Correction: In Mu'tazilite thought, existence is an accidental attribute that may be absent.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi, what does knowledge of the specific attribute necessarily imply?
A) Accidental existence
B) Essential generalities or necessary concomitants
C) The state itself
D) Nothing

Key Takeaway
From an essential specific attribute, you cannot logically infer an accidental existence.

The Insufficiency of Ray Connection for Vision
Sanusi refutes the claim that vision requires ray connection using four counterarguments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the claim that vision requires ray connection is insufficient, based on counterexamples including the indivisible atom and size change.

Core Idea
Ray connection alone does not guarantee vision; counterexamples prove it.

In this passage, Sanusi engages with an opponent's theory of vision that relies on a ray from the eye connecting to objects via luminous air. He systematically dismantles this view by showing that the claimed condition fails to account for ordinary experiences such as night vision, the perception of extended air, and especially the non-seeing of the indivisible atom. Let's follow his arguments.

Guided Path
1
Understand the opponent's claim
Read the first part of the passage where Sanusi presents the opponent's view: vision occurs when a ray from the eye connects to the object, aided by luminous air.
Notice the term 'ملازمة' (necessary connection) – Sanusi denies this connection.
2
First two refutations
Consider the first two objections: (1) if the ray were necessary, then at night (dark air) one would not see large objects, but experience shows otherwise; (2) if the ray only connects to part of the air, why does one see more air than the pupil?
These show that the condition is not necessary.
3
The atom argument
Focus on the indivisible atom (الجوهر الفرد). According to the opponent, when alone, the atom should be seen because the ray connects to it, but it is not. This refutes the sufficiency of ray connection.
4
The size change argument
Observe that a large object appears small from a distance even though the ray connects to all of it. This further undermines the opponent's claim that ray connection and correspondence (المقابلة) are sufficient.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
حدقتِهِ بأن منعوا الملازمةَ ، ومستندهم : أَنَّهُ إِنَّما رأى الكثير لأنَّ أجزاء الهواء مضيئة ، فيتصلُ الشعاعُ بها وهي تتصل بالسماءِ ، فتعين على الإبصار ، كما أنَّ البلور إذا اتَّصلَ الشعاعُ به وهو جسم مضيء متصل بما فيه فيُرى ما فيه . ورُدَّ عليهم : بأنَّهُ لو كانَ كذلكَ لزمَ ألا يُرى الكثيرُ مِنَ السماءِ وغيرها حيثُ يكون الهواء مظلماً بالليل مثلاً ، وأيضاً : فما باله رأى مِنَ الهواءِ نفسِهِ أكثرَ مِنْ حدقتِهِ معَ أَنَّ الشعاعَ إِنَّمَا اتَّصلَ ببعضه ؟! ومما ينقضُ عليهم : عدم رؤيةِ الجوهر الفردِ معَ اتصال الشعاع بهِ ، ولا ينالُهُ مِنْ ذلك وحدَهُ إِلا ما ينالُهُ معَ غيرِهِ ، ورؤية الكبير مع البعدِ صغيراً معَ اتصال الشعاع والمقابلة لجميعه يعني أنَّهُ ممَّا ينقصُ عليهم ادعاءَهم وجوبَ رؤيةِ مَا اتَّصلَ بهِ الشعاع . الجوهر الفردُ إذا كانَ في سمْتِ الشعاع ؛ فإنَّهُ لا مانع على زعمهم من اتصال الشعاع به ، بدليل أنَّها تتصل بهِ عندَ اجتماعِهِ معَ غيره ، ولا ينالُهُ مِنَ الشعاعِ عند الاتصال إلا ما يناله عند الانفصال ، فكانَ يجب على قولهم أَنْ يُرى عند الانفرادِ معَ أَنَّهُ لا يُرى وكذا ينتقص مذهبهم برؤية الكبير مع البعد صغيراً ، مع أن شرط الرؤية على زعمهم موجود؛ وهو اتصال الشعاع والمقابلة لجميعه )
His pupil, because they denied the necessary connection. Their basis: he only sees a large object because the parts of air are luminous, so the ray connects to them and they connect to the sky, thus helping sight, just as crystal, if the ray connects to it while it is a luminous body connected to what is inside it, one sees what is inside it. And it was refuted against them: that if it were so, it would follow that one would not see large objects from the sky and elsewhere when the air is dark, e.g. at night. Also: why does he see more of the air itself than his pupil, while the ray only connected to part of it?! And among what refutes them: the non-seeing of the indivisible atom (al-jawhar al-fard) despite the connection of the ray to it, and it only receives from the ray alone what it receives when with another; and seeing a large object from a distance as small, despite the connection of the ray and the correspondence of all of it. Meaning: that of what refutes them is their claim of the necessity of seeing what the ray has connected to. The indivisible atom, if it is in the direction of the ray, then there is no obstacle, according to their claim, to the connection of the ray to it, as evidence that it connects to it when it is combined with another, and it only receives from the ray when connected what it receives when disconnected. So it would be necessary, according to their statement, that it be seen when alone, while it is not seen. Likewise, their doctrine is refuted by seeing a large object from a distance as small, while the condition of vision according to their claim exists: which is the connection of the ray and the correspondence of all of it).
Source pages p. 511

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of an opponent's claim about the necessary condition for vision. The opponents held that vision occurs when a ray from the eye connects to the object, aided by luminous air. Sanusi refutes this with several arguments. First, if the ray were necessary, then in darkness one would not see large objects, but one does. Second, if the ray only connects to part of the air, why does one see more of the air than the pupil? Third, the indivisible atom (al-jawhar al-fard) is not seen despite the ray connecting to it when combined, and it should be seen alone if the condition were sufficient. Fourth, large objects appear small from a distance even though the ray connects to all of it. Thus, the claimed condition is not sufficient.

الشعاع

الملازمة

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Ray connection and correspondence are sufficient for vision.
Correction: The indivisible atom is not seen despite these conditions, showing they are not sufficient.

C) It becomes smaller.
D) It emits light.

Key Takeaway
The presence of ray connection does not guarantee vision, as shown by the indivisible atom and other counterexamples.

What in your spiritual vision has shrunk because you relied on your own limited perspective rather than the reality of what is before you? The Triangle Argument and Its Refutation
Sanusi presents an objection-and-answer on visual perception.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Sanusi's rebuttal to the triangle argument used to explain why distant objects appear small, and why that argument fails because it would lead to invisibility.

Core Idea
Geometric optics alone cannot explain perception; it would falsely predict invisibility.

In this passage, Sanusi engages with a physical argument from his opponents about why a large distant object appears small. They propose that the visual ray passes through a narrow triangle, making the edges invisible. Sanusi counters by showing that this logic would make the object invisible altogether if moved slightly further—a clear contradiction with experience. He then unpacks the opponents' fuller geometrical explanation. This lesson walks through the argument and its rebuttal.

Guided Path
1
Read the Objection
Read the opening statement by the opponents describing the triangle geometry. Note how they use an acute angle and a base to explain why far edges are not seen.
This is a physical theory based on ray optics.
2
Identify Sanusi's Rebuttal
Find the 'We said' part. Notice his counterexample: if the object is moved to the distance of the increase, it would not be seen at all—but observation shows it is still visible. This is a reductio ad absurdum.
The rebuttal uses empirical falsification.
3
Study the Elaborated Answer
Read the explanation beginning with 'Meaning: That they answered...' This is Sanusi presenting the opponents' more detailed geometric response. Understand how they try to refine their argument.
Even this elaboration may not fully resolve the flaw.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قالوا : إِنَّما ذلكَ لأنَّ الشعاع نفذَ مِنْ زاويةٍ حادةٍ لمثلث قاعدته المرئي ، فقامَ خطاً مستقيماً بوسط القاعدة على زوايا قائمة ، ومعلوم أنَّهُ أصغرُ ممَّا يقوم عليها مِنْ سائرِ الخطوطِ ، فزيادة ذلك البعدِ لغيره منعَتْ مِنْ رؤية طرفي المرئي . قلنا : فيلزمُ إذا انتقل المرئي إلى مقدارِ تلك الزيادةِ مِنَ البعد ألا يُرى ، والمشاهدة تُكَذِّبُهُ يعني : أنَّهم أجابوا عمّا نَقَضَ عليهم مِنْ رؤية الكبير صغيراً بأن قالوا : لا نُسلَّمُ استواءَ نسبةِ أجزاء الكبيرِ معَ البعدِ إلى الرائي حتى يلزمَ أن يراه على حالِهِ كبيراً ؛ وذلك لأنَّ الجزء الواقع في وسط المرئي أقرب إلى الناظرِ مِنَ الجزء الواقع في طرفِهِ . وبيانُهُ : أَنَّهُ إذا خرج خطَّانِ شعاعيانِ مَوْهُومان كساقَيْ مُثلث ، ونفرضُ أنَّ قاعدة هذا المثلث - أي : الخطَّ الذي يقومُ عليهِ ذانِكَ الساقان - جسم المرئي البعيد ، فيكون هذان الساقانِ على طرفيه ، وخرجَ مِنْ نقطةِ العينِ خط آخرُ قسم ذلكَ المثلث بنصفين ، وقامَ (1) ( بلغ )
They said: That is only because the ray penetrated from an acute angle of a triangle whose base is the visible object, so it established a straight line in the middle of the base at right angles, and it is known that it is smaller than the other lines that stand upon it, so the increase of that distance over others prevented the seeing of the two ends of the visible object. We said: Then it would follow that if the visible object is moved to the amount of that increase of distance, it would not be seen, but direct observation falsifies it. Meaning: That they answered what was refuted against them regarding seeing a large object as small by saying: We do not accept that the proportion of the parts of the large object with distance to the viewer is equal such that it would necessarily be seen as large; that is because the part in the middle of the visible object is closer to the viewer than the part at its side. And its explanation: That if two imaginary ray lines emerge like the two legs of a triangle, and we suppose that the base of this triangle—i.e., the line upon which those two legs stand—is the body of the distant visible object, then these two legs are at its two ends, and another line emerges from the point of the eye dividing that triangle into two halves, and it stood (1) (it has reached).
Source pages p. 512

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer pair regarding the perception of distant large objects as small. The opponents (said: 'They said') argue that the ray passes through an acute angle of a triangle whose base is the object, and a line at the center is shorter than side lines, so the far ends are not seen, causing the object to appear smaller. Sanusi rebuts (said: 'We said') that this would imply that if the object is moved to the distance of that increase, it would not be seen at all, but observation contradicts this. He then explains the opponents' fuller answer: they deny that all parts are equally proportioned to the viewer, because the middle part is closer. He then gives a geometric explanation with two ray lines and a dividing line.

مثلث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That geometric optics alone can explain why distant objects appear small.
Correction: Sanusi shows this leads to false predictions about invisibility, so perception involves more than simple geometry.

Checkpoint
What is the main rebuttal Sanusi gives to the opponents' triangle argument?
A) The triangle is not a right triangle.
B) The object would become invisible if moved to a certain distance, which is false.
C) The ray cannot penetrate the air.
D) The eye cannot perceive small objects.

Key Takeaway
Even the most plausible physical explanation must be tested against observable reality; Sanusi shows that the triangle argument fails because it would predict invisibility for objects at slightly greater distances.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Sanusi shows that a clever logical model can be demolished by simple observation. Where in your life have you clung to a neat explanation or excuse that contradicts what you clearly see—like a sin you rationalize or a relationship you know is unhealthy? Will you today stop hiding behind logic and face the truth you already know?
?
The opponents modified their argument rather than abandon their theory. When your own beliefs are challenged by evidence, do you adjust your reasoning to preserve your comfort, or do you humbly change what you believe? What is one belief you hold that you have never seriously tested against reality?
?
If you were in this debate, would you have been quick to accept the geometric explanation because it sounds scientific, or would you have paused to test it against everyday experience like Sanusi did? Right now, is there an assumption about Allah or His creation that you have accepted from scholars but never verified through reflection on the world? What step will you take this week to examine it?
