The Emission Theory of Vision Refuted: Accidents and Color
Sanusi presents a dialectical refutation of the ray-emission theory of vision.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the dialectical objections against the claim that vision occurs by ray contact, and how the proponents' revisions fail to explain the visibility of accidents and the invisibility of color in distant objects.

Core Idea
Ray contact alone cannot explain vision; accidents are seen without contact and colors disappear at a distance.

This lesson examines a classic kalam objection to the emission theory of vision. The argument proceeds through a series of exchanges: first showing that accidents like motion are seen without ray contact, then challenging the revised claim that whatever inheres in a contacted object is seen, and finally pointing out that a distant body is seen while its color (which inheres in it) is not. This forces the proponents into a contradiction or an arbitrary restriction.

Guided Path
1
Read the full objection and responses
Read the Arabic passage once to see the flow: objection from accident visibility, retraction, revised claim, further objection based on tastes/smells, restriction to 'possible vision', final objection about distant color.
Notice the pattern: the proponents keep narrowing their claim to avoid counterexamples.
2
Identify the first objection
The first objection: accidents (motion, rest, etc.) are seen even though rays cannot contact them because rays are bodies and accidents are not bodies.
This undercuts the original claim that only what rays contact is visible.
3
Trace the revised claim and its problem
The proponents revise: visible is what rays contact OR what inheres in what rays contact. The objector then says tastes and smells inhere in contacted bodies, so they should be visible. The proponents reply that only things possible of vision are seen.
The restriction 'possible of vision' is ad hoc—tastes and smells are not considered visible by consensus.
4
Analyze the final refutation
The final objection: a distant body is seen but its color is not. The color inheres in the body (which is contacted by rays), and color is something that can be seen by agreement. So the revised claim fails because the color should be seen but is not.
This shows that ray contact is not sufficient for vision; other factors (like distance) matter.
5
Reflect on the dialectical method
Notice how the argument uses logical consistency: each new claim is tested against a counterexample. The objector does not assert a new theory but shows internal contradictions.
This is typical of kalam argumentation—refutation by reductio ad absurdum.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومما ينقصُ عليهم : رؤية الأكوانِ معَ أَنَّ الأشعة لم تتصل بها. قالوا : المرئي ما اتَّصلَتْ بهِ ، أو قام بما اتَّصلَتْ بهِ. قلنا : فيلزمُ أنْ تُرى الطعوم والروائح ؛ لقيامها بما اتَّصلَتْ بهِ. قالوا : إِنَّما ذلك فيما يقبلُ الرؤية. قلنا : ها هو البعيدُ يُرى دونَ لونِهِ. هذا ممَّا نقض عليهم قولَهم بأنَّ سببَ الرؤيةِ اتصال الأشعة بالمرئي ، وأنَّهُ إِنَّما يُرى ما اتصلَ بهِ الشعاع ، وهذا قول الأقدمينَ منهم ، فقيل لهم : قد رُثِيَتِ الأكوان - وهي : الحركة والسكون ، والاجتماع والافتراقُ - والألوان ، والأشعة لا تتصل بها ؛ إذ الأشعة أجسام ، والعرضُ يستحيلُ عليه مماسَّةُ الأجسامِ. فأجابوا بالرجوع عن قولهمُ الأوَّلِ ، وقالوا : المرئي ما اتَّصلَ بهِ الشعاع أو قام بما اتَّصلَ بهِ الشعاع ، فقيل لهم : فيلزم أن تُرى الطعوم والروائح ؛ لأنَّها قائمة بما اتَّصلَ بهِ الشعاعُ ، فقالوا : إِنَّما نقولُ : ما قام بما اتَّصلَ بهِ الشعاعُ يُرى إذا كانَ ممَّا تجوزُ رؤيتُهُ. وهذا الذي أوردتُم .. عندنا لا تجوزُ رؤيتُهُ ، فقيل لهم : فالجسم إذا كان بعيداً يُرى ولا يُرى لونه ، وإِنَّما رُبَّيَ على زعمكم لاتصال الشعاع بهِ ، واللونُ قائم به ، وهو ممَّا يجوز أنْ يُرى اتفاقاً بيننا وبينكم.
And among what refutes them: seeing the accidents (akwān) while the rays did not contact them. They said: 'The visible is what the ray has contacted, or what inheres in what the ray has contacted.' We said: 'Then it would be necessary that tastes and smells be seen, because they inhere in what the ray has contacted.' They said: 'That is only for what admits of vision.' We said: 'Here is the distant [object] seen without its color.' This is among what refutes their saying that the cause of vision is the contact of rays with the visible, and that only what the ray has contacted is seen—this is the saying of their ancients. So it was said to them: 'The accidents—which are motion and rest, conjunction and separation—and colors have been seen, while rays do not contact them, because rays are bodies and it is impossible for an accident to touch bodies.' So they answered by retracting their first saying, and said: 'The visible is what the ray has contacted or what inheres in what the ray has contacted.' So it was said to them: 'Then it would be necessary that tastes and smells be seen, because they inhere in what the ray has contacted.' They said: 'We only say: what inheres in what the ray has contacted is seen if it is something whose vision is possible. And this [tastes and smells] which you have brought—according to us, its vision is not possible.' So it was said to them: 'When a body is distant, it is seen but its color is not seen, and yet [the body] is seen—according to your claim—due to the ray's contact with it, while the color inheres in it, and it [color] is something whose vision is possible by agreement between us and you.'
Source pages p. 514

Commentary
This passage presents a series of objections and rejoinders regarding the emission theory of vision. The author begins by stating an objection: the accidents (akwān: motion, rest, conjunction, separation) and colors are seen without the rays contacting them, because rays are bodies and accidents cannot touch bodies. The proponents of emission then revise their theory: the visible is either what the ray contacts or what inheres in what the ray contacts. The objector responds: then tastes and smells would have to be seen, since they inhere in what the ray contacts. The proponents counter: only things that admit of vision are seen—tastes and smells do not admit of vision. The objector then points to a distant body: the body is seen but its color is not seen, even though the color inheres in the body and is something that admits of vision by agreement. This refutes the revised theory because if the color inheres in what the ray contacts (the body), it should be seen, but it is not. The argument progresses dialectically, showing the inadequacy of explaining vision solely by ray contact.

Key Terms
الأكوان
الشعاع
الرؤية
اتصال
العرض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What does the final objection in the passage demonstrate about the proponents' revised claim?
A) That ray contact is necessary for vision of all objects.
B) That color is always seen when the body is seen.
C) That the revised claim fails because a distant body's color is not seen even though it inheres in a contacted body and admits of vision.
D) That tastes and smells are invisible because they do not admit of vision.

Key Takeaway
The emission theory of vision fails because it cannot coherently explain both the visibility of accidents and the invisibility of distant colors.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever clung to a belief because it was familiar, even when clear evidence or logic contradicted it? What would it take for you to honestly admit that belief was wrong, as the proponents in this passage had to retract their first claim?
?
The proponents kept adding conditions to save their theory. Are there areas in your own faith or worldview where you make exceptions or add qualifiers to avoid facing a fundamental flaw? Will you examine those today?
?
This lesson shows that even well-reasoned theories can collapse under logical scrutiny. Consider something you think is obviously true about your life or your deen—what would it mean if you found a single counterexample that unraveled it? Are you willing to test your certainties?

Two Objections to the Emission Theory
Sanusi objects that the sun's disc is seen without intermediate birds, and emission requires unfelt reliance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand two key objections to the emission theory of vision: the problem of seeing distant objects without seeing intermediate bodies, and the absence of felt reliance in the eye.

Core Idea
Emission theory fails because we see what our rays should have already hit.

Sanusi continues his refutation of the emission theory of vision with two fresh arguments. First, he points out that we see the sun's disc without seeing the birds that the rays would have to contact first. Second, he argues that emission requires a physical reliance that the seer does not feel. These objections aim to show that the theory is both empirically and rationally flawed.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyze. Then read the translation. Let the author's rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the passage ends abruptly; it is a continuous argument.
2
Identify the first objection
Find the argument about the sun disc and the birds. Understand why this contradicts the emission theory.
The key is that the rays must contact intermediate objects first.
3
Identify the second objection
The passage argues that emission requires a reliance (iʿtimād) that the seer does not feel. Locate this argument.
4
Examine the rebuttal and response
The proponents say eyelid movement causes emission. Find how Sanusi responds to this.
He points out that we see without moving eyelids, and even if conceded, directions are limited.
5
Summarize the two objections
In your own words, restate both objections and why they challenge the emission theory.
This will solidify your understanding.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومما ينقض عليهم : رؤيةُ قُرْص الشمس مع عدم رؤية ما دونَها مِنَ الطير إذا علا في الجو ، ورؤية النار على البعدِ دونَ ما دونَها ، وأيضاً : الانبعاثُ إِنَّما يكون عن اعتماد إلى جهة ، والسبر يبطله. يعني : ممَّا ينقصُ قولَهم برؤيةِ ما اتّصلَ بِهِ الشعاعُ : أَنَّا نرى قُرْصَ الشمس ولا نرى الجوارح التي بيننا وبينها إذا تعالَتْ في الجو ، ونرى في البرِّيَّةِ النارَ مِنْ بُعدِ ولا نرى ما بيننا وبينَها ، مع أنَّ الشعاع لم يتصل بقَرْصِ الشمس ولا بالنارِ إلا بعد أنِ اتَّصل بالأجسام التي بيننا وبينهما ، فهذا كلُّه يدلُّ على بطلانِ انبعاث الأشعةِ ، وَأَنَّ اتصالها سبب الرؤية : وأيضاً مما يبطل انبعاث الأشعة فى الرؤية : أنَّ انبعاثها لا يكون إلا باعتماد عليها ، والرائي لا يُحِسُّ في عينه اعتماداً . فإن قالوا : حركة الأجفانِ تُوجِبُ خروجها لخفتها ، فأدنى اعتماد يخرجها قيل : الرائي يرى ولا يُحرِّكُ شيئاً مِنْ عينيهِ ، ولو سُلَّمَ ذلكَ فجهات الاعتماد بحَسَبِ السبرِ منحصرة في الجهات الست ، فإذا
And among what refutes them: seeing the disc of the sun without seeing what is below it of birds when it rises in the sky, and seeing fire from a distance without seeing what is below it; and also: emission only occurs by a reliance (iʿtimād) toward a direction, and empirical investigation (sabr) invalidates it. Meaning: among what refutes their saying regarding seeing what the ray has contacted: we see the disc of the sun and we do not see the raptors that are between us and it when they rise in the sky, and we see in the desert fire from a distance and we do not see what is between us and it, while the ray does not contact the disc of the sun nor the fire except after it has contacted the bodies that are between us and them. So all of this indicates the invalidity of the emission of rays and that their contact is the cause of vision. And also among what invalidates the emission of rays in vision: that their emission only occurs by a reliance upon them, and the seer does not sense in his eye any reliance. If they say: 'The movement of the eyelids necessitates their exit due to their lightness, so the slightest reliance brings them out,' it is said: 'The seer sees without moving anything of his eyes; and even if that is conceded, the directions of reliance, according to empirical investigation, are limited to the six directions, so if...'
Source pages p. 515

Commentary
This passage continues the refutation of the emission theory with two further arguments. First, the author points out empirical counterexamples: we see the sun's disc but not the birds between us and the sun, and we see fire at a distance but not the objects between us and the fire. According to the emission theory, the rays must first contact the intervening bodies before reaching the distant object, so those bodies should be seen—but they are not. This contradicts the theory. Second, the author argues that emission itself requires a 'reliance' (iʿtimād) or pressure from the eye, yet the seer does not feel any such reliance. The proponents might respond that the movement of the eyelids pushes the rays out because they are light. But the author rejoins: the seer sees without moving his eyelids at all, and even if movement is conceded, the directions of reliance are limited to six, which does not account for all objects seen. The text breaks off at 'so if' (fa-idhā), likely leading to a further conclusion that reliance cannot explain vision in multiple directions simultaneously.

Key Terms

الانبعاث
اعتماد
السبر
الأجفان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The emission theory of vision is correct because we see distant objects.
Correction: The theory fails because it cannot explain why we do not see intermediate bodies, nor does the seer feel any emission from the eye.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why does seeing the sun disc without seeing birds in between refute the emission theory?
A) Because birds are too small to be seen.
B) Because the rays must contact the birds before the sun, so the birds should be seen.
C) Because the sun emits its own light.
D) Because the birds are moving too fast.

Key Takeaway
The emission theory fails because it cannot account for seeing distant objects without intermediate visibility, and because vision does not involve any felt physical emission from the eye.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a contradiction between a long-held belief and clear sensory evidence—like seeing the sun disc without intermediate objects—are you willing to abandon your theory? Or do you, like the emission theorists, search for excuses? This lesson shows that the author prioritized empirical truth over a cherished model. Where in your life are you ignoring obvious evidence because it conflicts with what you want to believe? What would it cost you to accept the truth, and are you ready to pay that price?
?
The emission theorists invented an ad hoc explanation about eyelid movement to salvage their theory. Have you ever twisted logic or made excuses to defend a flawed understanding rather than humbly admitting error? Think of a specific instance. If you were confronted with the same kind of refutation, would you have the courage to say 'I was wrong' and change your mind, or would you cling to your position out of pride?
?
The reliance argument points out that the seer does not 'feel' any emission from the eye. Yet many people trust their feelings as evidence. How often do you mistake a lack of feeling for proof that something is not happening? This lesson challenges you to distinguish between subjective feeling and objective reality. In your spiritual life, are there truths from revelation that you reject because you don't 'feel' them? What would it take for you to submit to evidence even when your feelings say otherwise?

Refuting the Theory of Ray Emission in Vision
Sanusi refutes the theory that vision occurs via rays emitted from the eye.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the key arguments that falsify the theory of ray emission in vision.

Core Idea
Vision does not require rays from the eye; empirical evidence disproves it.

Sanusi presents a powerful refutation of the ancient theory that vision works by emitting rays from the eye. He uses everyday observations and logical reasoning to show why this theory fails. Let's examine his arguments step by step.

Guided Path
1
Understand the agreed premise
Read the opening statement: both sides agree that a distant object's color can be seen if rays connect to it. This sets the stage for the refutation.
2
First refutation: missing intermediate objects
Sanusi points out that we see the sun disk and fire without seeing the birds or air in between, contradicting the ray theory which would require rays to connect everything along the line of sight.
3
Second refutation: the leaning argument
Emission requires a physical leaning or exertion from the eye, but we feel no such thing. The objection about eyelid movement is countered by noting that leaning would be directional, yet we see all directions simultaneously.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Consider how these two arguments together form a strong case against ray emission. The theory cannot explain simultaneous multi-directional vision or the absence of intermediate objects.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كان بعيداً يُرى ولا يُرى لونه ، وإنما رُبَّيَ على زعمكم لاتصال الشعاع بهِ ، واللون قائم به ، وهو مما يجوز أن يُرى اتفاقاً بيننا وبينكم. ومما ينقض عليهم : رؤية قرص الشمس مع عدم رؤية ما دونها من الطير إذا علا في الجو ، ورؤية النار على البعد دون ما دونها ، وأيضاً : الانبعاث إنما يكون عن اعتماد إلى جهة ، والسبر يبطله. يعني : مما ينقض قولهم برؤية ما اتصل به الشعاع : أنا نرى قرص الشمس ولا نرى الجوارح التي بيننا وبينها إذا تعالت في الجو ، ونرى في البرية النار من بعد ولا نرى ما بيننا وبينها ، مع أن الشعاع لم يتصل بقرص الشمس ولا بالنار إلا بعد أن اتصل بالأجسام التي بيننا وبينهما ، فهذا كله يدل على بطلان انبعاث الأشعة ، وأن اتصالها سبب الرؤية. وأيضاً مما يبطل انبعاث الأشعة في الرؤية : أن انبعاثها لا يكون إلا باعتماد عليها ، والرائي لا يحس في عينه اعتماداً. فإن قالوا : حركة الأجفان توجب خروجها لخفتها ، فأدنى اعتماد يخرجها. قيل : الرائي يرى ولا يحرك شيئاً من عينيه ، ولو سلم ذلك فجهات الاعتماد بحسب السبر منحصرة في الجهات الست ، فإذا خص الاعتماد بجهة منها لزم ألا تنبعث الأشعة إلى غيرها ، فلا يرى سوى ما في جهة واحدة ، ولكنا نرى دفعة ما في الجهات الست فبطل ما تخيلوه.
It was far, [but] seen, and its color is not seen, and it was only raised, in your claim, due to the connection of the ray to it, and color is subsistent in it, and it is possible that it be seen, by agreement between us and you. And among what refutes them: seeing the disk of the sun while not seeing what is below it of birds when it rises in the sky, and seeing fire at a distance without what is below it; and also: emission only occurs by leaning toward a direction, and empirical investigation invalidates it. That is: among what refutes their statement that [vision occurs] by seeing what the ray connects to: that we see the disk of the sun and do not see the birds of prey that are between us and it when they rise in the sky, and we see in the desert fire from afar and do not see what is between us and it, even though the ray has not connected to the disk of the sun nor to the fire except after it connected to the bodies that are between us and them. All of this indicates the invalidity of the emission of rays, and that their connection is the cause of vision. And also among what invalidates the emission of rays in vision: that their emission only occurs by a leaning upon them, and the seer does not feel any leaning in his eye. If they say: the movement of the eyelids necessitates their exit due to their lightness, so the slightest leaning brings them out. It is said: the seer sees and does not move anything from his eyes, and if that is granted, then the directions of leaning, according to empirical investigation, are confined to the six directions. So if the leaning is specific to one direction from them, it would necessitate that rays not emit to others, so only what is in one direction would be seen, but we see simultaneously what is in the six directions; thus what they imagined is invalidated.
Source pages p. 515

Commentary
This passage presents a refutation of the theory of ray emission in vision. The author begins by stating an agreed premise: that a distant object can be seen despite its color not being seen, and that this is only possible if rays connect the eye to the object. Then he gives two counterarguments: First, we see the sun's disk without seeing the birds between us and the sun, and we see fire without seeing intermediate objects. If rays were emitted and connected to everything along the line of sight, we would see all intervening objects, but we do not. Second, emission of rays requires a physical leaning or exertion from the eye, but the seer feels no such exertion. An objection is raised: eyelid movement causes the rays to exit due to their lightness. The author responds: even if that were true, the leaning would have to be in a specific direction, so rays would only go in one direction at a time, yet we see all six directions simultaneously. Thus the theory is invalid.

Misconception: Some think that vision requires something to physically leave the eye and touch the object.
Correction: The passage shows that empirical evidence (missing intermediate objects) and logical reasoning (simultaneous multi-directional vision) disprove this.

Key Takeaway
The theory of ray emission in vision is false because empirical observations and logical reasoning about directionality show its inconsistency.

Refuting the Condition of Direct Facing
Sanusi refutes the condition of direct facing using the mirror and water example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the example of seeing oneself in a mirror refutes the condition of direct facing in the theory of vision, and how the opponent's ray-theory explanation is self-defeating.

Core Idea
You don't need to face something directly to see it — mirrors prove it.

In this passage, Sanusi argues against the Ash'ari condition that vision requires direct facing (muqabila). He uses the everyday example of seeing yourself in a mirror: you are not facing yourself, yet you see. The opponent's attempts to explain this via reflection are shown to be self-contradictory. This lesson walks through the dialectical exchange.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Notice how the argument unfolds as a back-and-forth between Sanusi and his opponents.
The passage is cut off at the end, but the main argument is clear from what is present.
2
Identify the opponent's claim
The opponents (ray theorists) claim that in a mirror, rays reflect back to the viewer because the surface is polished. According to them, one sees an 'impressed image' not oneself.
3
Understand Sanusi's two counter-arguments
First, if rays reflect and don't rest, the mirror itself would not be seen. Second, the image would not track the person's distance or movement, contradicting experience.
Sanusi uses logical consequences (luzum) to refute.
4
See how the qualification fails
The opponent tries to say the seer is 'in the ruling of the facing' via reflection. Sanusi points out that according to their own theory, the mirror and water themselves would remain unseen.
This shows the opponent's explanation is incoherent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إبطال اشتراط المقابلة: ثم لزوم المقابلة يبطل برؤية الإنسان نفسه في المرآة والماء. قالوا: لم تتشبت الأشعة فيهما لعدم التضريس، فانعكست إلى الرائي. يعني: قلنا: فيلزم ألا يرى المرآة والماء لعدم قاعدة الأشعة. قالوا: إنما رأى صورة منطبعة، لا نفسه. قلنا: فيلزم ألا تبعد ببعد، ولا تتحرك بحركته. أنه مما يبطل اشتراط المقابلة في الرؤية: رؤية الإنسان نفسه في المرآة والماء، ومحال أن يكون مقابلاً لنفسه. أجابوا: بأننا نشترط أن يكون المرئي مقابلاً أو في حكم المقابل، والرائي في هذه الصورة في حكم المقابل. قالوا: لأن الشعاع لما لاقى جسماً صقيلاً لم يتشبت به، فانعكس إلى الناظر، فرأى نفسه. رد عليهم: بأنه يلزم على ما ذكروه ألا يرى الماء ولا المرآة؛ إذ
Invalidation of the condition of direct facing: Then the necessity of direct facing is invalidated by a person seeing himself in the mirror and water. They said: the rays did not stand firm in them due to lack of roughness, so they reflected back to the seer. That is: We said: then it would necessitate that he not see the mirror and water due to the lack of a basis for the rays. They said: he only saw an impressed image, not himself. We said: then it would necessitate that it not distance itself with his distance, nor move with his movement. That which invalidates the condition of direct facing in vision: a person seeing himself in the mirror and water, and it is impossible that he be facing himself. They answered: we stipulate that the seen object be facing or in the ruling of the facing, and the seer in this case is in the ruling of the facing object. They said: because when the ray encountered a polished body, it did not stand firm in it, so it reflected to the viewer, and he saw himself. It was refuted against them: that what they mentioned necessitates that he not see the water nor the mirror; since
Source pages p. 516

Commentary
This passage argues against the condition that direct facing (muqabila) is required for vision, using the example of a person seeing themselves in a mirror or water. The author first states that the necessity of direct facing is invalidated by this example, as a person cannot be facing themselves. The opponents (theorists of ray emission) respond that the rays reflect off the polished surface back to the viewer, and what is seen is an impressed image, not the actual person. The author counters with two points: if the rays reflect, they would not see the mirror/water itself (since no rays 'rest' there), and the image would not follow the person's distance or movement. The opponent further attempts to qualify that the seer is 'in the ruling of the facing' via reflection. But the author refutes this by noting that, according to their own theory, the mirror and water themselves would not be seen. The argument is cut off, but the gist is clear: the mirror example demonstrates that direct facing is not necessary.

Key Terms
اشتراط المقابلة
انعكاس
صورة منطبعة
قاعدة الأشعة
جسم صقيل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that direct facing is always necessary for vision; the mirror example shows it is not when one sees oneself.
Correction: Seeing oneself in a mirror is a case of reflected vision, not direct facing, yet it is valid vision. This refutes the necessity of muqabila.

Checkpoint
How does the mirror example refute the condition of direct facing?
A) The mirror is transparent, so no facing is needed.
B) A person sees himself without being directly in front of himself.
C) The mirror emits its own rays.
D) The image in the mirror is a different object.

Key Takeaway
The mirror example proves that direct facing is not a necessary condition for vision, as one can see oneself without being directly in front of oneself.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you look in the mirror, you see your physical self. But what about your spiritual self? This lesson shows that direct facing is not necessary to see; similarly, your inner faults are visible even if you don't directly confront them. What are you avoiding looking at in your soul, and when will you stop turning away?
?
Sanusi used simple, everyday examples (mirror, water) to refute a deeply held condition. Where in your own faith practice have you added unnecessary conditions or assumptions that a simple check against reality could correct? Will you examine them today?
?
The opponents claimed one sees only an 'impressed image,' but Sanusi demanded logical consistency: if that were true, the image wouldn't track your movement. Are you consistent in your own beliefs? Where do you hold contradictory ideas about Allah, yourself, or worship that you haven't followed to their logical consequences?

Preventers (Mawani') for Unseen Existents
Sanusi defines the preventer (man') and refutes Mu'tazila.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why unseen existents require a preventer (man') in the eye, and how this principle refutes the Mu'tazila denial of such a meaning.

Core Idea
Unseen existents are unseen because a preventer blocks perception in the eye.

Sanusi explains that when an observable existent is not seen, it is because a meaning that contradicts perception (a preventer) subsists in the eye. This principle derives from the rule that a receiver must have the thing or its opposite. He then addresses a potential objection about infinite preventers and refutes the Mu'tazila, except Abu al-Hudhayl al-`Allaf who agreed partially but erred.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage carefully, then follow the literal translation to grasp Sanusi's argument.
Notice the logical progression: claim, derivation, objection handling, refutation.
2
Identify the key rule about the receiver
The rule states that a receiver (qabil) of a thing must either have that thing, its opposite, or its like. Sanusi uses this to derive the necessity of a preventer when perception is absent.
This rule is foundational in kalam.
3
Understand the concept of preventer (man')
A preventer is a meaning (ma'na) that contradicts perception and therefore prevents seeing. It is the opposite of perception in the eye.
Sanusi distinguishes this from mere structural defects.
4
Analyze the refutation of Mu'tazila
Sanusi notes that most Mu'tazila denied the preventer, attributing blindness to structural issues. He then corrects Abu al-Hudhayl who accepted the preventer but wrongly allowed the eye to be devoid of both perception and preventer.
The correct view requires either perception or its opposite in the eye.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( وما لم يُرَ مِنَ الموجوداتِ فلموانع ) يعني : كلُّ ما يجوزُ أنْ يُدرَكَ إذا لم يقم بالمحل إدراك يتعلَّقُ بهِ.. لزم أن يقوم بالمحل معنى يُضادُّ إدراكه ؛ وهو المعبّر عنه في اصطلاح الموحدين بالمانع ، وهو مأخوذُ مِنَ القاعدة التي سبق بيانُها ؛ وهي أنَّ القابل للشيء لا يخلو عنه أو عن ضده أو مثلِهِ ، وتتعدد تلك الموانع بحسب تعدُّدِ تلك الموجوداتِ التي لم تُرَ ، ولا يلزمُ مِنْ تعددِ الإدراكات وتعدُّدِ موانعها قيام ما لا يتناهى عدده بالعين ؛ لأنَّ إدراك البصرِ إِنَّما يتعلَّقُ بالموجود ، والموجودات متناهية ، فإدراكاتها وموانعها متناهية . وأنكرت المعتزلة أنْ يقوم بالعين هذا المعنى الذي سميناه مانعاً ، وحملوا العمى على انتقاض البنية ، إلا أبا الهذيل العلاف ؛ فإنَّهُ اعترف بالمانع على الوجه الذي نقولهُ ، غيرَ أَنَّهُ يُجوِّزُ عُرُو المحلَّ عنهُ وعن الإدراك ، وذلك باطل .
His statement: '(And what is not seen of existents is due to preventers)' means: anything that can be perceived, if perception that belongs to it does not subsist in the locus, then it is necessary that a meaning that contradicts its perception subsists in the locus; and it is what is called in the terminology of the monotheists (muwahhidun) a 'preventer' (man'), and it is taken from the rule previously explained: that the receiver of a thing is not devoid of it or its opposite or its like. And those preventers multiply according to the multiplicity of those existents that are not seen. And it does not follow from the multiplicity of perceptions and the multiplicity of their preventers that what is infinite in number subsists in the eye, because the perception of sight only pertains to existents, and existents are finite, so their perceptions and preventers are finite. And the Mu'tazila denied that this meaning which we call a preventer subsists in the eye, and they attributed blindness to the disruption of the structure, except Abu al-Hudhayl al-`Allaf, for he admitted the preventer in the way we say, except that he allowed the locus to be devoid of it and of perception, and that is false.
Source pages p. 520

Commentary
This passage is an explanation and proof regarding why some existents are not seen. The author (Sanusi) first states the principle: for any possible object of vision that is not perceived, a meaning (ma'na) that contradicts that perception must subsist in the eye; this meaning is called a 'preventer' (man'). This is derived from a general rule: a receptive entity (qabil) must either have the thing or its opposite or its like. The preventer is the opposite of the perception. These preventers are multiple corresponding to the multiple unseen existents. A potential objection is raised: if there are many perceptions and many preventers, would that not lead to infinite qualities in the eye? The author refutes this: since the number of existents is finite (mutanahiya), the perceptions and preventers are also finite. Then the author notes that most Mu'tazila denied this concept of a preventer and explained blindness as a structural defect, except Abu al-Hudhayl al-`Allaf who agreed with the existence of a preventer but incorrectly allowed the eye to be devoid of both perception and preventer. The author rejects this as false, presumably because the rule requires either perception or its opposite (preventer) to be present.

Key Terms
المانع
الإدراك
المحل
القابل
الضد
المتناهية
المعتزلة
أبو الهذيل العلاف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Blindness or lack of vision is purely a structural defect (e.g., damage to the eye).
Correction: According to Sanusi, the absence of vision requires a specific contradictory meaning (man') that subsists in the eye, not merely a structural issue.

Checkpoint
Why do the perceptions and their preventers not become infinite according to this passage?
A) Because the preventer is one single meaning
B) Because existents are finite
C) Because the eye cannot hold infinite qualities
D) Because the Mu'tazila deny them

Key Takeaway
Unseen existents are explained by the presence of preventers (mawani') in the eye, a principle derived from the rule of the receiver and defended against Mu'tazila denials.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This lesson explains that unseen existents are hidden due to a preventer in the eye. Are there truths about yourself—faults, patterns, or sins—that you have not 'seen' because you have allowed a preventer of pride or fear to block your perception? Will you ask Allah to remove that man' today so you can see yourself clearly?
?
The Mu'tazila reduced blindness to mere structural defect, denying the metaphysical preventer. In your life, do you explain away spiritual realities (like the influence of shaytan or the presence of barakah) with purely material reasons, missing the deeper truth? What step will you take this week to look beyond the material and acknowledge the unseen causes of your experiences?
?
Abu al-Hudhayl accepted the preventer but allowed the eye to be empty of both perception and preventer—a compromise Sanusi declares false. Where in your faith do you hold a 'middle ground' that actually contradicts the clear rule of revelation? Are you willing to abandon that comfortable half-truth and fully submit to what Allah and His Messenger have commanded?

Rational Proof from God's Free Agency
Sanusi begins a rational proof using God's free agency to refute the Mu'tazilite position.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Ash'ari doctrine of God's free agency refutes the Mu'tazilite claim that certain acts are obligatory upon God.

Core Idea
If God were obligated, He would not be free—and freedom is essential to His agency.

Sanusi begins his rational refutation of the Mu'tazilites by recalling a foundational truth: God is an agent by choice (fāʿil bi-l-ikhtiyār), not by compulsion or nature. This principle, already proven earlier, serves as the cornerstone of the argument. From it, he draws two logical consequences that undermine the idea of any obligation upon God. Although the passage is cut short, the direction of the proof is clear.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Notice that Sanusi first announces the two types of proofs (rational and transmitted) but only begins the rational one here.
The proof is incomplete in the provided text.
2
Identify the central premise
Locate the statement that God is 'an agent by choice, not by compulsion or nature.' This is the key premise that the Mu'tazilites reject.
This premise has already been proven earlier in the text.
3
Follow the first logical consequence
If an act were obligatory upon God, He would not be free in that act, since freedom requires the ability to do or not do. Obligation negates choice.
This directly refutes the Mu'tazilite position.
4
Examine the second argument (incomplete)
Sanusi begins a second argument: if the obligator (mūjib) were eternal, the act would also have to be eternal. The text cuts off, but the implication is that obligations cannot apply to God.
The conclusion would likely be that nothing can be obligatory upon God.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أدلة أهل الحق وفساد مذهب المعتزلة ]
ثم دليلُ فسادِ مذهبهم ، ودليلُ صحة ما يقولُ أهل الحقِّ .. المعقول والمنقولُ :
أما المعقولُ : فلانه سبحانه فاعل بالاختيار ، لا بالإيجاب والطبيعة ، وقد سبق برهان ذلكَ ، فلو وجب عليهِ فعل لَمَا كَانَ مختاراً فيه ؛ إذ المختارُ هو الذي يتأتّى منه الفعل والتركُ ، ولأنَّ الموجِبَ في حقِّهِ إنْ كانَ قديماً لزمَ قدم الفعل ، وقد سبق وجوب
[The proofs of the people of truth and the corruption of the Mu'tazilite doctrine.] Then the proof of the corruption of their doctrine, and the proof of the correctness of what the people of truth say – the rational and the transmitted: As for the rational: because He – glorified is He – is an agent by choice (fāʿilun bi-l-ikhtiyār), not by compulsion or nature, and the proof of that has preceded. So if an act were obligatory upon Him, He would not be free in it; for the free agent is the one from whom both the act and its abandonment proceed. And because the obligator (al-mūjib) with respect to Him, if it were eternal, would necessitate the eternity of the act, and the obligation has preceded...
Source pages p. 523

Commentary
This passage begins a rational proof (al-maʿqūl) to demonstrate the corruption of the Mu'tazilite doctrine and the correctness of the Ash'ari position. The author states that the proof consists of both rational and transmitted arguments, but here he presents only the rational. The central premise is that God is an agent by choice (fāʿil bi-l-ikhtiyār), not by compulsion or nature. This has already been proven earlier (reference to p. 268). From this it follows that if an act were obligatory upon God, He would not be free in performing it, because freedom entails the ability to do or not do the act. The author then presents a second argument: if the obligator (al-mūjib) – the cause that makes the act necessary – were eternal, then the act would also have to be eternal (since an eternal cause necessitates an eternal effect). He then starts a third point ('wa qad sabaqa wujūb') but the text breaks off. The argument is incomplete in the provided window.

الإيجاب
الطبيعة
المختار
الفعل والترك
الموجب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some Mu'tazilites believe that certain acts (like rewarding the obedient and punishing the disobedient) are rationally obligatory upon God, even if He is free.
Correction: Sanusi argues that obligation contradicts God's free agency because obligation would remove His ability to choose. Since God is an agent by choice, nothing can be obligatory upon Him.

Checkpoint
What is the key premise of the rational proof against the Mu'tazilites according to this passage?

B) God is an agent by choice, not by compulsion or nature.
C) The Mu'tazilites reject the hadith about Qadariyya.
D) Actions are not created by God.

Key Takeaway
Because God is an agent by choice, no act can be obligatory upon Him, refuting the Mu'tazilite position.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever felt that God owes you something—good health, a reward, or an answer to a prayer? How does Sanusi's argument that no act is obligatory upon God challenge that feeling right now?
?
Do you sometimes act as if your good deeds force God to repay you? How can you train yourself to see your worship as a gift you give, not a debt God owes?
?
Reflect on a time you doubted God's wisdom because He didn't act as you expected. How might the truth that God acts by free choice (not obligation) reshape your trust in Him?

The Rational Proof of God's Freedom of Action
Sanusi presents the rational proof that God is an agent by choice, refuting any obligation upon Him.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Sanusi's rational argument that God is an agent by choice, not by necessity, and why this refutes the Mu'tazila claim that lutf is obligatory upon Him.

Core Idea
God's freedom means no act is obligatory upon Him.

Sanusi begins his rational refutation of the Mu'tazila by establishing a foundational principle: God acts by choice, not by compulsion. This principle, once proven, dismantles the notion that anything—including lutf—can be obligatory for God. In this lesson, we will examine the proof that God is a free agent and the logical consequences of that freedom.

Guided Path
1
Read the rational proof
Read the passage carefully, noting the opening claim and the structure of the argument.
2
Trace the two branches of the obligator
Identify the conditional statements: if the obligator is eternal, then act is eternal (impossible); if originated, then God's essence is subject to originated things (impossible).
3
Understand the conclusion
Notice that the conclusion is that God does not gain perfection from acts; acts only indicate His existence and attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم دليلُ فسادِ مذهبهم ، ودليلُ صحة ما يقولُ أهل الحقِّ .. المعقول والمنقولُ :
أما المعقولُ : فلانه سبحانه فاعل بالاختيار ، لا بالإيجاب والطبيعة ، وقد سبق برهان ذلكَ (۲) ، فلو وجب عليهِ فعل لَمَا كَانَ مختاراً فيه ؛ إذ المختارُ هو الذي يتأتّى منه الفعل والتركُ ، ولأنَّ الموجِبَ في حقِّهِ إنْ كانَ قديماً لزمَ قدم الفعل ، وقد سبق وجوب الحدوثِ لما سواه (۱) ، وإنْ كانَ حادثاً لزمَ أيضاً اتصافُ ذاتِهِ تعالى بالحوادثِ ، وقد سبق استحالتها عليهِ (۲) ؛ فهو سبحانَهُ لا يتجدَّدُ لهُ بفعلٍ مِنَ الأفعالِ كمال ، ولا بتركِهِ نقص ، بل هو الكامل بذاتِهِ وصفاتِهِ في أزلِهِ وفيما لا يزالُ ، وإنَّما الأفعال دلتنا نحنُ على معرفة وجودِهِ وصفاتِهِ على حسَبِ ما هو مُقرَّرٌ ، وإلى هذا المعنى أشرتُ بقولي : ( والأفعالُ كلُّها ؛ خيرها وشرها . . . ) إلى آخره .
وأيضاً : لو وجبَ عليهِ صلاح العبدِ لَمَا كَلَّفَهُ ؛ لما فيهِ مِنْ تعريضِهِ للمعصية .
فإن قيل : إنَّما كَلَّفَهُ ليثيبه
قلنا : هو قادرٌ أن يعطيه ذلكَ مِنْ غيرِ عمل ولا تكليف
ولو وجبَ عليهِ الأصلحُ لَمَا خلق الكافر الفقيرَ ؛ لأنَّ الأصلح لهُ ألا يخلقَهُ ؛ حتى لا يكونَ مُعذَّباً في الدنيا والآخرةِ .
وأيضاً : الأصلحُ للعبادِ أن يخلقهم في الجنَّةِ ، فلو وجب عليهِ لَمَا خلقهم في الدنيا
•
وبالجملة : لو وجبَ عليهِ الأصلحُ لَمَا وُجِدَتْ محنةٌ دنيويَّةٌ ولا أخروية
Then the proof of the corruption of their doctrine, and the proof of the correctness of what the people of truth say: the rational and the transmitted. As for the rational: because He, exalted, is an agent by choice, not by necessitation and nature, and its proof has preceded. So if an act were obligatory upon Him, He would not be free in it; for the free agent is the one from whom both action and omission can proceed. And because the obligator with respect to Him: if it were eternal, then the eternity of the act would follow, and the necessity of contingency for everything other than Him has preceded; and if it were originated, then the description of His essence with originated things would also follow, and their impossibility for Him has preceded. So He, exalted, does not acquire any perfection by any act, nor any deficiency by omitting it. Rather, He is perfect in His essence and attributes in His eternity and in what never ceases. Acts only indicate to us the knowledge of His existence and attributes according to what is established. And to this meaning I referred by my saying: 'All acts, their good and their evil...' to its end. Also: if the welfare of the servant were obligatory for Him, He would not have charged him, because of the exposure to sin involved. If it is said: He charged him only to reward him. We say: He is able to give him that without any action or charge. And if what is most beneficial were obligatory for Him, He would not have created the poor disbeliever, because what is most beneficial for him is not to create him, so that he would not be punished in this world and the hereafter. Also: what is most beneficial for the servants is to create them in Paradise. So if it were obligatory, He would not have created them in this world. In summary: if the most beneficial were obligatory for Him, no worldly or otherworldly trial would exist.
Source pages p. 523

Commentary
This passage presents the rational proof of the corruption of the Mu'tazila doctrine and the correctness of the Ash'ari position. The author begins by stating that the proof is both rational and transmitted, but focuses on the rational. The main argument: God is an agent by choice, not by necessity. If an act were obligatory upon Him, He would not be free, because a free agent can either act or refrain. Moreover, the obligating factor (al-mujib) must be either eternal or originated. If eternal, then the act would be eternal, contradicting the contingency of all things other than God. If originated, then God's essence would be subject to originated events, which is impossible. Therefore, God is perfect in His essence and attributes, and acts merely indicate His existence and attributes, not add perfection. Then the author gives additional arguments: if securing the welfare of the servant were obligatory, God would not have imposed the test of obedience and sin (taklif). To the objection that taklif is for reward, he replies that God can give reward without any test. Further, if the most beneficial were obligatory, God would not have created the poor disbeliever (who will suffer), nor would He have placed people in this world instead of directly in Paradise. The conclusion: if the most beneficial were obligatory, no trial (mihna) would exist at all, contradicting the observed reality.

Key Terms
فاعل بالاختيار
موجِب
حوادث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think God is obliged to do what is most beneficial for His creatures.
Correction: Sanusi argues that if that were true, God would not be free, and the existence of trials would be impossible.

Checkpoint
According to the rational proof, if something obligatory (mujib) were originated, what would follow?
A) The act would be eternal.
B) God's essence would be described with originated things.
C) The act would be impossible.
D) God would be a free agent.

Key Takeaway
God is a free agent, and no act is obligatory upon Him.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God is under no obligation to act, then what right do you have to demand anything from Him in your prayers? Have you ever complained to God as if He owes you something? How can you reshape your relationship with Him to be one of humble request rather than entitlement?
?
Sanusi says God does not acquire any perfection by His acts nor deficiency by omitting them. Do you live as if your own actions define your worth before God, or do you rest in His perfect acceptance? When you fail, do you truly believe God's love for you does not change because your deeds do not affect His essence?
?
This proof frees God from being constrained by any external standard of 'good' or 'obligation'. Are there areas in your life where you secretly believe God should act according to your sense of justice? Will you release that expectation and trust that His free will is perfectly wise, even when it doesn't match your plans?

Arguments Against Obligatory Welfare and the Most Beneficial
Sanusi presents additional rational arguments against the Mu'tazila doctrine of obligatory welfare.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Sanusi's corollary arguments that if welfare or the most beneficial were obligatory on God, then taklif and worldly trials would be pointless.

Core Idea
If God were obliged to secure welfare, He would not impose tests.

After establishing that God is a free agent, Sanusi applies this principle to specific Mu'tazila claims. He argues that if securing the servant's welfare were obligatory, then God would not have imposed the burden of taklif (religious obligation). Likewise, if the most beneficial were obligatory, then disbelievers would not have been created, nor would people be placed in this world of trials. This lesson examines these concrete implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the additional arguments
Read the passage about taklif, the poor disbeliever, and creation in Paradise. Observe how each argument shows a contradiction if obligation were true.
2
Examine the objection and reply
The Mu'tazila might object that taklif is for reward. The reply is that God can give reward without test.
3
Synthesize the summary
The summary states that if the most beneficial were obligatory, no trials would exist, which contradicts reality.

•
وبالجملة : لو وجبَ عليهِ الأصلحُ لَمَا وُجِدَتْ محنةٌ دنيويَّةٌ ولا أخروية
Then the proof of the corruption of their doctrine, and the proof of the correctness of what the people of truth say: the rational and the transmitted. As for the rational: because He, exalted, is an agent by choice, not by necessitation and nature, and its proof has preceded. So if an act were obligatory upon Him, He would not be free in it; for the free agent is the one from whom both action and omission can proceed. And because the obligator with respect to Him: if it were eternal, then the eternity of the act would follow, and the necessity of contingency for everything other than Him has preceded; and if it were originated, then the description of His essence with originated things would also follow, and their impossibility for Him has preceded. So He, exalted, does not acquire any perfection by any act, nor any deficiency by omitting it. Rather, He is perfect in His essence and attributes in His eternity and in what never ceases. Acts only indicate to us the knowledge of His existence and attributes according to what is established. And to this meaning I referred by my saying: 'All acts, their good and their evil...' to its end. Also: if the welfare of the servant were obligatory for Him, He would not have charged him, because of the exposure to sin involved. If it is said: He charged him only to reward him. We say: He is able to give him that without any action or charge. And if what is most beneficial were obligatory for Him, He would not have created the poor disbeliever, because what is most beneficial for him is not to create him, so that he would not be punished in this world and the hereafter. Also: what is most beneficial for the servants is to create them in Paradise. So if it were obligatory, He would not have created them in this world. In summary: if the most beneficial were obligatory for Him, no worldly or otherworldly trial would exist.
Source pages p. 523

Commentary
This passage presents the rational proof of the corruption of the Mu'tazila doctrine and the correctness of the Ash'ari position. The author begins by stating that the proof is both rational and transmitted, but focuses on the rational. The main argument: God is an agent by choice, not by necessity. If an act were obligatory upon Him, He would not be free, because a free agent can either act or refrain. Moreover, the obligating factor (al-mujib) must be either eternal or originated. If eternal, then the act would be eternal, contradicting the contingency of all things other than God. If originated, then God's essence would be subject to originated events, which is impossible. Therefore, God is perfect in His essence and attributes, and acts merely indicate His existence and attributes, not add perfection. Then the author gives additional arguments: if securing the welfare of the servant were obligatory, God would not have imposed the test of obedience and sin (taklif). To the objection that taklif is for reward, he replies that God can give reward without any test. Further, if the most beneficial were obligatory, God would not have created the poor disbeliever (who will suffer), nor would He have placed people in this world instead of directly in Paradise. The conclusion: if the most beneficial were obligatory, no trial (mihna) would exist at all, contradicting the observed reality.

Key Terms
صلاح
تكليف
محنة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Mu'tazila believe that God must always choose the best option for each individual.
Correction: Sanusi shows that this would prevent taklif and creation in this world of trials, which we observe.

Checkpoint
What does Sanusi conclude would happen if the most beneficial were obligatory on God?
A) Taklif would be more severe.
B) God would still create disbelievers.
C) No trial would exist.
D) All would be created in Paradise.

Key Takeaway
The existence of taklif and trials proves that God is not obliged to secure the most beneficial for each creature.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God were obligated to give you what is best for you in every situation, would you ever experience hardship? Yet you do. How does this reality change the way you respond to difficulties instead of demanding relief?
?
Consider a trial you are currently facing. The passage says that if the most beneficial were obligatory, no trial would exist. Since trials do exist, what does that imply about what God owes you? What is your duty toward Him in this trial instead of expecting immediate comfort?
?
The passage uses the example of the poor disbeliever to argue that non-existence could be better for him. How does this challenge any assumption you hold that God must treat everyone equally in this life? Are you willing to trust that His wisdom may involve letting some suffer for purposes beyond your understanding?

No Rational Good or Evil: The Ash'ari Position
Sanusi argues against rational good and evil and defines them by revelation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Ash'ari school denies that actions are inherently good or evil and how only divine command determines moral value.

Core Idea
Good and evil are defined by Allah's command, not by reason.

Sanusi presents the Ash'ari argument that moral judgments cannot be made by the intellect alone. He explains that calling an action 'good' or 'evil' in relation to Allah is impossible, and that all actions are equal in themselves. Therefore, we only know good and evil through revelation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the passage is a single continuous argument.
2
Identify the main claim about Allah
Sanusi begins by denying the application of 'good' and 'evil' to Allah. Why does he say this is problematic? Look for the phrase 'لإيهامهما معنى يستحيل في حقه'.
This is about divine transcendence.
3
Understand the equality of actions
The author states that all actions are equal in relation to Allah because He creates them all directly. Find the sentence that says 'الأفعال كلها مستوية' and reflect on its implications.
4
Grasp the definition of good and evil
At the end, Sanusi gives the Ash'ari definitions of good and evil purely in terms of divine command. Write down the key phrases: 'ليس الحسن شرعاً إلا ما قيل فيه افعلوه' and 'وليس القبيح شرعاً إلا المقول فيه لا تفعلوه'.
This is the core of the Ash'ari ethical theory.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقصارى الأمر : أنَّا نمنع على هذا التقرير إطلاق هذين اللفظين بالنسبة إليه تعالى ؛ لإيهامهما معنى يستحيلُ في حقه ؛ وهو ما ذكرنا أنَّهما يدلان عليه عرفاً ، لا لما ذكروهُ مِنْ دلالتهما على نفي الغرض
(1)
بيان أن العقل لا يُحسنُ ولا يُقبِّحُ
وبطلان التحسين والتقبيح فيما فيه الجزاء ]
وإذا عرفت بما ذُكِرَ عدم رجحان بعض الأفعال على بعض بالنسبة إليه تعالى .. عرفت جهالة مَنْ تسَوَّرَ على الغيبِ ورأى أنَّ العقل يتوصل دون شرع إلى إدراك الحسن والقبيح عندَهُ جلَّ وعلا ، على أنَّهُ لو سُلّم لهم ذلك جدلاً لم يجزم العقل بشيء ؛ لتعارض أوجُهِ مِنَ النظر في ذلكَ مُتضادة فإذاً ؛ لم نعرف وجوبَ الإيمان ولا تحريم الكفران إلا بعد مجيء الشرع
لمَّا حقَّقَ أَنَّ مذهب أهلِ السنَّةِ أَنَّ الأفعال كلَّها مسندة إلى اللهِ تعالى ابتداءً مِنْ غيرِ واسطةٍ ، لا تأثير لغيرِهِ في شيء منها .. لزمَ أَنَّ الأفعال كلها مستويةٌ ؛ لا يتّصفُ بعضُها بالحسنِ مِنْ حيثُ ذاته أو صفته ، ولا يتّصفُ بعضُها بالقبحِ مِنْ حيثُ ذاتُهُ أو صفتُهُ ، فلا مجال للعقل إذاً في إدراك حكم شرعي لها ؛ إذ لا سبب له على ما عرفت .
فليسَ الحسَنُ شرعاً عند أهل الحقِّ إلا ما قيلَ فيهِ : افعلوه ، وليس القبيح شرعاً إلا المقول فيه : لا تفعلوه ، وتخصيص كلّ واحدٍ منهما بما اختصَّ بِهِ مِنَ الأَفعالِ لَا عَلَّةَ لَهُ
And the sum of the matter: we deny, on this explanation, the application of these two terms to Him, the Exalted, because they suggest a meaning impossible with respect to Him, which is what we mentioned that they indicate conventionally, not because of what they mentioned of their indicating the negation of purpose. (1) Statement that the intellect does not deem good nor deem evil, and the invalidity of (the theory of) rational good and evil in matters requiring reward and punishment. And if you have understood, from what has been mentioned, the lack of preferability of some actions over others with respect to Him, the Exalted, you will understand the ignorance of one who leaps into the unseen and opines that the intellect can, without revelation, attain to knowledge of the good and the evil with Him, the Mighty and Majestic. Moreover, even if that were granted to them as a dialectical assumption, the intellect would not be certain of anything, because of conflicting aspects of consideration in that matter. Therefore, we do not know the obligation of faith nor the prohibition of disbelief except after the coming of revelation. Since it has been established that the doctrine of the People of the Sunnah is that all actions are ascribed to Allah, the Exalted, primarily without intermediary, that no other has any effect in any of them, it necessarily follows that all actions are equal; none of them is characterized as good in terms of its essence or attribute, nor is any of them characterized as evil in terms of its essence or attribute. So there is no place for the intellect in perceiving a legal judgment for them, since there is no cause for that, as you have learned. So 'good' according to the People of Truth is only that concerning which it is said: 'Do it,' and 'evil' is only that concerning which it is said: 'Do not do it,' and the specification of each one of them to the actions to which it is assigned has no cause.
Source pages p. 535

Commentary
This passage is a proof from the Ash'ari school against the possibility of rational judgments of good and evil. The author begins by forbidding the application of terms like 'good' and 'evil' to Allah's actions because they imply a purpose or benefit, which is impossible for Him. He then declares that the intellect cannot rationally discover good and evil; even if it could, it would be uncertain due to conflicting considerations. The only way to know the obligation of faith and the prohibition of disbelief is through revelation. Next, he establishes the Ash'ari principle: all actions are directly created by Allah with no intermediary, so they are all equal in relation to Him—none is inherently good or evil. Therefore, the intellect cannot derive any legal ruling for them. Finally, he defines 'good' and 'evil' purely in terms of divine command: what is commanded is good, what is prohibited is evil, and there is no rational cause for why each action is assigned to either category.

Key Terms
الحسن
القبيح
العقل
الشرع
التحسين والتقبيح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Many people think that reason alone can determine which actions are good or evil without needing revelation.
Correction: The Ash'ari school teaches that all actions are equal in themselves and only become good or evil based on Allah's command, which is known through revelation.

Checkpoint
According to the Ash'ari position in this passage, how do we know that an action is good or evil?
A) Through rational intuition about the action's essence
B) Through divine command: what Allah commands is good, what He prohibits is evil
C) Through the inherent benefit or harm of the action
D) Through consensus of the Muslim community

Key Takeaway
Good and evil have no objective reality; they are purely determined by Allah's command.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If good and evil are defined solely by Allah's command, and there is no inherent morality, then what is the basis for your own moral judgments? Do you ever rely on your own sense of right and wrong apart from what Allah has commanded? Reflect on an action you considered 'obviously good' – is it truly good because Allah commanded it, or did you assume it was good by reason? How does that shift your accountability?
?
Sanusi says we do not know the obligation of faith or the prohibition of disbelief except after revelation. Have you ever felt that believing in Allah is 'obviously' rational or that disbelief is 'obviously' wrong? According to this lesson, that feeling is not from reason but from the light of faith. Do you rely on that light or on your own intellect? What does that imply about the source of your certainty?
?
The passage ends with 'and the specification of each one to the actions to which it is assigned has no cause.' This means Allah's commands are not arbitrary but also not based on any reason we can grasp. Does that trouble you? Do you find it difficult to submit to commands that have no apparent reason? How can you cultivate a heart that accepts Allah's commands without needing to understand the wisdom behind them?

Mu'tazila's Threefold Classification of Rational Good and Evil
Sanusi presents the Mu'tazila classification of rational judgments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Mu'tazila classify voluntary actions into three categories based on the source of rational knowledge: necessary, reflective, and revelatory.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazila see reason as the primary judge, but revelation still informs some actions.

The Mu'tazila are famous for arguing that good and evil are known by reason. But they do not claim that reason alone can judge every action. In this passage, Sanusi explains their nuanced view: some actions are known necessarily, some by reflection, and some only through revelation. Understanding this classification is key to grasping the broader Mu'tazila ethical framework.

Guided Path
1
Read the opening claim
Begin by reading the first sentence: the Mu'tazila assert that voluntary actions are good or evil from the aspect of reason. Notice that this is the foundational claim.
They do not deny revelation, but they prioritize reason.
2
Identify the three categories
The passage then lists three kinds of actions: those perceived necessarily, those perceived by reflection, and those requiring revelation. Work through each example given.
Examples: necessary – beneficial truthfulness; reflective – harmful truthfulness; revelatory – fasting on specific days.
3
Understand the role of revelation
Focus on the third category. The Mu'tazila argue that revelation merely informs about an action's inherent moral state, not that it creates the ruling. They compare it to a wise man reporting a herb's property.
This shows their commitment to objective morality independent of divine command.
4
Test your understanding
Try to come up with your own example for each type, or match the given examples to the categories. Then answer the checkpoint question.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقالَتِ المعتزلة : الأفعالُ الاختياريَّةُ حسنة وقبيحةً مِنْ جهة العقل ، وزعموا أنَّ منها : ما يدركه العقلُ بالضرورة ؛ كحسن الصدقِ النافع والإيمانِ ، وقبْحِ الكذب الضارّ والكفرانِ ، ومنها : ما يدركه بالنظر ؛ كحسْنِ الصدْقِ الضار، وقبْحِ الكذب النافع ، ومنها : ما يقفُ عن إدراكه إلا بإنباء الشرع ؛ كحسْنِ صومِ آخرِ يومٍ مِنْ رمضانَ وقبح صوم أوَّلِ يومٍ مِنْ شوَّالٍ ، وقضوا أنَّ الشارع في هذا النوع مخبر عن حال المحل ، لا أنَّه أنشأ فيه حكماً ، قالوا : كالحكيم الذي يخبرُ أنَّ هذا العَقَارَ حارٌ أو بارد
ثم اختلفوا : فذهبَ القدماء إلى أنَّ الأفعال حسنةٌ وقبيحة لذاتها ، وقال قوم منهم : هي حسنة وقبيحة لصفة لازمة ؛ كالصوم المشتمل على كسْرِ الشهوة المقتضي عدم المفسدة، وكالزنا المشتمل على اختلاط الأنساب المقتضي ترك تعاهد الأولادِ ، وقال قوم منهم بالفرق
And the Mu'tazila said: Voluntary actions are good and evil from the aspect of reason. They claimed that among them are those which the intellect perceives necessarily, such as the goodness of beneficial truthfulness and faith, and the evilness of harmful lying and disbelief; and among them are those which it perceives by reflection, such as the goodness of harmful truthfulness and the evilness of beneficial lying; and among them are those which it is prevented from perceiving except by the announcement of revelation, such as the goodness of fasting the last day of Ramadan and the evilness of fasting the first day of Shawwal. And they ruled that the Lawgiver in this type is an informer about the state of the object, not that He institutes a ruling in it. They said: like the wise man who informs that this herb is hot or cold. Then they differed: the 'Ancients' held that actions are good and evil by their essences; a group among them said they are good and evil by a necessary attribute, like fasting which includes breaking the lust that necessitates the absence of corruption, and like adultery which includes mixing lineages that necessitates abandonment of caring for children; and a group among them held a distinction...
Source pages p. 536

Commentary
This passage presents the Mu'tazila doctrine on rational good and evil. It begins by stating that voluntary actions are good or evil from the standpoint of reason. The Mu'tazila classify such actions into three types: first, those known necessarily by reason (e.g., goodness of beneficial truthfulness, evil of harmful lying); second, those known through reflection (e.g., goodness of harmful truthfulness, evil of beneficial lying); third, those that can only be known through revelation (e.g., fasting on specific days). For the third type, they maintain that the Lawgiver merely informs about the inherent state of the action, not that He creates the ruling—comparing it to a wise man reporting a herb's property. Then the passage notes an internal Mu'tazili disagreement: the 'Ancients' (probably earlier Mu'tazila) held that actions are good or evil by their essences; another group held that they are good or evil by a necessary attribute (giving examples like fasting having the attribute of breaking lust, and adultery mixing lineages). A third group held a distinction (the text breaks off before detailing this).

Key Terms
الضرورة
النظر
إنباء الشرع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Mu'tazila deny any role for revelation in ethics.
Correction: They affirm that some moral judgments require revelation (e.g., specific days of fasting), but revelation only reports an objective moral fact.

Checkpoint
According to the Mu'tazila as presented, what is the role of revelation in the third type of actions?
A) Revelation creates the moral ruling
B) Revelation informs about the inherent state of the action
C) Revelation overrides reason
D) Revelation is not needed for any action

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila hold that moral values are objective and known by reason, but for some actions revelation is needed to inform about their inherent moral status.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If moral value is objective and your reason already knows the evil of harmful lying, why do you still find yourself tempted to lie when it benefits you? What does that struggle reveal about the state of your heart? Today, commit to one situation where you will choose truth despite personal cost.
?
The Mu'tazila say revelation only informs about moral facts; it does not create them. When you read Allah's commands, do you receive them as confirmation of what your conscience already tells you, or do you resist because they clash with your desires? Which response is more faithful? This week, pick one command you find difficult and ask yourself: is it my reason or my desire that objects?
?
Consider the third category: actions whose moral value is only known by revelation. Have you ever dismissed a revealed ruling as 'unreasonable'? If so, what does that suggest about your view of revelation's role? Before tomorrow, reflect on how you can submit to Allah's information even when it challenges your independent reasoning.

Mu'tazila's Internal Disagreement on the Basis of Good and Evil
Sanusi outlines three Mu'tazila positions on the basis of good and evil.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three positions within the Mu'tazila on what makes an action good or evil: by essence, by a necessary attribute, or a distinction.

Core Idea
Not all Mu'tazila agree: actions may be good/evil by essence, attribute, or a nuanced distinction.

While the Mu'tazila share the core belief that good and evil are rational, they disagree on the precise basis. Some early Mu'tazila held that actions are intrinsically good or evil by their very essence. Others argued that actions are good or evil due to a necessary attribute they possess. A third group proposed a distinction between the two. This lesson unpacks these positions as presented by Sanusi.

Guided Path
1
Read the internal disagreement introduction
Start from 'ثم اختلفوا' which signals the internal dispute. The Mu'tazila agreed on the rational basis but split on its grounding.
2
Identify the first position
The 'Ancients' (قداء) held that actions are good or evil by their essences (لذاتها). Reflect on what this means: the action itself is the source of its value.
This is a strong objectivist position.
3
Identify the second position
Another group said actions are good or evil by a necessary attribute (صفة لازمة). Examples: fasting has the attribute of breaking lust; adultery mixes lineages.
The attribute is inseparable from the action.
4
Note the third position
The passage ends with 'a group held a distinction' (بالفرق) but does not elaborate. Understand that there is a third view, though its details are not given here.
This shows further diversity within the school.
5
Reflect on the significance
Consider why this debate matters: it affects how the Mu'tazila understand the objectivity of ethics and the relationship between actions and their moral values.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقالَتِ المعتزلة : الأفعالُ الاختياريَّةُ حسنة وقبيحةً مِنْ جهة العقل ، وزعموا أنَّ منها : ما يدركه العقلُ بالضرورة ؛ كحسن الصدقِ النافع والإيمانِ ، وقبْحِ الكذب الضارّ والكفرانِ ، ومنها : ما يدركه بالنظر ؛ كحسْنِ الصدْقِ الضار، وقبْحِ الكذب النافع ، ومنها : ما يقفُ عن إدراكه إلا بإنباء الشرع ؛ كحسْنِ صومِ آخرِ يومٍ مِنْ رمضانَ وقبح صوم أوَّلِ يومٍ مِنْ شوَّالٍ ، وقضوا أنَّ الشارع في هذا النوع مخبر عن حال المحل ، لا أنَّه أنشأ فيه حكماً ، قالوا : كالحكيم الذي يخبرُ أنَّ هذا العَقَارَ حارٌ أو بارد

Commentary
This passage presents the Mu'tazila doctrine on rational good and evil. It begins by stating that voluntary actions are good or evil from the standpoint of reason. The Mu'tazila classify such actions into three types: first, those known necessarily by reason (e.g., goodness of beneficial truthfulness, evil of harmful lying); second, those known through reflection (e.g., goodness of harmful truthfulness, evil of beneficial lying); third, those that can only be known through revelation (e.g., fasting on specific days). Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All Mu'tazila agree that actions are good or evil by their essence.
Correction: The passage shows at least two other positions: by necessary attribute and by distinction.

A) The goodness of faith

D) The distinction between good and evil

The Mu'tazila disagreed on whether actions are good/evil by essence, by a necessary attribute, or by another distinction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The Mu'tazila debated whether actions are good or evil by essence or by attribute. When you face a moral decision, do you rely more on your own reasoning or on divine revelation? How does that affect your obedience to Allah? This week, before making a questionable choice, consciously ask: 'What does Allah command in this situation?' and act accordingly.
?
Consider the examples given: fasting is good because it breaks lust, adultery is evil because it mixes lineages. Do you evaluate your own deeds by their apparent worldly consequences or by their alignment with Allah's command? What is one sin you continue to commit because you see no immediate harm? Repent and seek Allah's forgiveness tonight.
?
The early Mu'tazila believed actions are good/evil by essence—independent of revelation. Does this thought ever tempt you to think you can determine right and wrong without the Quran and Sunnah? How can you humble your intellect before divine wisdom? Commit to reading a verse about obedience every morning for a week.

The Ash'ari Doctrine of Moral Neutrality of Actions
Sanusi states the Ash'ari position that actions are not intrinsically good or bad.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari position that actions have no intrinsic moral value and that moral judgments are established solely by divine command.

Core Idea
Actions are morally neutral; only divine law gives them goodness or evil.

This passage sets out the core Ash'ari stance on ethics and law. Imam Sanusi explains that actions are not inherently good or bad; rather, their moral status is determined entirely by God's command. This principle distinguishes Ash'ari theology from rationalist ethical theories.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic text once to get a feel for the language, then read the translation to grasp the central claim about moral neutrality.
Pay attention to the terms 'حسن' and 'قبح'.
2
Identify the three key claims
The passage makes three interconnected claims: (1) actions are equal with no intrinsic good or evil, (2) the intellect cannot perceive a legal ruling, (3) good and evil are defined only by divine command. Find each in the text.
3
Reflect on the theological implications
Consider why this doctrine emphasizes God's sovereignty. Ask yourself: What would change if actions had intrinsic moral value?
This connects to the broader Ash'ari emphasis on divine will and power.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كلها مستويةٌ ; لا يتّصفُ بعضُها بالحسنِ مِنْ حيثُ ذاته أو صفته ، ولا يتّصفُ بعضُها بالقبحِ مِنْ حيثُ ذاتُهُ أو صفتُهُ ، فلا مجال للعقل إذاً في إدراك حكم شرعي لها ; إذ لا سبب له على ما عرفت . فليسَ الحسَنُ شرعاً عند أهل الحقِّ إلا ما قيلَ فيهِ : افعلوه ، وليس القبيح شرعاً إلا المقول فيه : لا تفعلوه ، وتخصيص كلّ واحدٍ منهما بما اختصّ بِهِ مِنَ الأَفعالِ لَا عَلَّةَ لَهُ
All of them are equal; none of them is described as good by virtue of its essence or attribute, and none of them is described as evil by virtue of its essence or attribute. So there is no scope for the intellect to perceive a legal ruling for them, since there is no cause for it, as you have learned. So 'good' in the Law, according to the people of truth, is only that which is said about it: 'Do it', and 'evil' in the Law is only that about which it is said: 'Do not do it'. And the specification of each one of them with what it is specifically assigned among actions has no cause.
Source pages p. 536

Commentary
This passage defines the Ash'ari position on moral valuation. The author states that all actions are equal in themselves—none is intrinsically good or bad by essence or attribute. Therefore, the intellect cannot independently determine a legal ruling for an action, as there is no rational cause for such a ruling. Instead, good and bad are purely legal designations: good is what the Law commands, evil is what the Law prohibits. The specific assignment of goodness or badness to particular actions has no underlying cause; it is simply a matter of divine will.

Key Terms
الحسن
القبح
أهل الحق
العقل
حكم شرعي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think Ash'aris deny any reality to moral goodness or evil, or that they are simply arbitrary.
Correction: Ash'aris affirm that good and evil are real categories, but they are entirely defined by divine command, not by intrinsic properties of actions.

Checkpoint
According to the Ash'ari position in this passage, what defines an action as 'good' (حسن) in the Law?
A) Its intrinsic nature
B) Its beneficial consequences
C) The fact that the Law says 'Do it'
D) The intellect's independent judgment

Key Takeaway
Actions have no intrinsic moral value; good and evil are determined solely by divine command.

?

?
'Good is only what God commands.' Is there an area of your life where you are acting as if something is good or evil based on your own judgment rather than what God has commanded? What would it cost you to submit?
?
The specification of actions has no cause. Does this comfort you (because God's will is absolute) or disturb you (because it seems arbitrary)? How does this truth affect your trust in God's wisdom even when you don't understand His commands?
