Mu'tazila Three Categories of Rational Knowledge
Sanusi presents the three types of rational moral perception and the Lawgiver as informer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazila classification of moral knowledge into necessary, reflective, and revealed categories, and their view of the Lawgiver's role.

Core Idea
Reason independently knows good and evil in three distinct ways.

The Mu'tazila were known for asserting that human reason can discern moral values without revelation. In this passage, Sanusi presents their categorization of moral judgments into three types: those known by necessity, those known by reflection, and those known only through divine law. He also explains their unique view that for the third type, the Lawgiver merely informs, not creates.

Guided Path
1
Read the Mu'tazila claim
Start by reading the opening statement that voluntary actions are good or evil by reason, then identify the three categories.
Notice the examples given for each category.
2
Understand necessary vs reflective
Compare the first two categories: what is known necessarily (like beneficial truth) vs what requires reflection (like harmful truth). Ask why harmful truth is good? Reflect on utilitarian rationale.
3
Examine the revealed category
The third category includes actions whose moral value is only known through revelation. Note the examples of fasting on specific days.
The passage says the Lawgiver is just informing, not creating the ruling.
4
Analyze the Lawgiver's role
Consider the analogy of the wise man informing about a herb's properties. This means moral values exist objectively, and revelation just reveals them.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقالَتِ المعتزلة : الأفعالُ الاختياريَّةُ حسنة وقبيحةً مِنْ جهة العقل ، وزعموا أنَّ منها : ما يدركه العقلُ بالضرورة ; كحسن الصدقِ النافع والإيمانِ ، وقبْحِ الكذب الضارّ والكفرانِ ، ومنها : ما يدركه بالنظر ; كحسْنِ الصدْقِ الضار، وقبْحِ الكذب النافع ، ومنها : ما يقفُ عن إدراكه إلا بإنباء الشرع ; كحسْنِ صومِ آخرِ يومٍ مِنْ رمضانَ وقبح صوم أوَّلِ يومٍ مِنْ شوَّالٍ ، وقضوا أنَّ الشارع في هذا النوع مخبر عن حال المحل ، لا أنَّه أنشأ فيه حكماً ، قالوا : كالحكيم الذي يخبرُ أنَّ هذا العَقَارَ حارٌ أو بارد . ثم اختلفوا : فذهبَ القدماء إلى أنَّ الأفعال حسنةٌ وقبيحة لذاتها ، وقال قوم منهم : هي حسنة وقبيحة لصفة لازمة ; كالصوم المشتمل على كسْرِ الشهوة المقتضي عدم المفسدة، وكالزنا المشتمل على اختلاط الأنساب المقتضي ترك تعاهد الأولادِ ، وقال قوم منهم بالفرق بين القبيح فهو قبيح لصفتِهِ ، والحَسَنِ فهو حسن لذاتِهِ ، حجتهم أنَّ الذواتِ كلَّها مستويةٌ ، والتمييز إنَّما هو بالصفاتِ ; فلو قَبحَ الفعلُ لذاتِهِ لزمَ قُبْحُ فعل الله تعالى . و قالَ الجبائي وأتباعُهُ : الفعلُ يحسنُ أو يقبحُ بوجه واعتبار ; كضرب اليتيم يحسنُ إِنْ كانَ لتأديب ، ويقبحُ إِنْ كانَ لغيره .
And the Mu'tazila said: Voluntary actions are good and evil from the perspective of reason. They claimed that among them: what the intellect perceives necessarily, like the goodness of beneficial truthfulness and faith, and the evil of harmful lying and disbelief; and among them: what it perceives by reflection, like the goodness of harmful truthfulness and the evil of beneficial lying; and among them: what stops from its perception except by the announcement of the Law, like the goodness of fasting on the last day of Ramadan and the evil of fasting on the first day of Shawwal. And they ruled that the Lawgiver in this type is informing about the state of the locus, not that He creates a ruling in it. They said: Like the wise man who informs that this herb is hot or cold. Then they differed: the earlier ones (al-qudama') held that actions are good and evil by their essences. And a group among them said: They are good and evil by a necessary attribute, like fasting which involves breaking the desire, entailing absence of corruption, and like adultery which involves mixing of lineages, entailing abandonment of caring for children. And a group among them said with the distinction: the evil is evil by its attribute, and the good is good by its essence. Their argument is that all essences are equal, and distinction is only by attributes; so if an action were evil by its essence, it would necessitate the evil of Allah's action. And al-Jubba'i and his followers said: An action is good or evil by a respect and consideration, like striking an orphan: it is good if it is for discipline, and evil if it is for something else.
Source pages p. 536

Commentary
This passage presents the Mu'tazila doctrine on moral reasoning and its internal divisions. The author begins by stating the Mu'tazila position: voluntary actions are rationally good or evil. They categorize actions into three types: those known necessarily (e.g., goodness of beneficial truth), those known by reflection (e.g., goodness of harmful truth), and those known only by revelation (e.g., fasting on specific days). For the third type, they consider the Lawgiver as merely informing, not creating the ruling. Then the author details four Mu'tazila subgroups: (1) the early ones (al-qudama') who say actions are good/evil by essence; (2) those who say by a necessary attribute; (3) those who distinguish: evil by attribute, good by essence, arguing that if evil were by essence, then Allah's actions would be evil; (4) al-Jubba'i and his followers who say by respect and consideration (e.g., striking an orphan can be good or evil depending on intent).

Key Terms
المعتزلة
حسن
قبح
العقل
الضرورة
النظر
الشرع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Mu'tazila believe reason alone can know all moral values.
Correction: They recognize a third category known only through revelation, though they still consider the moral value objective.

Checkpoint
According to the Mu'tazila, which of the following is an example of an action known to be good or evil by necessity?
A) The goodness of harmful truthfulness
B) The evil of harmful lying
C) The goodness of fasting on the last day of Ramadan
D) The evil of beneficial lying

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila classify moral knowledge into three types: necessary, reflective, and revelation-dependent, and they view the Lawgiver as an informer rather than a creator of moral values.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever felt absolutely certain that something was morally right or wrong, only to later discover that your certainty was based on your own reasoning rather than divine revelation? How does the Mu'tazila distinction between necessary and revelation-dependent knowledge challenge you to re-examine your moral convictions? Will you submit even your strongest moral intuitions to the Lawgiver's announcement, or do you secretly trust your own judgment above His?
?
The Mu'tazila claim that the Lawgiver is merely an informer about moral values, not a creator. Do you live as though God's commands reveal pre-existing moral facts, or as though He creates what is right and wrong by His will? Which view makes you more accountable to His every command? Examine your heart: is there any command of Allah that you resist because it conflicts with your own sense of good and evil?
?
The third category of moral knowledge—what is only known through revelation—includes specific acts like fasting dates. In your daily life, do you treat revealed obligations as mere information about what is already good, or as divine decrees that demand your obedience regardless of your reason? What does your attitude toward a command you don't fully understand reveal about your relationship with the Lawgiver?

Mu'tazila Internal Divisions on Moral Basis
Sanusi details four internal Mu'tazila positions on the basis of moral valuation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four distinct Mu'tazila positions on what makes actions good or evil: by essence, by attribute, a mixed view, and by consideration.

Core Idea
Mu'tazila disagreed on the ontological basis of moral value.

After presenting the general Mu'tazila framework of rational moral knowledge, Sanusi details the internal disagreements among them. Some held that actions are good or evil by their very essence, others by a necessary attribute, a third group proposed a distinction between good (by essence) and evil (by attribute), while al-Jubba'i and his followers argued that moral value depends on respect and consideration.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four subgroups
Read the passage and list the four different positions mentioned. Note the key terms: لذاتها, لصفة لازمة, الفرق, بوجه واعتبار.
2
Understand the first view
The early ones (القدماء) held that actions are good or evil by their own essences. Consider the implication that moral properties are intrinsic.
3
Examine the second view
Another group said actions are good or evil by a necessary attribute, giving examples of fasting (breaks desire) and adultery (mixes lineages).
These attributes entail consequences.
4
Analyze the mixed view
A third group distinguished: evil is by attribute, good is by essence. Their argument: all essences are equal, so distinction must be by attributes; otherwise Allah's actions would be evil if evil were by essence.
This argument is aimed at preserving divine transcendence.
5
Consider al-Jubba'i's view
Al-Jubba'i and his followers said moral value is by respect and consideration (بوجه واعتبار), giving the example of striking an orphan which can be good or evil depending on intent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

The First Refutation: Commanding the Impossible
Sanusi presents the first refutation argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first kalam argument against Mu'tazila ethics: that if actions were intrinsically good or evil, then commanding the disbeliever to believe would be commanding the impossible, which is absurd.

Core Idea
If actions are inherently good, then impossible commands would be evil.

Sanusi begins his refutation of the Mu'tazila by recalling a foundational premise: human actions have no independent effect. He then launches the first of two arguments, showing that the Mu'tazila position leads to a contradiction: if actions were good or evil by their own essence, then Allah's command to the disbeliever to believe would be an impossible command, which the Mu'tazila themselves consider evil. This argument forces the Mu'tazila to abandon their claim.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Notice how Sanusi restates a previous premise before presenting his argument.
The premise about actions having no effect is crucial.
2
Identify the premise and conclusion
Find the conditional statement: 'If actions were good/evil by essence... then Allah would not have charged the disbeliever with faith.' Note that the consequent is false.
3
Analyse the explanation
The text explains why the consequent is impossible: because Allah knows the disbeliever will not believe, so the command is impossible, which the Mu'tazila consider evil.
This is a reductio ad absurdum.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والردُّ على الجميع : ما مضى مِنْ كونِ الأفعال لا تأثير للعبادِ في شيء منها حتى يحسنَ عقلاً طلبها منهم أو النهي عنها ، وإِنَّما مرجع الأحكام الشرعيَّةِ إلى بيان كون تلك الأفعال أمارة على ما جُعِلَتْ عليهِ مِنْ ثواب أو عقاب أو عدمهما ، ولوِ اتَّصفَ الفعلُ بالحسْنِ أوِ القبح لذاته أو لصفةٍ لازمةٍ .. لَمَا كَلَّفَ اللهُ الكافر بالإِيمَانِ ، والتالي باطل بالإجماع ، وبيانُ الملازمة : أنَّهُ تعالى علمَ أَنَّ الكافر لا يؤْمِنُ فتكليفه بالإيمان تكليف بمستحيل ، وهو قبيح عندهم . وأيضاً : لو كانَ الفعلُ حَسَناً أو قبيحاً لذاتِهِ أو لصفةٍ لازمةٍ . . لَمَا اختَلَفَ ؛ بأنْ يكونَ تارةً حَسَناً وتارةً قبيحاً ، ولاجتمعَ النقيضان في قول القائل : ( لأَكْذِبَنَّ غداً ) ، صدق أو كذبَ(۱) . (۱) قال العلامة المنجور في ( حاشيته » ( ص ۲۱۷ ) : ( هذا الإلزام والذي قبله إنما هو على الأقوال الثلاثة السابقة ، لا على الرابع ; لكون الفعل قد يتصف عليه بالحسن والقبح معاً ، لكن باعتبارين مختلفين ; كاجتماع الأبوة والبنوة في شخص بإضافتين مختلفتين ) ، وأراد بالنقيضين : المتنافيين ، وبيان اجتماعهما في قوله : ( لأكذبن غداً ( أنَّ ذلك الخبر إن طابق الواقع بأنْ كَذَبَ في الغد.. كان حسناً ; لصدقه بالمطابقة ، وقبيحاً ; لاستلزامه وقوع متعلقه الذي هو صدور =
And the refutation of all of them: what has preceded of the fact that actions have no effect from the servants in anything of them such that requesting them or prohibiting them could be rationally good, and that the reference of legal rulings is only to the clarification of those actions being indicators of what has been assigned to them of reward or punishment or their absence. And if an action were described as good or evil by its essence or a necessary attribute, then Allah would not have charged the disbeliever with faith, and the consequent is false by consensus. And the explanation of the correlation: that He, exalted, knew that the disbeliever will not believe, so charging him with faith is charging with something impossible, and that is evil (qabih) according to them. And also: if an action were good or evil by its essence or a necessary attribute, it would not differ, such that it would sometimes be good and sometimes evil, and the two contradictories would be combined in the statement of someone saying: 'I will certainly lie tomorrow', whether he tells the truth or lies. (1) The scholar al-Manjur said in his gloss (p. 217): 'This implication and the one before it apply only to the three preceding statements, not to the fourth; because an action may be described by it as both good and evil, but in two different respects, like the combination of fatherhood and sonship in a person by two different relations.' And he meant by 'contradictories' the mutually exclusive opposites, and the explanation of their combination in his saying 'I will certainly lie tomorrow' is that that report, if it matches reality by his having lied tomorrow, would be good because of its truth in matching, and evil because it necessitates the occurrence of its object which is the occurrence =
Source pages p. 537

Commentary
This passage contains the refutation of all the Mu'tazila views on the intrinsic goodness or badness of actions. The author first reiterates the earlier point (from a previous discussion) that actions have no effect from servants, so rational demand or prohibition is not based on intrinsic qualities; rather, legal rulings merely indicate what reward or punishment is attached. Then he presents two arguments. First: If actions were intrinsically good or evil, then Allah would not have commanded the disbeliever to believe, because He knows he will not believe, making it an impossible command, which the Mu'tazila themselves consider evil (qabih). Since the consequence is false (by consensus), the premise is false. Second: If actions were intrinsically good or evil, then they could not vary — but we see that the same action can be good in one context and evil in another. The example: someone says 'I will lie tomorrow.' If he lies tomorrow, the statement 'I will lie tomorrow' is true (good) but the act of lying is evil, so both good and evil would be combined, which is impossible. The footnote by al-Manjur clarifies that these two arguments apply only to the first three Mu'tazila views (by essence, by attribute, and the mixed view), not to the fourth view of al-Jubba'i, because that view allows an action to be both good and evil under different respects, just as a person can be both father and son in different relations.

Key Terms
الرد
تكليف
مستحيل
الكافر
الإيمان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the Mu'tazila do not consider commanding the impossible as evil, but the text states they do.
Correction: The Mu'tazila hold that commanding what cannot be done (like a disbeliever believing when Allah knows he won't) is qabih, so they are forced to reject their own premise.

Checkpoint
According to the first refutation, why would Allah not command the disbeliever to believe if actions were intrinsically good or evil?
A) Because the disbeliever is not obligated
B) Because it would be commanding the impossible, which is evil
C) Because faith is not an action
D) Because the disbeliever has free will

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila position on intrinsic moral values leads to the absurd conclusion that Allah commanded something impossible, which is evil.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read that Allah commands the disbeliever to believe despite knowing he never will, do you sometimes doubt the sincerity of your own obedience? Will you today trust His command more than your own feelings of futility?
?
If your actions have no inherent effect, how does that reshape your motivation to pray, fast, and give charity? Are you obeying Allah because He commands it or because you expect a result? Commit to identifying one action you will do solely because He commanded it, regardless of outcome.
?
The Mu'tazila claim that commanding the impossible is evil. How would your life change if you submitted to every command of Allah, even those that seem impossible for you to fulfill? Are there any commands you have avoided because you thought they were too hard? Will you repent and begin striving today?

The Second Refutation: The Liar Paradox
Sanusi presents the second refutation argument with a footnote clarifying scope.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Sanusi's second argument against intrinsic moral values: that a single action cannot be both good and evil, but the Mu'tazila position allows this contradiction, as shown by the statement 'I will lie tomorrow.', and you will grasp the footnote's clarification on the scope of this argument.

Core Idea
If actions are inherently good or bad, they cannot change, but the same act can be both.

Sanusi presents a second powerful argument. If actions were good or evil by their essence or a necessary attribute, they could not vary in moral value depending on context. Yet we find actions that are good in one respect and evil in another, such as the statement 'I will lie tomorrow.' The footnote by al-Manjur clarifies that this argument targets only the first three Mu'tazila views, not the fourth.

Guided Path
1
Read the second argument
Read the Arabic and translation. Note the conditional and the example of the liar statement.
2
Analyse the liar statement
See how the same statement can be both good (true) and evil (a lie) simultaneously. This is supposed to be impossible if actions are intrinsically one or the other.
The argument shows a contradiction.
3
Examine the footnote
Read al-Manjur's clarification: the argument applies only to the first three views, not to al-Jubba'i's fourth view which allows different respects.
This limits the refutation's scope.
4
Reflect on the scope
Consider why the fourth view avoids the contradiction: by distinguishing between two respects (truthfulness vs. lying), the same action can be good and evil without contradiction.
Similar to a person being both father and son.

Source Text

And the refutation of all of them: what has preceded of the fact that actions have no effect from the servants in anything of them such that requesting them or prohibiting them could be rationally good, and that the reference of legal rulings is only to the clarification of those actions being indicators of what has been assigned to them of reward or punishment or their absence. And if an action were described as good or evil by its essence or a necessary attribute, then Allah would not have charged the disbeliever with faith, and the consequent is false by consensus. And the explanation of the correlation: that He, exalted, knew that the disbeliever will not believe, so charging him with faith is charging with something impossible, and that is evil (qabih) according to them. And also: if an action were good or evil by its essence or a necessary attribute, it would not differ, such that it would sometimes be good and sometimes evil, and the two contradictories would be combined in the statement of someone saying: 'I will certainly lie tomorrow', whether he tells the truth or lies. (1) The scholar al-Manjur said in his gloss (p. 217): 'This implication and the one before it apply only to the three preceding statements, not to the fourth; because an action may be described by it as both good and evil, but in two different respects, like the combination of fatherhood and sonship in a person by two different relations.' And he meant by 'contradictories' the mutually exclusive opposites, and the explanation of their combination in his saying 'I will certainly lie tomorrow' is that that report, if it matches reality by his having lied tomorrow, would be good because of its truth in matching, and evil because it necessitates the occurrence of its object which is the occurrence =
Source pages p. 537

Commentary
This passage contains the refutation of all the Mu'tazila views on the intrinsic goodness or badness of actions. The author first reiterates the earlier point (from a previous discussion) that actions have no effect from servants, so rational demand or prohibition is not based on intrinsic qualities; rather, legal rulings merely indicate what reward or punishment is attached. Then he presents two arguments. First: If actions were intrinsically good or evil, then Allah would not have commanded the disbeliever to believe, because He knows he will not believe, making it an impossible command, which the Mu'tazila themselves consider evil (qabih). Since the consequence is false (by consensus), the premise is false. Second: If actions were intrinsically good or evil, then they could not vary — but we see that the same action can be good in one context and evil in another. The example: someone says 'I will lie tomorrow.' If he lies tomorrow, the statement 'I will lie tomorrow' is true (good) but the act of lying is evil, so both good and evil would be combined, which is impossible. The footnote by al-Manjur clarifies that these two arguments apply only to the first three Mu'tazila views (by essence, by attribute, and the mixed view), not to the fourth view of al-Jubba'i, because that view allows an action to be both good and evil under different respects, just as a person can be both father and son in different relations.

إلزام
تكليف

Correction: The footnote clarifies it does not apply to the fourth view of al-Jubba'i, which distinguishes between different respects.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila view that actions are intrinsically good or evil leads to contradictions, except for the view that distinguishes respects.

?

?
This lesson shows that the Mu'tazila who claimed actions are intrinsically good or evil fell into contradiction. Are you willing to submit your moral judgments completely to Allah's commands, even when they contradict your sense of what seems 'obviously' right or wrong?
?

Have you been relying on your own sense of right and wrong rather than seeking the Quran and Sunnah for every decision? Three Positions on Inherent Good and Evil
Presents the first two views on inherent good/evil: (1) ugly due to attribute/good due to essence, (2) al-Jubba'i's contextual view.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three classical positions on whether actions are inherently good or evil, including the argument from attribute vs. essence and al-Jubba'i's contextual view.

Core Idea
Moral value is either intrinsic (to essence or attribute) or contextual.

The debate on al-tahseen wa al-taqbeeh al-'aqliyy (rational good and evil) is central to Islamic theology. This passage introduces two opposing views: one that locates moral value in attributes, and another that sees it as context-dependent. Understanding these positions is essential before engaging the Ash'ari refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the two positions
Read the Arabic and translation carefully. Notice the first view: ugly due to attribute, good due to essence. Their argument relies on the equality of essences.
2
Identify the second view
Al-Jubba'i and his followers argue that actions are good or ugly based on aspect/consideration (wajh wa i'tibar). The example of striking an orphan illustrates this.
This is a Mu'tazilite position.
3
Compare the two views
Contrast the two views. The first ties moral value to the action's attribute or essence; the second ties it to the intention or circumstance.
Both are rejected by Ash'aris.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بين القبيح فهو قبيح لصفتِهِ ، والحَسَنِ فهو حسن لذاتِهِ ، حجتهم أنَّ الذواتِ كلَّها مستويةٌ ، والتمييز إنَّما هو بالصفاتِ ؛ فلو قَبحَ الفعلُ لذاتِهِ لزمَ قُبْحُ فعل الله تعالى. وقالَ الجبائي وأتباعُهُ : الفعلُ يحسنُ أو يقبحُ بوجه واعتبار ؛ كضرب اليتيم يحسنُ إِنْ كانَ لتأديب ، ويقبحُ إِنْ كانَ لغيره . والردُّ على الجميع : ما مضى مِنْ كونِ الأفعال لا تأثير للعبادِ في شيء منها حتى يحسنَ عقلاً طلبها منهم أو النهي عنها ، وإِنَّما مرجع الأحكام الشرعيَّةِ إلى بيان كون تلك الأفعال أمارة على ما جُعِلَتْ عليهِ مِنْ ثواب أو عقاب أو عدمهما ، ولوِ اتَّصفَ الفعلُ بالحسْنِ أوِ القبح لذاته أو لصفةٍ لازمةٍ .. لَمَا كَلَّفَ اللهُ الكافر بالإِيمَانِ ، والتالي باطل بالإجماع ، وبيانُ الملازمة : أنَّهُ تعالى علمَ أَنَّ الكافر لا يؤْمِنُ فتكليفه بالإيمان تكليف بمستحيل ، وهو قبيح عندهم
Between the ugly, it is ugly due to its attribute, and the good, it is good due to its essence. Their argument is that essences are all equal, and distinction is only by attributes; so if an action were ugly due to its essence, the ugliness of God's action would follow. Al-Jubba'i and his followers said: an action is good or ugly by aspect and consideration, like striking an orphan: it is good if it is for discipline, and ugly if it is for something else. The refutation of all: what has passed that actions have no effect from the servants in anything such that requesting them or forbidding them would be rationally good, and rather the reference of legal rulings is to the indication that those actions are signs of what is established upon them of reward or punishment or their absence. And if an action were characterized by goodness or ugliness due to its essence or a necessary attribute, then God would not have charged the disbeliever with faith, and the consequent is false by consensus. The explanation of the entailment: that He, exalted, knew that the disbeliever will not believe, so charging him with faith is charging with the impossible, and that is ugly according to them.
Source pages p. 537

Commentary
This passage presents the three views on the inherent moral value of actions (al-tahseen wa al-taqbeeh al-'aqliyy) and then gives the first refutation from an Ash'ari perspective. It first states the view that actions are ugly due to a necessary attribute and good due to essence. Their argument is that essences are equal, so distinction must be by attributes; if an action were ugly by essence, God's actions would also be ugly (which is impossible). Then it presents al-Jubba'i's view that actions are good or ugly by consideration (e.g., striking an orphan is good if for discipline, ugly otherwise). The refutation begins by recalling that actions have no effect from servants in terms of generating moral value; rather, legal rulings merely indicate what is assigned as reward or punishment. Then it argues: if actions were inherently good or ugly, God would not command the disbeliever to believe, because He knows the disbeliever will not believe—that would be a command of the impossible (takleef bimumtani'), which is ugly. But God does command the disbeliever to believe, so the premise that actions are inherently good/evil is false.

Key Terms
التحسين والتقبيح العقلي
حسن لذاته
قبيح لصفته
الجبائي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the first view presented, why can't ugliness be due to essence?
A) Because essences are equal, so ugliness would also apply to God's actions.
B) Because ugliness is a subjective judgment.
C) Because actions have no essence.
D) Because God's actions are always good.

Key Takeaway
The first two positions on inherent good/evil differ on whether moral value resides in the action itself or in its context.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you label an action as 'clearly wrong,' do you ever stop to consider that your judgment might be based on your own limited perspective rather than an objective truth? How does the second view (al-Jubba'i) challenge you to reconsider a recent judgment you made? Commit to pausing before condemning others.
?
The first group argues that if actions were bad by essence, then even God's actions could be bad. Have you ever assumed that God must act in ways that align with your personal sense of fairness? How does this argument force you to reexamine that assumption and submit to God's transcendence? What will you do to remind yourself of this in daily life?
?
Striking an orphan is a vivid example: it can be either good (discipline) or evil (abuse). Think of a time you were quick to judge someone's action without knowing the full context. How does this passage expose your tendency toward self-righteousness? Will you seek to understand intentions before passing moral verdicts from now on? Take a concrete step this week.

The Refutation: Impossibility of Commanding the Impossible
Provides the Ash'ari refutation of inherent good/evil via the argument of takleef bimumtani'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari argument against inherent good/evil: if actions had inherent value, then God commanding the impossible would be inherently ugly, but He does command the impossible (e.g., the disbeliever's faith).

Core Idea
If actions were inherently good or evil, God could not command the impossible—but He does.

After presenting the two Mu'tazilite views, the text now offers the Ash'ari refutation. The key move is to argue that if actions had inherent moral value, then it would be impossible for God to command the disbeliever to believe, because He knows the disbeliever will not believe—that would be commanding the impossible, which would be evil. Since God does command faith, the premise of inherent value must be false.

Guided Path
1
Read the refutation passage
Read the Arabic and translation. Note the initial reminder that actions have no effect from servants, and rulings are mere signs.
2
Understand the entailment
The text says: if actions were inherently good/ugly, then God would not command the disbeliever to believe. Why? Because the disbeliever will not believe, so it's a command of the impossible, which would be ugly.
This is the core argument.
3
See the conclusion
The consequent (God not commanding the disbeliever) is false by consensus, so the antecedent (inherent value) is false.

The Liar Example: Refuting Inherent Morality
Presents the second refutation using the liar statement and a clarifying footnote.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the statement 'I will certainly lie tomorrow' is used to argue against the view that actions are inherently good or evil, and how the Ash'ari position resolves the apparent contradiction.

Core Idea
Moral judgments can vary based on consideration, not inherent nature.

The proponents of inherent morality argued that if actions were good or evil by essence, they could never change. But consider a liar's statement about future lying: it can be both truthful and entail a lie, leading to contradictory moral evaluations. This lesson unpacks that example and shows how the Ash'ari view avoids the contradiction through 'difference of consideration.'

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation to understand the overall argument.
Pay attention to the footnote by al-Manjoorni; it explains the scope of the argument.
2
Understand the refutation logic
The passage states: if actions were good/evil by essence or necessary attribute, they would not vary. The liar's statement is then used to show that it would be both good and ugly under different conditions, which is impossible under inherent morality.
The two conditions are: if he actually lies, the statement is true (good) but entails the lie (ugly); if he doesn't lie, the statement is false (ugly) but entails no lie (good).
3
Note the footnote's clarification
Al-Manjoorni clarifies that this argument applies only to the three views that hold goodness/ugliness by essence, attribute, or a single consideration. It does not apply to the Ash'ari view that allows different considerations.
This shows the argument is not a proof against all views, especially the Ash'ari position.
4
Grasp the resolution
The apparent contradiction is resolved because the two moral valuations arise from different aspects: the truth value versus the entailment. Therefore, there is no real contradiction.
This demonstrates that moral judgments are not inherent but depend on perspective (difference of consideration).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأيضاً : لو كانَ الفعلُ حَسَناً أو قبيحاً لذاتِهِ أو لصفةٍ لازمةٍ . . لَمَا اختَلَفَ ؛ بأنْ يكونَ تارةً حَسَناً وتارةً قبيحاً ، ولاجتمعَ النقيضان في قول القائل : ( لأَكْذِبَنَّ غداً ) ، صدق أو كذبَ. قال العلامة المنجور في ( حاشيته » ( ص ۲۱۷ ) : ( هذا الإلزام والذي قبله إنما هو على الأقوال الثلاثة السابقة ، لا على الرابع ؛ لكون الفعل قد يتصف عليه بالحسن والقبح معاً ، لكن باعتبارين مختلفين ؛ كاجتماع الأبوة والبنوة في شخص بإضافتين مختلفتين ) ، وأراد بالنقيضين : المتنافيين ، وبيان اجتماعهما في قوله : ( لأكذبن غداً ( أنَّ ذلك الخبر إن طابق الواقع بأنْ كَذَبَ في الغد.. كان حسناً ؛ لصدقه بالمطابقة ، وقبيحاً ؛ لاستلزامه وقوع متعلقه الذي هو صدور الكذب عنه في الغد ، وإن لم يطابق الواقع .. كان قبيحاً ؛ لكذبه بعدم المطابقة ، وحسناً ؛ لاستلزامه انتفاء مُتعلّقه الذي هو الكذب القبيح ، ولهم أن يقولوا : إنه لا تناقض ؛ لاختلاف الاعتبار . حامدي » ( ص ٤٣١ ) .
Also: if an action were good or ugly due to its essence or a necessary attribute, it would not vary—being sometimes good and sometimes ugly—and the two opposites would not combine in the statement of one who says: 'I will certainly lie tomorrow,' whether he tells the truth or lies. Al-Allama al-Manjoorni said in his gloss: 'This entailment and the one before it apply only to the three previous views, not to the fourth; because an action can be characterized by both goodness and ugliness simultaneously, but by two different considerations, like the combination of fatherhood and sonship in one person by two different relations.' He meant by the two opposites the mutually exclusive ones. The explanation of their combination in his statement 'I will certainly lie tomorrow' is that if that report corresponds to reality, in that he lied tomorrow, it would be good because of its truth in correspondence, and ugly because it entails the occurrence of its object, which is the occurrence of the lie from him tomorrow; and if it does not correspond to reality, it would be ugly because of its falsehood in non-correspondence, and good because it entails the non-occurrence of its object, which is the ugly lie. And they could say: there is no contradiction because of the difference in consideration.
Source pages p. 537

Commentary
This passage provides the second refutation of the claim that actions are inherently good or evil. It argues that if actions had inherent moral value, they could not vary; they would always be the same. But consider the statement 'I will certainly lie tomorrow.' If the person actually lies tomorrow, the statement is true (good) but it entails the occurrence of the lie (ugly). If he does not lie, the statement is false (ugly) but it entails the non-occurrence of the lie (good). Thus the action (the statement) is both good and ugly simultaneously, which would be a contradiction if moral value were inherent. However, the footnote by al-Manjoorni clarifies that this argument applies only to the three views (by essence, by attribute, by consideration) but not to the fourth view (likely the Ash'ari position) because the fourth view allows an action to have both qualities by different considerations. He explains that the apparent contradiction is resolved by the fact that the two valuations are from different aspects—the truth value versus the entailment—so they do not truly conflict. This shows that the opponent's assumption leads to an absurdity, but the Ash'ari position avoids it by recognizing that moral judgments are not inherent but depend on perspective.

Key Terms
النقيضان
لأكذبن غداً
اختلاف الاعتبار
العلامة المنجور

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think the liar example proves that actions cannot be judged good or evil at all, or that it leads to a logical contradiction for all views.
Correction: The example only creates a contradiction if one assumes moral value is inherent (first three views). The Ash'ari view avoids this by allowing different considerations, so the action is not truly both good and ugly from the same perspective.

Checkpoint
What does the statement 'I will certainly lie tomorrow' illustrate according to this refutation?
A) That lying is always ugly.
B) That an action can be both good and ugly from different perspectives, contradicting inherent moral value.
C) That truth-telling is always good.
D) That the intellect cannot judge actions.

Key Takeaway
The liar example demonstrates that moral judgments are not inherent but depend on perspective (difference of consideration), aligning with Ash'ari theology.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you find yourself excusing a sin by focusing on some 'good' aspect of it, are you falling into the trap of confusing different considerations? How will you ensure that your moral judgments are anchored in Allah's command rather than your own shifting perspectives?
?
The liar example shows that even a lie can be 'good' in one sense (if it prevents a greater lie). Have you ever used a partial truth or omission to justify a deception? What would it mean for you to commit to complete honesty, trusting that Allah's guidance is sufficient even when it seems costly?
?
This lesson teaches that actions are not inherently good or evil – they are judged by Allah. How does that change the way you view your own past mistakes? Will you stop labeling yourself as 'good' or 'bad' based on your actions, and instead turn to Allah for His assessment and mercy?

Rational Judgments

Why the Intellect Cannot Judge Moral Obligation Before Revelation
Sanusi presents a dialectical refutation of Mu'tazilite rational moral judgment using the benefit argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari argument that even if rational good and evil are granted, the intellect cannot determine moral obligation because it cannot establish a benefit for gratitude before revelation.

Core Idea
Even if reason can know good and evil, it cannot know obligation without a benefit that only revelation can confirm.

Sanusi presents a final refutation: even if we concede the Mu'tazilite premise of rational moral judgment, the intellect still cannot reach a decisive verdict. The reason is that any moral obligation must have a benefit (fa'ida), and reason alone cannot determine that benefit. Using the example of thanking the Benefactor, he shows that the supposed benefit cannot be said to return to God or to the servant in a way that reason can confirm, thus the intellect remains unable to judge.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Notice the structure: a concession followed by a refutation through the benefit argument.
The author begins by saying the truth is already clear, but adds this argument for completeness.
2
Identify the key claim
Identify the claim that the intellect cannot decide because of conflicting aspects of consideration (ta'arudh wujuh al-nazar).
3
Trace the benefit argument
Follow the division of benefit: it returns either to God or to the servant, and if to the servant, either in this life or the next. Understand why all divisions are declared false.
The passage does not detail why each is false, but the assertion itself suffices for the argument.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Understand that this argument shows the intellect's inability to determine moral obligation without revelation, even if rational good and evil are granted.
This refutes the Mu'tazilite position without denying that gratitude is good in itself.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والبحثُ في المسألة طويلٌ ، وقد بان الحقُّ فيها ، فلا حاجة إلى التطويل . قوله : ( على أنَّهُ لو سُلَّمَ لهم ذلك جدلاً لم يجزم العقل بشيء ؛ لتعارض أوجهِ مِنَ النظرِ في ذلكَ مُتضادَّةٍ ) يعني : أَنَّهُ لا خفاءَ فسادِ مذهبِ المعتزلة على أصولِ أهلِ الحقِّ كما سبق ، وكذا أيضاً يستبينُ فساد مذهبهم في أنَّ العقل يدرك حكم الشرع في الأفعالِ وإِنْ لم يُبعث نبي على تقديرِ أنْ نُسلّم لهم جدلاً أصل التحسين والتقبيح عقلاً ؛ لتضاداً أوجه النظرِ بحيثُ يستبين بها فساد رأيهم في ذلك ، فإِنَّا لو نظرنا قبل مجيء الشرع في شكرِهِ تعالى على إنعامِهِ علينا .. لكانَ العقلُ يقتضي عندَ المعتزلة أنَّ شكرَهُ تعالى واجبٌ مِنْ غيرِ أنْ يتوقَّفَ ذلك على مجيء شرع ؛ لأنَّ معرفته تعالى ومعرفة كونه مُنعِماً يدركُهُما العقلُ بدونِ شرع ، وكذا يدرك بدونِهِ حسن شكرِ المنعمِ وقبْحَ كفرانِهِ ، فيدركُ إذا وجوبَ الشكر وتحريم الكفران بدونِ شرع . فيُقالُ لهم : هذا الشكرُ لو وجبَ قبل الشرع لكانَ لهُ فائدةٌ ؛ إذْ ما لا فائدة لهُ ليس بحسن حتى يجبَ ، لكن ثبوت الفائدة فيهِ قبل الشرع باطل ؛ لأنَّ الفائدة فيهِ إِمَّا أن ترجع إلى العبدِ الشاكر ، أو إلى الرب المشكور ، وعودها إلى العبدِ إمَّا في العاجل ، أو في الآجل . والأقسام كلُّها باطلة
And the discussion on the issue is long, and the truth has become clear in it, so there is no need for prolongation. His statement: 'Granted that it is conceded to them dialectically, the intellect does not decide anything because of the conflicting aspects of consideration in that matter which are opposed' means: that the corruption of the Mu'tazilite doctrine is not hidden according to the principles of the people of truth, as previously; and likewise the corruption of their doctrine is also evident in that the intellect perceives the ruling of the law in actions even if a prophet is not sent, on the assumption that we concede to them dialectically the principle of rational good and evil, because the aspects of consideration are contradictory such that the corruption of their view in that is made clear. For if we considered before the coming of the law regarding thanking Him, exalted, for His grace upon us, the intellect would require according to the Mu'tazilites that thanking Him is obligatory without that depending on the coming of a law, because knowledge of Him and knowledge that He is a benefactor are perceived by the intellect without law, and likewise it perceives without it the goodness of thanking the benefactor and the ugliness of ingratitude, so it perceives then the obligation of thanks and the prohibition of ingratitude without law. So it is said to them: this thanks, if it were obligatory before the law, would have some benefit, for that which has no benefit is not good such that it should be obligatory. But the establishment of benefit in it before the law is false, because the benefit in it either returns to the servant who thanks, or to the Lord who is thanked. And its return to the servant is either in the immediate or in the future. And all the divisions are false.
Source pages p. 538

Commentary
This passage concludes the discussion by asserting that the truth is already clear, but it adds a further argument even if one grants the Mu'tazilite premise of rational good and evil. The argument is that the intellect cannot make a decisive judgment because the considerations are contradictory. The specific example is thanking the Benefactor (shukr al-mun'im). The Mu'tazilites hold that reason alone determines that gratitude to God is obligatory before any revelation. However, the Ash'ari response is that such gratitude would have to have a benefit (fa'ida) to be obligatory. That benefit cannot be to God (since He is self-sufficient), nor to the servant in this world (since it may bring no worldly gain), nor in the afterlife (since reward depends on revelation). Therefore, no benefit can be established by reason alone, so the obligation cannot be rationally known. Thus, even if moral rationalism is granted dialectically, the intellect's conflicting aspects prevent a definitive judgment, undermining the Mu'tazilite claim.

Key Terms
شكر المنعم
فائدة
تعارض أوجه النظر
المعتزلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Ash'ari argument denies that gratitude is good.
Correction: The argument only denies that reason alone can prove it obligatory; it does not deny its goodness.

Checkpoint
According to the third refutation, why can the intellect not determine that thanking God is obligatory before revelation?
A) Because the intellect is incapable of knowing God.
B) Because any supposed benefit of gratitude cannot be established by reason alone.
C) Because gratitude is always harmful.
D) Because revelation prohibits gratitude.

Key Takeaway
Reason alone cannot establish a benefit for moral obligations, so revelation is necessary to determine moral duty.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever dismissed an Islamic ruling because you could not personally see a benefit in it? How does Sanusi's argument challenge that attitude?
?
When you pray or give charity, do you do so out of a sense of obligation to Allah, or because your own mind deems it good? Which motive is more spiritually secure?
?
If you were left with only your intellect to decide how to worship God, what doubts would you face? Are you willing to trust revelation even when your mind struggles to see the wisdom?

The Benefit Analysis of Gratitude
Sanusi presents the Mu'tazilite position and refutes it through a threefold benefit analysis.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi refutes the Mu'tazilite claim that reason alone can know the obligation of gratitude to God by analyzing the concept of benefit (fa'idah).

Core Idea
Gratitude must have benefit to be obligatory, but before revelation, no benefit can be rationally established.

Sanusi begins by allowing the Mu'tazilite premise that reason can judge good and evil, then shows that even on that ground, the obligation of gratitude cannot be maintained. The key is that an obligatory act must have a benefit, and reason cannot assign a valid benefit to gratitude before revelation. This lesson traces that argument through its threefold division of possible benefit recipients.

Guided Path
1
Read the Mu'tazilite Position
Read the first part of the passage where Sanusi presents the Mu'tazilite view that reason perceives the obligation of gratitude without revelation. Notice the three things reason supposedly knows: God's existence, His being a benefactor, and the goodness of gratitude.
This sets up the view to be refuted.
2
Identify the Three Divisions of Benefit
Sanusi argues that if gratitude were obligatory, it must have a benefit. He divides the possible recipient into three: immediate return to the servant, deferred return in the afterlife, or return to God. Find these three in the Arabic.
This is the core of the refutation.
3
Understand Why Each Division Fails
Read the explanations for why each division is invalid. The immediate benefit is only fatigue; the afterlife benefit is beyond reason's scope before revelation; and benefit to God is impossible because He is perfect. See how each is systematically negated.
The argument is airtight within the premises.
4
Connect to the Overall Refutation
Notice how this benefit analysis 'repels the obligation of gratitude' and contradicts the Mu'tazilite rational consideration. This shows that even if reason could judge good and evil, it cannot establish obligation because it cannot find a benefit.
This is the conclusion of this part.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
في قوله : ( على أنَّهُ لو سُلَّمَ لهم ذلك جدلاً لم يجزم العقل بشيء ; لتعارض أوجهِ مِنَ النظرِ في ذلكَ مُتضادَّةٍ ) يعني : أَنَّهُ لا خفاءَ فسادِ مذهبِ المعتزلة على أصولِ أهلِ الحقِّ كما سبق ، وكذا أيضاً يستبينُ فساد مذهبهم في أنَّ العقل يدرك حكم الشرع في الأفعالِ وإِنْ لم يُبعث نبي على تقديرِ أنْ نُسلّم لهم جدلاً أصل التحسين والتقبيح عقلاً ; لتضاداً أوجه النظرِ بحيثُ يستبين بها فساد رأيهم في ذلك ، فإِنَّا لو نظرنا قبل مجيء الشرع في شكرِهِ تعالى على إنعامِهِ علينا .. لكانَ العقلُ يقتضي عندَ المعتزلة أنَّ شكرَهُ تعالى واجبٌ مِنْ غيرِ أنْ يتوقَّفَ ذلك على مجيء شرع ; لأنَّ معرفته تعالى ومعرفة كونه مُنعِماً يدركُهُما العقلُ بدونِ شرع ، وكذا يدرك بدونِهِ حسن شكرِ المنعمِ وقبْحَ كفرانِهِ ، فيدركُ إذا وجوبَ الشكر وتحريم الكفران بدونِ شرع . فيُقالُ لهم : هذا الشكرُ لو وجبَ قبل الشرع لكانَ لهُ فائدةٌ ; إذْ ما لا فائدة لهُ ليس بحسن حتى يجبَ ، لكن ثبوت الفائدة فيهِ قبل الشرع باطل ; لأنَّ الفائدة فيهِ إِمَّا أن ترجع إلى العبدِ الشاكر ، أو إلى الرب المشكور ، وعودها إلى العبدِ إمَّا في العاجل ، أو في الآجل . والأقسام كلُّها باطلة
أمَّا بطلان عودِها إلى العبدِ عاجلاً : فلأنَّهُ إِنَّما يحصل له في العاجل التعب فقط
وأمَّا بطلان عودِها إليهِ آجلاً : فلأنَّ العقل لا مجال له قبل الشرع في شيء من أمور الآخرة إجماعاً
وأمَّا بطلان رجوعها إلى الربّ تعالى : فلتعاليه جلَّ وعلا عن أن يتجدَّدَ له كمال ، بل هو الكامل بذاتِهِ وصفاته الأزليَّة ، والغني عن الخلق وأعمالهم فهذا الوجهُ مِنَ النظرِ العقلي يدفعُ وجوبَ الشكرِ ، ويعارض الوجه الذي أوجبَهُ عندَهم ; وهو إدراك كونه تعالى مُنعِماً
فإن قالوا : لا تُسلَّمُ أنَّهُ ليسَ في الشكرِ فائدةٌ قبل الشرع ، بل فيه فائدة للعبد ; وهو الأمْنُ مِنَ العقوبة التي يُحتمل ثبوتُها على تقدير الإعراض عن الشكر
قلنا : وكذا يُحتملُ أَنْ يُعاقَبَ في فعل الشكرِ مِنْ وجهين : الأَوَّلُ : أَنَّهُ أتعبَ فيه الذات المملوكة لله تعالى ، وتصرَّفَ في ذلكَ بغيرِ إِذْنِهِ ، فصارَ كَمَنْ يشكرُ مَلِكاً أوصل إليه نعمةً ; بأنْ يُتِعِبَ عبيد الملكِ في أداء شكرِها بغير إذنِهِ ، فلا إشكالَ أَنَّهُ قد عَرَّضَ نفسَهُ بشكرِ الملكِ على هذا الوجه للعقوبة (1) .
(1) والحاصل : أنه إذا أتعب أعضاءه في الشكر كان شكره هذا بمنزلة قولك لعبيد ملك من غير إذنه : قوموا واخدموا سيدكم بسبب إنعامه عليَّ . ( حامدي ( ص ٤٣٣ ) .
In his statement: (That even if that were granted to them as a dialectical premise, the intellect would not be certain of anything, due to the conflicting aspects of consideration in that matter, contradictory) meaning: that the corruption of the Mu'tazilite school is not hidden according to the foundations of the People of Truth, as has preceded. Likewise, the corruption of their school in that the intellect perceives the ruling of the Law in actions even if no prophet is sent becomes evident, on the supposition that we grant them the principle of rational judgment of good and evil as a dialectical premise, because the aspects of consideration are contradictory, such that the corruption of their opinion in that becomes clear through them. For if we were to consider before the coming of the Law regarding gratitude to Him, Exalted is He, for His blessing upon us, then reason would require, according to the Mu'tazilites, that gratitude to Him is obligatory without that being dependent on the coming of a Law, because knowledge of Him and knowledge of His being a benefactor are perceived by reason without a Law. Likewise, without it, reason perceives the goodness of gratitude to the benefactor and the evil of ingratitude, so it perceives the obligation of gratitude and the prohibition of ingratitude without a Law. So it is said to them: This gratitude, if it were obligatory before the Law, would have benefit; for whatever has no benefit is not good such that it becomes obligatory. But the establishment of benefit in it before the Law is invalid, because the benefit in it either reverts to the grateful servant, or to the Lord who is thanked, and its return to the servant is either in the immediate [world] or in the afterlife. And all the divisions are invalid. As for the invalidity of its return to the servant immediately: because only fatigue occurs for him in the immediate. As for the invalidity of its return to him in the afterlife: because reason has no scope before the Law in anything of the matters of the afterlife by consensus. As for the invalidity of its return to the Lord, Exalted is He: because of His transcendence, may He be glorified and exalted, from having perfection renewed for Him; rather, He is perfect in His essence and eternal attributes, and independent of creation and their deeds. So this aspect of rational consideration repels the obligation of gratitude, and contradicts the aspect that obligated it according to them, which is the perception of His being a Benefactor. If they say: We do not grant that there is no benefit in gratitude before the Law; rather, there is a benefit for the servant, which is security from the punishment that is possible to be established on the supposition of turning away from gratitude. We say: Likewise, it is possible that one be punished in performing gratitude from two aspects: The first: that he has fatigued the self that is owned by God, and acted in that without His permission, so he becomes like one who thanks a king who conveyed a blessing to him by fatiguing the king's servants in performing gratitude for it without his permission. There is no doubt that he has exposed himself through thanking the king in this manner to punishment. (1) The summary: that if he fatigues his limbs in gratitude, his gratitude is like your saying to a king's servants without his permission: Stand up and serve your master due to his blessing upon me. (Hamidi, p. 433).
Source pages p. 538

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Mu'tazilite claim that reason alone can determine moral obligations before revelation. It does so by analyzing the example of gratitude to God (shukr). The author first restates the Mu'tazilite position: before any prophetic law, reason is supposed to perceive that gratitude to God for His blessings is obligatory because reason recognizes God as a benefactor and knows that gratitude to a benefactor is good and ingratitude evil. The author then raises an objection: if gratitude were obligatory before the Law, it must have some benefit (fa'idah), as an act without benefit cannot be good enough to be obligatory. He then examines the possible recipient of this benefit: either the servant (the one grateful), or the Lord (God). The benefit to the servant could be immediate (in this world) or deferred (in the afterlife). The author argues all these are invalid. First, the immediate benefit: the servant only gains fatigue (ta'ab) in this world. Second, the deferred benefit: reason has no knowledge of the afterlife before revelation (ijma'), so it cannot claim such benefit. Third, the benefit to God is impossible because God is perfect and independent; He does not acquire new perfection from our actions. Thus, the rational consideration that gratitude lacks benefit contradicts the Mu'tazilite rational consideration that it is obligatory. The author then anticipates an objection: the Mu'tazilites might say there is a benefit: security from possible punishment for ingratitude. The response is that the same uncertainty applies: one might also be punished for performing gratitude without permission, since one is using one's body (which belongs to God) without His command. The author illustrates this with an analogy: a man thanks a king by making the king's servants work without permission, exposing himself to punishment. The footnote by Hamidi summarizes this analogy: fatiguing one's limbs in gratitude is like commanding the king's servants to serve him without his leave.

Key Terms
الشكر (gratitude)
الوجوب (obligation)
الفائدة (benefit)
العقل (reason)
التحسين والتقبيح (rational judgment of good and evil)
المعتزلة (Mu'tazilites)
الشرع (Revealed Law)
التعب (fatigue)
الآخرة (afterlife)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that the Mu'tazilite argument for gratitude is logically unassailable.
Correction: Sanusi shows that even if we grant their premise of rational judgment of good and evil, the obligation fails because no rational benefit can be established for gratitude before revelation.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the reason why benefit cannot return to God?
A) God does not care about gratitude.
B) God is perfect and does not acquire new perfection.
C) Gratitude is not good enough for God.
D) God is not a benefactor.

Key Takeaway
Before revelation, reason cannot establish any benefit for gratitude, so it cannot be obligatory even on Mu'tazilite grounds.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you thank Allah in your prayers, do you ever feel like it's just fatigue? How does knowing that the true benefit of gratitude lies only in the unseen, revealed through scripture, change your intention right now? Will you this day thank Him purely because He commanded it, not because you feel a benefit?
?
Sanusi shows that reason alone finds no benefit in gratitude before revelation. But in your daily life, do you sometimes subconsciously expect a worldly return for your worship? Can you identify one act of worship you do primarily because you 'feel' it benefits you, and commit to doing it solely out of obedience, even if it brings only fatigue?
?
The passage argues that God cannot benefit from our gratitude because He is perfect. Yet, do you sometimes act as if your worship adds something to Him, or that He 'needs' your praise? Confess honestly: do you ever feel proud of your gratitude, as if you are doing God a favor? How will you uproot this hidden Mu'tazilism from your heart today?

Objection and Response: The Punishment Argument
Sanusi responds to the Mu'tazilite objection about benefit from security against punishment, using a king analogy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi responds to the Mu'tazilite objection that gratitude provides benefit by securing against punishment, using an analogy of a king.

Core Idea
If gratitude could bring punishment for acting without permission, then the benefit is uncertain and the obligation collapses.

This lesson continues the refutation by addressing a possible Mu'tazilite counterargument. They claim that gratitude does have a benefit: it prevents possible punishment for ingratitude. Sanusi responds by raising a parallel possibility: gratitude itself might incur punishment because one uses one's body (owned by God) without His command. He illustrates this with a striking analogy of a man who thanks a king by ordering the king's servants to serve him without permission. This shows that the benefit is not certain, and thus the obligation is undermined.

Guided Path
1
Read the Mu'tazilite Objection
Read the objection starting with 'If they say: We do not grant...'. They argue that gratitude has a benefit: security from possible punishment for ingratitude.
This is a plausible objection based on fear of punishment.
2
Examine Sanusi's Response
Sanusi counters by saying that the same uncertainty applies to gratitude: one might be punished for performing gratitude without permission. Note the two aspects he mentions, especially the first: fatiguing the self owned by God.
He is using the same logic of possibility against them.
3
Understand the King Analogy
Read the analogy: a man thanks a king by making the king's servants work without permission. This action invites punishment, not reward. See how this mirrors the case of gratitude to God.
The analogy is meant to show that gratitude could be seen as unauthorized use of God's property.
4
Reflect on the Implications
Consider how this response neutralizes the Mu'tazilite objection. Since both ingratitude and gratitude carry possibilities of punishment, reason cannot prefer one over the other, so no obligation can be established.
This completes the refutation of rational obligation.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
في قوله : ( على أنَّهُ لو سُلَّمَ لهم ذلك جدلاً لم يجزم العقل بشيء ; لتعارض أوجهِ مِنَ النظرِ في ذلكَ مُتضادَّةٍ ) يعني : أَنَّهُ لا خفاءَ فسادِ مذهبِ المعتزلة على أصولِ أهلِ الحقِّ كما سبق ، وكذا أيضاً يستبينُ فساد مذهبهم في أنَّ العقل يدرك حكم الشرع في الأفعالِ وإِنْ لم يُبعث نبي على تقديرِ أنْ نُسلّم لهم جدلاً أصل التحسين والتقبيح عقلاً ; لتضاداً أوجه النظرِ بحيثُ يستبين بها فساد رأيهم في ذلك ، فإِنَّا لو نظرنا قبل مجيء الشرع في شكرِهِ تعالى على إنعامِهِ علينا .. لكانَ العقلُ يقتضي عندَ المعتزلة أنَّ شكرَهُ تعالى واجبٌ مِنْ غيرِ أنْ يتوقَّفَ ذلك على مجيء شرع ; لأنَّ معرفته تعالى ومعرفة كونه مُنعِماً يدركُهُما العقلُ بدونِ شرع ، وكذا يدرك بدونِهِ حسن شكرِ المنعمِ وقبْحَ كفرانِهِ ، فيدركُ إذا وجوبَ الشكر وتحريم الكفران بدونِ شرع . فيُقالُ لهم : هذا الشكرُ لو وجبَ قبل الشرع لكانَ لهُ فائدةٌ ; إذْ ما لا فائدة لهُ ليس بحسن حتى يجبَ ، لكن ثبوت الفائدة فيهِ قبل الشرع باطل ; لأنَّ الفائدة فيهِ إِمَّا أن ترجع إلى العبدِ الشاكر ، أو إلى الرب المشكور ، وعودها إلى العبدِ إمَّا في العاجل ، أو في الآجل . والأقسام كلُّها باطلة
أمَّا بطلان عودِها إلى العبدِ عاجلاً : فلأنَّهُ إِنَّما يحصل له في العاجل التعب فقط
وأمَّا بطلان عودِها إليهِ آجلاً : فلأنَّ العقل لا مجال له قبل الشرع في شيء من أمور الآخرة إجماعاً
وأمَّا بطلان رجوعها إلى الربّ تعالى : فلتعاليه جلَّ وعلا عن أن يتجدَّدَ له كمال ، بل هو الكامل بذاتِهِ وصفاته الأزليَّة ، والغني عن الخلق وأعمالهم فهذا الوجهُ مِنَ النظرِ العقلي يدفعُ وجوبَ الشكرِ ، ويعارض الوجه الذي أوجبَهُ عندَهم ; وهو إدراك كونه تعالى مُنعِماً
فإن قالوا : لا تُسلَّمُ أنَّهُ ليسَ في الشكرِ فائدةٌ قبل الشرع ، بل فيه فائدة للعبد ; وهو الأمْنُ مِنَ العقوبة التي يُحتمل ثبوتُها على تقدير الإعراض عن الشكر
قلنا : وكذا يُحتملُ أَنْ يُعاقَبَ في فعل الشكرِ مِنْ وجهين : الأَوَّلُ : أَنَّهُ أتعبَ فيه الذات المملوكة لله تعالى ، وتصرَّفَ في ذلكَ بغيرِ إِذْنِهِ ، فصارَ كَمَنْ يشكرُ مَلِكاً أوصل إليه نعمةً ; بأنْ يُتِعِبَ عبيد الملكِ في أداء شكرِها بغير إذنِهِ ، فلا إشكالَ أَنَّهُ قد عَرَّضَ نفسَهُ بشكرِ الملكِ على هذا الوجه للعقوبة (1) .
(1) والحاصل : أنه إذا أتعب أعضاءه في الشكر كان شكره هذا بمنزلة قولك لعبيد ملك من غير إذنه : قوموا واخدموا سيدكم بسبب إنعامه عليَّ . ( حامدي ( ص ٤٣٣ ) .
In his statement: (That even if that were granted to them as a dialectical premise, the intellect would not be certain of anything, due to the conflicting aspects of consideration in that matter, contradictory) meaning: that the corruption of the Mu'tazilite school is not hidden according to the foundations of the People of Truth, as has preceded. Likewise, the corruption of their school in that the intellect perceives the ruling of the Law in actions even if no prophet is sent becomes evident, on the supposition that we grant them the principle of rational judgment of good and evil as a dialectical premise, because the aspects of consideration are contradictory, such that the corruption of their opinion in that becomes clear through them. For if we were to consider before the coming of the Law regarding gratitude to Him, Exalted is He, for His blessing upon us, then reason would require, according to the Mu'tazilites, that gratitude to Him is obligatory without that being dependent on the coming of a Law, because knowledge of Him and knowledge of His being a benefactor are perceived by reason without a Law. Likewise, without it, reason perceives the goodness of gratitude to the benefactor and the evil of ingratitude, so it perceives the obligation of gratitude and the prohibition of ingratitude without a Law. So it is said to them: This gratitude, if it were obligatory before the Law, would have benefit; for whatever has no benefit is not good such that it becomes obligatory. But the establishment of benefit in it before the Law is invalid, because the benefit in it either reverts to the grateful servant, or to the Lord who is thanked, and its return to the servant is either in the immediate [world] or in the afterlife. And all the divisions are invalid. As for the invalidity of its return to the servant immediately: because only fatigue occurs for him in the immediate. As for the invalidity of its return to him in the afterlife: because reason has no scope before the Law in anything of the matters of the afterlife by consensus. As for the invalidity of its return to the Lord, Exalted is He: because of His transcendence, may He be glorified and exalted, from having perfection renewed for Him; rather, He is perfect in His essence and eternal attributes, and independent of creation and their deeds. So this aspect of rational consideration repels the obligation of gratitude, and contradicts the aspect that obligated it according to them, which is the perception of His being a Benefactor. If they say: We do not grant that there is no benefit in gratitude before the Law; rather, there is a benefit for the servant, which is security from the punishment that is possible to be established on the supposition of turning away from gratitude. We say: Likewise, it is possible that one be punished in performing gratitude from two aspects: The first: that he has fatigued the self that is owned by God, and acted in that without His permission, so he becomes like one who thanks a king who conveyed a blessing to him by fatiguing the king's servants in performing gratitude for it without his permission. There is no doubt that he has exposed himself through thanking the king in this manner to punishment. (1) The summary: that if he fatigues his limbs in gratitude, his gratitude is like your saying to a king's servants without his permission: Stand up and serve your master due to his blessing upon me. (Hamidi, p. 433).
Source pages p. 538

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Mu'tazilite claim that reason alone can determine moral obligations before revelation. It does so by analyzing the example of gratitude to God (shukr). The author first restates the Mu'tazilite position: before any prophetic law, reason is supposed to perceive that gratitude to God for His blessings is obligatory because reason recognizes God as a benefactor and knows that gratitude to a benefactor is good and ingratitude evil. The author then raises an objection: if gratitude were obligatory before the Law, it must have some benefit (fa'idah), as an act without benefit cannot be good enough to be obligatory. He then examines the possible recipient of this benefit: either the servant (the one grateful), or the Lord (God). The benefit to the servant could be immediate (in this world) or deferred (in the afterlife). The author argues all these are invalid. First, the immediate benefit: the servant only gains fatigue (ta'ab) in this world. Second, the deferred benefit: reason has no knowledge of the afterlife before revelation (ijma'), so it cannot claim such benefit. Third, the benefit to God is impossible because God is perfect and independent; He does not acquire new perfection from our actions. Thus, the rational consideration that gratitude lacks benefit contradicts the Mu'tazilite rational consideration that it is obligatory. The author then anticipates an objection: the Mu'tazilites might say there is a benefit: security from possible punishment for ingratitude. The response is that the same uncertainty applies: one might also be punished for performing gratitude without permission, since one is using one's body (which belongs to God) without His command. The author illustrates this with an analogy: a man thanks a king by making the king's servants work without permission, exposing himself to punishment. The footnote by Hamidi summarizes this analogy: fatiguing one's limbs in gratitude is like commanding the king's servants to serve him without his leave.

Key Terms
الشكر (gratitude)
الفائدة (benefit)
العقوبة (punishment)
الشرع (Revealed Law)
الملك (king)
العبيد (servants)
الإذن (permission)
التعب (fatigue)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that the Mu'tazilite objection about punishment is a strong defense of rational obligation.
Correction: Sanusi shows that the same logic of possibility can be used against gratitude, so the benefit is not certain and the obligation fails.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why might one be punished for performing gratitude?
A) Gratitude is always bad.
B) One uses their body (owned by God) without permission.
C) God does not like to be thanked.
D) Gratitude causes fatigue.

Key Takeaway
Even the claimed benefit of avoiding punishment is counterbalanced by the possibility of punishment for gratitude itself, so rational obligation remains unproven.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you perform acts of worship, do you ever assume they automatically earn you reward? How does this passage challenge that assumption by showing that even gratitude can be punishable without God's command? Are you more focused on your own reasoning or on seeking His explicit guidance?
?
The analogy speaks of using the king's servants without permission. In your life, what 'servants' (your time, health, wealth) have you directed toward God without first asking if He wants them that way? What would it look like to truly submit every action to His permission?
?
This passage reveals that we cannot compute salvation through our own logic—even our good deeds can be misdirected. Does this unsettle you? If so, why? What does your discomfort reveal about where you place your trust—in your own estimation of virtue or in God's revealed command?

Refutation of Gratitude's Return to Slave and God
Sanusi enumerates three aspects invalidating rational obligation of gratitude.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Sanusi's threefold argument against the rational obligation of gratitude: it cannot benefit the slave in this life or the afterlife, nor can it add perfection to God.

Core Idea
Gratitude cannot benefit the slave or God, so reason cannot obligate it.

Sanusi begins by examining the supposed benefits of gratitude. He shows that gratitude cannot return any benefit to the slave in this life or the afterlife, nor can it add perfection to God. This dismantles the rationalist claim that gratitude is obligatory.

Guided Path
1
Read the threefold argument
Read the passage carefully, noting the three distinct aspects Sanusi presents: immediate return to the slave, afterlife return, and return to God.
The argument is structured as three separate refutations.
2
Understand each aspect
For each aspect, identify why gratitude fails to provide a benefit. First, only fatigue in this life; second, reason has no scope before revelation; third, God is perfect and self-sufficient.
3
Connect to the broader debate
Consider how this argument opposes the rationalist view that gratitude is obligatory simply because God is a benefactor.
This sets up the need for revelation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أمَّا بطلان عودِها إلى العبدِ عاجلاً : فلأنَّهُ إِنَّما يحصل له في العاجل التعب فقط وأمَّا بطلان عودِها إليهِ آجلاً : فلأنَّ العقل لا مجال له قبل الشرع في شيء من أمور الآخرة إجماعاً أن وأمَّا بطلان رجوعها إلى الربّ تعالى : فلتعاليه جلَّ وعلا عن يتجدَّدَ له كمال ، بل هو الكامل بذاتِهِ وصفاته الأزليَّة ، والغني عن الخلق وأعمالهم فهذا الوجهُ مِنَ النظرِ العقلي يدفعُ وجوبَ الشكرِ ، ويعارض الوجه الذي أوجبَهُ عندَهم ؛ وهو إدراك كونه تعالى مُنعِماً فإن قالوا : لا تُسلَّمُ أنَّهُ ليسَ في الشكرِ فائدةٌ قبل الشرع ، بل فيه فائدة للعبد ؛ وهو الأمْنُ مِنَ العقوبة التي يُحتمل ثبوتُها على تقدير الإعراض عن الشكر قلنا : وكذا يُحتملُ أَنْ يُعاقَبَ في فعل الشكرِ مِنْ وجهين : الأَوَّلُ : أَنَّهُ أتعبَ فيه الذات المملوكة لله تعالى ، وتصرَّفَ في ذلكَ بغيرِ إِذْنِهِ ، فصارَ كَمَنْ يشكرُ مَلِكاً أوصل إليه نعمةً ؛ بأنْ يُتِعِبَ عبيد الملكِ في أداء شكرِها بغير إذنِهِ ، فلا إشكالَ أَنَّهُ قد عَرَّضَ نفسَهُ بشكرِ الملكِ على هذا الوجه للعقوبة الثاني : أنَّ مَنْ أعطاهُ ملكٌ جوادٌ في غاية الجودِ كِسْرةً صغيرةً مِنْ خبز الشعير مثلاً ، ولهُ مِنْ خزائن أنواع الأطعمة وأجناس الأموال ما لا نهاية له ، ولا ينقصُ بما يعطي منهُ ، ثم صارَ ذلك الفقيرُ المحتاجُ يذكرُ الملك ويُثني عليهِ في المحافلِ على إعطاء تلك الكسرةِ مِنَ الشعير . . لاستحق العقوبة ؛ لاستهزائِهِ بالملكِ واستصغارِهِ قدرَهُ حينَ يمدحه بما لا بالَ له عندَهُ ، ولا شك أنَّ نِعَمَ الدنيا والآخرة كلَّها بالنسبة إلى عظيمِ قدرةِ اللهِ تعالى وسَعَةِ مُلكه وجلاله . . كلا شيء فقد بان لك بهذا : أنَّ دخول العقلِ إلى طلب أحكام الله تعالى في الأفعال بميزان التحسين والتقبيح.. دخول بميزان مختل ، ينقلبُ به صاحبه خاسئاً وهو حسيرٌ ، فالحَقُّ : وقف ذلك على الشرع ، والدَّجَا في معرفتِهِ إلى السمع ، فوجب البحث عن النبوَّةِ ، وتحقيق شروط الرسالة ، وهو الفصل الذي نشرع فيهِ الآنَ
As for the invalidity of its return to the slave immediately: because he only obtains in the immediate term mere fatigue. And as for the invalidity of its return to him in the hereafter: because reason has no scope before revelation in any matter of the afterlife by consensus. And as for the invalidity of its return to the Lord, Exalted: because of His being exalted and sublime above that a perfection should be renewed for Him; rather, He is perfect in His essence and eternal attributes, and independent of creation and their actions. So this aspect of rational consideration repels the obligation of gratitude and opposes the aspect that made it obligatory according to them—which is the perception that He, Exalted, is a bestower of favors. If they say: We do not grant that there is no benefit in gratitude before revelation; rather, there is a benefit for the slave—namely, security from the punishment that might possibly be established on the occasion of turning away from gratitude. We say: Similarly, it is possible that one may be punished in performing gratitude from two aspects. The first: that he has fatigued therein the self that is owned by God, Exalted, and acted therein without His permission. So he becomes like someone who thanks a king who has conveyed a favor to him by fatiguing the king's slaves in performing its thanks without his permission. There is no doubt that he has exposed himself by thanking the king in this manner to punishment. The second: that one whom a generous king of extreme generosity gives a small morsel, for example of barley bread, while he has from the treasuries of types of foods and kinds of wealth that which has no end, and it does not diminish by what he gives from it; then that needy poor person starts mentioning the king and praising him in gatherings for giving that barley morsel—he deserves punishment, because of his mockery of the king and belittling of his status when he praises him for something insignificant in his sight. And there is no doubt that all the favors of this world and the next, in comparison to the greatness of the power of God, Exalted, and the vastness of His kingdom and His majesty, are as nothing. So it has become clear to you by this: that the entry of reason into seeking God's rulings about actions with the scale of assigning goodness and ugliness (tahsin wa taqbih) is an entry with a faulty scale, by which its possessor returns disgraced and regretful. So the truth is: that [judgment] is suspended on revelation, and the darkness in knowing it is [dispelled] by hearing. So the search for prophethood (nubuwwa) and the verification of the conditions of the message become obligatory, and that is the chapter we will now begin.
Source pages p. 539

Commentary
This passage is a sustained argument refuting the rational obligation of gratitude (shukr) to God, thereby demonstrating the insufficiency of rational judgment (tahsin wa taqbih) and establishing the necessity of prophethood (nubuwwa). The author proceeds in a structured argumentative order. First, he states the invalidity of gratitude returning to the slave immediately (only fatigue), in the hereafter (reason has no jurisdiction before revelation), and to God (who cannot acquire new perfection and is self-sufficient). Thus, rational consideration opposes the obligation derived from perceiving God as a benefactor. Then he addresses an objection: the objector claims there is a benefit—security from possible punishment. The author answers by showing that punishment is equally possible on two grounds: first, one fatigues limbs that belong to God without permission, analogous to thanking a king by exhausting his slaves; second, praising God for a trivial favor given His infinite generosity is like praising a king for a barley morsel when he owns endless treasures—an insult deserving punishment. The conclusion is that rational judgment with the scale of goodness and ugliness is a faulty scale; the truth is to rely on revelation (shar'), which makes it necessary to investigate prophethood and its conditions—the subject of the next chapter.

Key Terms
الشكر
العقل
الشرع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Gratitude to God is rationally obligatory because it benefits the slave or pleases God.
Correction: Sanusi shows gratitude cannot benefit the slave in this world or the hereafter, nor does it add perfection to God, so reason cannot obligate it.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi, why does gratitude not benefit the slave in the afterlife?
A) Because the slave will be punished anyway.
B) Because reason has no scope before revelation in matters of the afterlife.
C) Because the slave does not believe in the afterlife.
D) Because God does not reward gratitude.

Key Takeaway
The threefold argument shows that rational obligation of gratitude is invalid because it benefits neither the slave nor God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You perform many acts of gratitude—prayer, charity, dhikr—but if reason alone cannot obligate them, why do you still do them? Is it out of habit, fear, or genuine response to revelation? How does that change the sincerity of your worship?
?
Sanusi says that in this life gratitude brings only fatigue. Have you ever felt your religious duties are burdensome? When you feel that way, do you turn to revelation for motivation or rely on your own reasoning? What does your response reveal about your heart?
?
God is perfect without your gratitude. Does that truth make you feel insignificant or liberate you from the illusion that God needs your worship? If you truly believed that your gratitude adds nothing to God, would you still worship Him with the same zeal? If not, what is the real object of your worship?
