Response to Objection: Punishment Possible in Gratitude
Sanusi presents and refutes an objection using two analogies.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi refutes the objection that gratitude provides security from punishment, using two analogies to show that punishment is equally possible.

Core Idea
Gratitude can lead to punishment if done without permission or if it belittles God's greatness.

The rationalist objector claims gratitude benefits the slave by securing him from possible punishment. Sanusi responds by showing that punishment is equally possible on two grounds: first, fatigue of the body without God's permission, and second, praising God for trivial favors compared to His infinite generosity. These analogies undermine the objector's confidence in rational judgment.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
Read the objector's claim: gratitude benefits the slave by providing security from punishment.
2
Examine the first analogy
Understand the analogy of thanking a king by fatiguing his slaves without permission.
This shows that acting without permission can bring punishment.
3
Examine the second analogy
Consider the analogy of praising a king for a barley morsel when he has infinite treasures.
This shows that praising for something insignificant can be an insult.
4
Draw the conclusion
Realize that both analogies demonstrate that gratitude can lead to punishment, not just reward.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أمَّا بطلان عودِها إلى العبدِ عاجلاً : فلأنَّهُ إِنَّما يحصل له في العاجل التعب فقط وأمَّا بطلان عودِها إليهِ آجلاً : فلأنَّ العقل لا مجال له قبل الشرع في شيء من أمور الآخرة إجماعاً أن وأمَّا بطلان رجوعها إلى الربّ تعالى : فلتعاليه جلَّ وعلا عن يتجدَّدَ له كمال ، بل هو الكامل بذاتِهِ وصفاته الأزليَّة ، والغني عن الخلق وأعمالهم فهذا الوجهُ مِنَ النظرِ العقلي يدفعُ وجوبَ الشكرِ ، ويعارض الوجه الذي أوجبَهُ عندَهم ؛ وهو إدراك كونه تعالى مُنعِماً فإن قالوا : لا تُسلَّمُ أنَّهُ ليسَ في الشكرِ فائدةٌ قبل الشرع ، بل فيه فائدة للعبد ؛ وهو الأمْنُ مِنَ العقوبة التي يُحتمل ثبوتُها على تقدير الإعراض عن الشكر قلنا : وكذا يُحتملُ أَنْ يُعاقَبَ في فعل الشكرِ مِنْ وجهين : الأَوَّلُ : أَنَّهُ أتعبَ فيه الذات المملوكة لله تعالى ، وتصرَّفَ في ذلكَ بغيرِ إِذْنِهِ ، فصارَ كَمَنْ يشكرُ مَلِكاً أوصل إليه نعمةً ؛ بأنْ يُتِعِبَ عبيد الملكِ في أداء شكرِها بغير إذنِهِ ، فلا إشكالَ أَنَّهُ قد عَرَّضَ نفسَهُ بشكرِ الملكِ على هذا الوجه للعقوبة الثاني : أنَّ مَنْ أعطاهُ ملكٌ جوادٌ في غاية الجودِ كِسْرةً صغيرةً مِنْ خبز الشعير مثلاً ، ولهُ مِنْ خزائن أنواع الأطعمة وأجناس الأموال ما لا نهاية له ، ولا ينقصُ بما يعطي منهُ ، ثم صارَ ذلك الفقيرُ المحتاجُ يذكرُ الملك ويُثني عليهِ في المحافلِ على إعطاء تلك الكسرةِ مِنَ الشعير . . لاستحق العقوبة ؛ لاستهزائِهِ بالملكِ واستصغارِهِ قدرَهُ حينَ يمدحه بما لا بالَ له عندَهُ ، ولا شك أنَّ نِعَمَ الدنيا والآخرة كلَّها بالنسبة إلى عظيمِ قدرةِ اللهِ تعالى وسَعَةِ مُلكه وجلاله . . كلا شيء فقد بان لك بهذا : أنَّ دخول العقلِ إلى طلب أحكام الله تعالى في الأفعال بميزان التحسين والتقبيح.. دخول بميزان مختل ، ينقلبُ به صاحبه خاسئاً وهو حسيرٌ ، فالحَقُّ : وقف ذلك على الشرع ، والدَّجَا في معرفتِهِ إلى السمع ، فوجب البحث عن النبوَّةِ ، وتحقيق شروط الرسالة ، وهو الفصل الذي نشرع فيهِ الآنَ
As for the invalidity of its return to the slave immediately: because he only obtains in the immediate term mere fatigue. And as for the invalidity of its return to him in the hereafter: because reason has no scope before revelation in any matter of the afterlife by consensus. And as for the invalidity of its return to the Lord, Exalted: because of His being exalted and sublime above that a perfection should be renewed for Him; rather, He is perfect in His essence and eternal attributes, and independent of creation and their actions. So this aspect of rational consideration repels the obligation of gratitude and opposes the aspect that made it obligatory according to them—which is the perception that He, Exalted, is a bestower of favors. If they say: We do not grant that there is no benefit in gratitude before revelation; rather, there is a benefit for the slave—namely, security from the punishment that might possibly be established on the occasion of turning away from gratitude. We say: Similarly, it is possible that one may be punished in performing gratitude from two aspects. The first: that he has fatigued therein the self that is owned by God, Exalted, and acted therein without His permission. So he becomes like someone who thanks a king who has conveyed a favor to him by fatiguing the king's slaves in performing its thanks without his permission. There is no doubt that he has exposed himself by thanking the king in this manner to punishment. The second: that one whom a generous king of extreme generosity gives a small morsel, for example of barley bread, while he has from the treasuries of types of foods and kinds of wealth that which has no end, and it does not diminish by what he gives from it; then that needy poor person starts mentioning the king and praising him in gatherings for giving that barley morsel—he deserves punishment, because of his mockery of the king and belittling of his status when he praises him for something insignificant in his sight. And there is no doubt that all the favors of this world and the next, in comparison to the greatness of the power of God, Exalted, and the vastness of His kingdom and His majesty, are as nothing. So it has become clear to you by this: that the entry of reason into seeking God's rulings about actions with the scale of assigning goodness and ugliness (tahsin wa taqbih) is an entry with a faulty scale, by which its possessor returns disgraced and regretful. So the truth is: that [judgment] is suspended on revelation, and the darkness in knowing it is [dispelled] by hearing. So the search for prophethood (nubuwwa) and the verification of the conditions of the message become obligatory, and that is the chapter we will now begin.
Source pages p. 539

Commentary
This passage is a sustained argument refuting the rational obligation of gratitude (shukr) to God, thereby demonstrating the insufficiency of rational judgment (tahsin wa taqbih) and establishing the necessity of prophethood (nubuwwa). The author proceeds in a structured argumentative order. First, he states the invalidity of gratitude returning to the slave immediately (only fatigue), in the hereafter (reason has no jurisdiction before revelation), and to God (who cannot acquire new perfection and is self-sufficient). Thus, rational consideration opposes the obligation derived from perceiving God as a benefactor. Then he addresses an objection: the objector claims there is a benefit—security from possible punishment. The author answers by showing that punishment is equally possible on two grounds: first, one fatigues limbs that belong to God without permission, analogous to thanking a king by exhausting his slaves; second, praising God for a trivial favor given His infinite generosity is like praising a king for a barley morsel when he owns endless treasures—an insult deserving punishment. The conclusion is that rational judgment with the scale of goodness and ugliness is a faulty scale; the truth is to rely on revelation (shar'), which makes it necessary to investigate prophethood and its conditions—the subject of the next chapter.

Key Terms
الشكر
الشرع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Gratitude always secures one from punishment.
Correction: Sanusi shows that gratitude can itself lead to punishment if done without permission or if it belittles God's greatness.

Checkpoint
What is the purpose of the first analogy (thanking a king by fatiguing his slaves)?
A) To show that gratitude is always beneficial.
B) To show that acting without permission can lead to punishment.
C) To prove that kings are generous.
D) To argue that slaves should not be thanked.

Key Takeaway
Gratitude can lead to punishment if done without permission or if it belittles God's infinite generosity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you thank God for a blessing, do you ever stop to consider whether your gratitude truly honors His infinite greatness, or have you ever praised Him in a way that, if you were honest, reduces His majesty? What would it look like to thank Him in a manner befitting His vastness?
?
Sanusi warns that fatiguing your body in gratitude without divine permission can lead to punishment. Are there any acts of worship or expressions of thankfulness in your life that you have not verified through the Shari'ah? How can you ensure your gratitude is performed with divine authorization?
?
The second analogy compares praising God for worldly favors to praising a king for a barley morsel. Are there areas in your life where you focus excessively on small blessings while neglecting the immense, unending gifts of God? How can you shift your perspective to recognize His infinite generosity and respond with deeper gratitude?

Conclusion: Faulty Scale of Reason and Necessity of Prophethood
Sanusi concludes the argument and transitions to the necessity of prophethood.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Sanusi's conclusion that rational judgment (tahsin wa taqbih) is a faulty scale and that the search for prophethood is necessary.

Core Idea
Reason's scale for judging actions is faulty; truth depends on revelation, making prophethood necessary.

Sanusi concludes his argument by declaring that using reason to assess God's rulings is like using a defective scale. The correct approach is to rely on revelation. This realization makes it obligatory to investigate prophethood and its conditions, which is the subject of the next chapter.

Guided Path
1
Read the conclusion
Read the passage where Sanusi declares the scale of reason faulty and truth suspended on revelation.
2
Understand the imagery
Note the simile of a faulty scale and the phrase 'disgraced and regretful'.
This emphasizes the failure of rationalism.
3
Connect to prophethood
See how this conclusion naturally leads to the necessity of investigating prophethood.
This is the transition to the next chapter.

The Parable of the King and the Necessity of Revelation
Sanusi uses a parable to prove reason alone cannot determine moral duty.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi's parable demonstrates that reason alone is insufficient for determining moral obligation, and why revelation is necessary.

Core Idea
Reason without revelation is like praising a gift while ignoring the Giver's infinite majesty.

Sanusi presents a vivid parable to show the limitations of human intellect in judging divine commands. Before we can understand the role of reason in theology, we must see why it cannot serve as the ultimate judge of good and evil. This passage prepares the ground for the study of prophethood.

Guided Path
1
Read the parable carefully
Read the story of the king and the poor person. Notice the king's immense generosity and the smallness of the gift.
2
Identify the analogy
The king represents Allah, and the gifts represent all blessings. The poor person's praise for the small gift mirrors our tendency to rely on reason alone.
3
Recognize the moral
Praising the king for a trivial gift while ignoring his vast treasures is mockery. Analogously, using reason alone to judge divine commands is flawed.
This is the key turning point.
4
Apply the lesson to theology
Reason's entry into moral judgment via taḥsīn and taqbīḥ is a faulty scale. The correct scale is revelation (samʿ).
5
Understand the implication
Since moral obligation depends on revelation, we must investigate prophethood and the conditions of the messenger.
This leads to the next chapter.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني : أنَّ مَنْ أعطاهُ ملكٌ جوادٌ في غاية الجودِ كِسْرةً صغيرةً مِنْ خبز الشعير مثلاً ، ولهُ مِنْ خزائن أنواع الأطعمة وأجناس الأموال ما لا نهاية له ، ولا ينقصُ بما يعطي منهُ ، ثم صارَ ذلك الفقيرُ المحتاجُ يذكرُ الملك ويُثني عليهِ في المحافلِ على إعطاء تلك الكسرةِ مِنَ الشعير . . لاستحق العقوبة ؛ لاستهزائِهِ بالملكِ واستصغارِهِ قدرَهُ حينَ يمدحه بما لا بالَ له عندَهُ ، ولا شك أنَّ نِعَمَ الدنيا والآخرة كلَّها بالنسبة إلى عظيمِ قدرةِ اللهِ تعالى وسَعَةِ مُلكه وجلاله . . كلا شيء فقد بان لك بهذا : أنَّ دخول العقلِ إلى طلب أحكام الله تعالى في الأفعال بميزان التحسين والتقبيح.. دخول بميزان مختل ، ينقلبُ به صاحبه خاسئاً وهو حسيرٌ ، فالحَقُّ : وقف ذلك على الشرع ، والدَّجَا في معرفتِهِ إلى السمع ، فوجب البحث عن النبوَّةِ ، وتحقيق شروط الرسالة ، وهو الفصل الذي نشرع فيهِ الآنَ
The second: that a generous king of utmost generosity gave a person a small morsel of barley bread, for example, while he has from the treasuries of all kinds of foods and types of wealth that which has no end, and it does not diminish by what he gives from it. Then that needy poor person took to mentioning the king and praising him in assemblies for giving that morsel of barley... he deserves punishment; because of his mockery of the king and belittling his status when he praises him for that which is insignificant to him. And there is no doubt that all the blessings of this world and the Hereafter, in relation to the greatness of Allah's power, the vastness of His dominion and His majesty, are nothing. So it has become clear to you by this: that the intellect's entering into seeking Allah's rulings regarding actions by the scale of tafsīr and taqbīḥ (rational judgments of good and bad) is an entry with a faulty scale, by which its possessor turns back despised and regretful. So the truth is: that [knowledge of obligation] is restricted to the Sharia, and in knowing it we must rely on revelation. Therefore, it is necessary to investigate prophethood and verify the conditions of the message, and this is the chapter we are now beginning.
Source pages p. 540

Commentary
This passage is an argument by analogy (tamthīl) demonstrating the inadequacy of reason alone for determining moral obligation. Sanusi presents the example of a generous king who gives a poor person a tiny piece of barley bread, while possessing infinite treasures. If the poor man then publicly praises the king only for that small gift, he would be mocking the king by treating a trivial gift as if it were great. The moral is clear: all worldly and otherworldly blessings from Allah are as nothing compared to His majesty. Therefore, when a person tries to use his intellect to judge which actions are good or bad (taḥsīn and taqbīḥ) in order to derive divine commands, he is using a faulty scale. He cannot properly assess the weight of Allah's favors. The correct position is that knowledge of moral obligation depends exclusively on revelation (samʿ). Consequently, one must investigate prophethood and the conditions of the messenger, which is the topic of the next chapter.

Key Terms
التحسين والتقبيح
الشرع
السمع
العقل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The parable teaches the importance of gratitude for small blessings.
Correction: The parable actually teaches that reason alone cannot properly evaluate divine commands; gratitude is not the main point.

Checkpoint
What does the parable of the king and the poor person illustrate?
A) The importance of gratitude for small blessings.
B) That reason cannot correctly determine moral obligation without revelation.
C) That the king's generosity is infinite.
D) That poverty is a punishment.

Key Takeaway
Reason alone cannot determine moral obligation; only revelation can.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Are there areas in your life where you have trusted your own reasoning over the clear commands of revelation? What is one specific decision you are currently rationalizing that you know contradicts the Sharia?
?
When you praise Allah for His blessings, do you truly appreciate His infinite majesty, or do you treat His gifts as if they are the main thing? How can you shift your focus from the gift to the Giver today?
?
This parable shows that praising Allah for small things while ignoring His greatness is akin to mockery. Where in your worship have you reduced Allah to something manageable? What would it mean for you to live with the constant awareness of His absolute greatness, and what is one practical change you will make this week?

Two Refutations on Delaying Miracles
Sanusi refutes a claim using two arguments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand two arguments Sanusi uses to refute the claim that delaying miracles is impermissible, and why the second is a concessive argument that does not accept the opponent's principle.

Core Idea
Miracles may be delayed for wise reasons known to Allah.

Sanusi addresses a common objection: that a prophet must perform a miracle immediately upon claiming prophethood. He dismantles this objection using two distinct arguments, the first striking at its foundation, the second granting the premise but showing it fails. This passage is a model of theological refutation: one argument destroys the opponent's basis, the other defeats them on their own terms.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the two refutations
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice that Sanusi presents two clear 'ways' (وجهين) of refuting the claim. Mark the first and second arguments.
The first argument is against the principle of husn-qubh; the second is a concessive argument.
2
Understand the first refutation
The first refutation invalidates the very foundation of the opponent's claim: the principle that actions are inherently good or bad (al-taḥsīn wa al-taqbīḥ) and that God must consider benefit. Sanusi says this has already been refuted earlier.
This is a direct attack on the Mu'tazilite framework.
3
Understand the second refutation
The second refutation assumes (for the sake of argument) that the principle is valid, but argues that delaying miracles may still be beneficial because of envy and rivalry among people. Allah knows that some people will only accept after the prophet's death.
This is a concessive argument: it does not accept the principle, but shows it does not lead to the opponent's conclusion.
4
Reflect on the concession
Notice that the second argument does not endorse the principle of husn-qubh. It simply shows that even if one accepts it, the opponent's claim that miracles must be immediate does not follow. This is a powerful rhetorical strategy.
Many students mistakenly think Sanusi accepts the principle in the second argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإبطال قولهم بوجهين :
أحدهما : مِنْ جهةِ إبطالِ أصل التحسين والتقبيح ، ومراعاة الصلاحِ والأصلح ، وقد سبق تحقيق ذلك (۱)
الثاني : على تقديرِ تسليم هذا الأصل الفاسد لهم قد يُقالُ : يمتنع أن يكونَ صلاحُ بعض الخلْقِ في ذلكَ ؛ إِذْ قد يعلمُ اللَّهُ مِنْ طائفة حسدَ الأحياء ومنافستهم ، واستحكام هذا الخُلُقِ في قلوبهم ، ويزول عنهم هذا الخُلُقُ بموتِ محسودِهم ، ويتلقون حينئذٍ ما يكونُ منه بالقبولِ ، وأكثرُ الكَفَرةِ والفَجَرةِ إنَّما أُتُوا ؛ مِنْ حسد وحبِّ رئاسة وأنفةٍ مِنَ التبعيَّةِ ، فلا يمتنع في المعلوم - على تسليم أصل التحسينِ والتقبيحِ ـ أنْ يكونَ صلاح قومٍ في تأخير المعجزة .
And refuting their statement in two ways: First: from the aspect of invalidating the principle of declaring good and evil (al-taḥsīn wa al-taqbīḥ), and consideration of benefit and what is more beneficial, and that has already been verified earlier (1). Second: on the assumption of conceding this corrupt principle to them, it may be said: it is impossible that the benefit of some of creation lies in that; for Allah knows from a group [the trait of] envy of the living and rivalry, and the firmness of this disposition in their hearts, and this disposition leaves them upon the death of the one they envy, and they then receive what comes from him with acceptance. And most of the disbelievers and immoral people come [to disbelief] only from envy, love of leadership, and pride of being followers. So it is not impossible in the divine knowledge – on the assumption of conceding the principle of declaring good and evil – that the benefit of some people lies in delaying the miracle.
Source pages p. 559

Commentary
This passage presents two refutations of an opposing claim (likely that it is obligatory to perform a miracle immediately upon claiming prophethood, and delaying it is impossible). The author first refutes by invalidating the very foundation of the argument, namely the Mu'tazilite principle of objective good and evil (al-taḥsīn wa al-taqbīḥ) and the obligation to consider what is most beneficial (al-aṣlaḥ). He refers to a previous discussion where this was already refuted. Then, even if one concedes that principle (second way), the author argues that it is not necessary that benefit lies in immediate miracles; indeed, divine knowledge may deem delaying better because envy and rivalry would prevent acceptance during the prophet's life, but after his death those obstacles may vanish. Thus, postponement may be beneficial for some people, so the claim of impossibility collapses.

Key Terms
التحسين والتقبيح
الصلاح والأصلح
حسد
المعجزة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that Sanusi accepts the Mu'tazilite principle of husn-qubh in the second argument.
Correction: The second argument is explicitly concessive: 'on the assumption of conceding this corrupt principle to them'. It does not affirm the principle but shows that even on that premise the opponent's conclusion does not follow.

Checkpoint
What is the first way the author refutes the claim that delaying miracles is impossible?
A) By affirming the principle of good and evil
B) By invalidating the very principle of rational good and evil (al-taḥsīn wa al-taqbīḥ)
C) By citing the Qur'an
D) By arguing that envy prevents acceptance

Key Takeaway
Sanusi refutes the claim that miracles must be immediate by both attacking its foundational principle and showing that even on that principle the claim fails.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you find within yourself any envy or rivalry towards those whom Allah has favored with knowledge or leadership? How does the fact that such envy can blind people from accepting the truth affect you personally? What will you do this week to purify your heart from this?
?
If Allah delays an answer to your prayer or a sign you seek, can you accept that it may be for your ultimate benefit, just as a miracle may be delayed for a people's benefit? How does your patience reveal the state of your heart?
?
The lesson shows that even a flawed principle (if conceded) does not force the conclusion you want. Are you willing to examine your own beliefs and see if you hold onto them only because of pride or fear of being a follower? What would it take for you to submit to the truth even if it costs you your comfort?

Three Views on the Indication of Miracles
Sanusi enumerates and explains three views on miracle indication.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three scholarly views on how miracles indicate the truthfulness of a prophet, and note how two of them reduce to a rational basis.

Core Idea
Miracles indicate truth either by rational necessity or by custom.

How do we know that a miracle proves a prophet's truth? Sanusi presents three views from the scholars. He notes that two views reduce to a single rational foundation, while the third relies on customary indication. This lesson lays the groundwork for understanding the proof that miracles cannot come from liars.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and its translation to get the flow of the argument. Notice the classification of views.
The passage uses technical terms like 'عقلي' and 'عادي'.
2
Identify the three views
Locate the three views: first two (rational) and third (customary). Note how Sanusi says the first two reduce to one statement.
The first two views both hold that indication is rational, differing only in explanation.
3
Understand the third view
The third view analogizes miracle indication to circumstantial evidence of emotions. Also note its connection to muwaḍa‘a (conventional assignment) and divine speech.
The phrase 'كَدَلالة قرائنِ الأحوالِ' is key.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وظاهر كلام المقترح وكثيرٍ : أنَّ هذين الرأيين يرجعان إلى قول واحد ؛ وهو أنَّ الدلالة عقليَّةٌ ، وإنَّما اختلفا في تقرير كونها عقليَّةً والأمر في هذا قريب (۲) الثالثُ : أنَّ دلالتها عاديَّةٌ : كدَلالة قرائنِ الأحوالِ على خَجَلِ الخَجِلِ ووَجَلِ الوَجِلِ وخوفِ الخائف رجوع دلالة المعجزة بالمواضعة لصفة الكلام ، فلذا يذكرون معها استحالة الكذب على الله تعالى ، فحكم الفعل في المواضعة حكم الكلام الصريح. قالوا : فإنَّ خَلْقَ اللهِ تعالى لهذا الخارقِ على هذا الوجه المفروض يدلُّ على صدقه بالضرورة عادةً فعلى الرأيين الأولين : يستحيلُ عقلاً صدور المعجزاتِ على أيدي الكاذبين : أما على الأَوَّلِ : فلما يلزمُ مِنْ  َنقْضِ الدليل العقليّ ؛ بأنْ يُوجَدَ ولا يُوجَدَ مدلوله ، فيصيرُ ذلكَ الدليلُ شبهةً ، ويصيرُ العلم الذي استلزمَهُ جهلاً مُركَّباً ؛ وذلكَ قلب للحقائق ، ولا خفاءَ باستحالتِهِ وأما على الثاني - وهو المواضعة - : فلما يلزمُ مِنَ الخُلْفِ في خبرِهِ تعالى ؛ لأنَّ حكم المواضعة في الفعل حكم الكلام الصريح. لمَّا كانَ هذا يتوقَّفُ على معرفة استحالة الكذب على الله تعالى . .
And the apparent meaning of the speaker's statement and many others is that these two views reduce to one statement: that the indication is rational, and they only differed in establishing that it is rational, and the matter is close. The third: that its indication is customary: like the indication of circumstantial evidence of the embarrassment of the embarrassed, the fear of the fearful, and the dread of the dreading. The indication of the miracle by muwaḍa‘a returns to the attribute of speech, so they mention along with it the impossibility of lying for God Almighty, so the ruling of the act in muwaḍa‘a is the ruling of explicit speech. They said: For God Almighty's creation of this miracle in this supposed manner necessarily indicates his truthfulness by custom. So according to the first two views: it is rationally impossible for miracles to occur at the hands of liars. As for the first: because of the necessary consequence of breaking the rational proof: that it exists without its referent, so that proof becomes a fallacy, and the knowledge it entailed becomes compound ignorance; that is an inversion of realities, and its impossibility is clear. And as for the second – which is muwaḍa‘a: because of the necessary consequence of breaking God's promise; because the ruling of muwaḍa‘a in the act is the ruling of explicit speech. Since this depends on knowing the impossibility of lying for God Almighty...
Source pages p. 566

Commentary
This passage presents the three views on the dalaala (indication) of miracles and then demonstrates that miracles cannot be performed by liars. The author first notes that two of the views reduce to one: that the indication is rational ('aqliyya). The third view is that it is customary ('ādiyya), like how circumstantial evidence indicates embarrassment or fear. He then connects the indication of miracles by muwaḍa‘a to the attribute of speech, explaining that the act in muwaḍa‘a has the same ruling as explicit speech. Consequently, the creation of a miracle by God indicates the prophet's truthfulness necessarily by custom. Based on the first two views (rational indication and muwaḍa‘a), it is rationally impossible for miracles to occur at the hands of liars. For the first view (rational), a miracle that does not indicate truth would break the rational proof, turning it into a fallacy and causing compound ignorance—an impossibility. For the second view (muwaḍa‘a), allowing a miracle for a liar would entail a breach of God's promise (khulf al-khabar), because the act in muwaḍa‘a is treated like explicit speech, and God cannot lie. The passage ends by stating that this argument depends on establishing the impossibility of lying for God, which introduces the next topic.

Key Terms
الدلالة العقلية
الدلالة العادية
المواضعة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What do the first two views on miracle indication have in common according to Sanusi?
A) They both rely on customary indication.
B) They both reduce to rational indication.
C) They both deny that miracles indicate truth.
D) They both use the concept of muwaḍa‘a.

Key Takeaway
The three views on miracle indication are rational (first two) and customary (third).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read that the customary indication is like contextual clues of embarrassment or fear, does your heart accept that God's signs in your life are as clear as speech? Or do you secretly demand a more dramatic sign? What does that reveal about your trust in God's wisdom?
?
The passage says the two rational views actually agree. Are you someone who gets caught up in minor disputes while missing the core truth? How can you apply this lesson to your own disagreements with others?
?
If miracles indicate truth necessarily by custom, have you ever ignored the clear signs God has placed in your life—like answered prayers or protected moments—because you were waiting for something more obvious? What would it take for you to treat God's ordinary signs as binding evidence?

Why Miracles Cannot Be Performed by Liars
Sanusi proves miracles cannot come from liars using two arguments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational proof that it is impossible for a liar to perform a miracle, based on the first two views.

Core Idea
A miracle from a liar would break God’s promise or invert rational proof.

Having established the nature of miracle indication, Sanusi now proves that miracles cannot come from liars. He gives two arguments corresponding to the first two views: the rational view leads to an inversion of realities, and the muwaḍa‘a view leads to a breach of God’s promise. This lesson unpacks those arguments.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof
Read the Arabic and translation. Note that the proof assumes one of the first two views.
2
First argument (rational view)
Understand why a miracle from a liar would break rational proof and turn knowledge into ignorance.
The phrase 'قلب للحقائق' captures the absurdity.
3
Second argument (muwaḍa‘a view)
Understand why it would entail a breach of God's promise (khulf al-khabar). Relate to the ruling of explicit speech.
4
Connect to the next topic
Note that the proof depends on establishing that lying is impossible for God, which will be covered next.
The passage ends with 'لما كانَ هذا يتوقَّفُ على معرفة استحالة الكذب على الله تعالى'

Refuting Badā': The Analogy of God's Actions
Sanusi refutes the Jewish argument that naskh implies badā' by analogy with God's actions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi refutes the claim that abrogation implies badā' by using an analogy with God's actions in the world.

Core Idea
Abrogation is like changing seasons, not changing God's knowledge.

The claim that abrogation implies badā'—a change in God's mind—is criticized by Sanusi through a powerful analogy. He argues that if changing divine commands were a sign of badā', then God's constant changes in creation (health, wealth, life) would also be badā'. This lesson unpacks that argument and shows why it is logically sound.

Guided Path
1
Understand the claim
Learn what the Jewish argument about badā' is: that abrogation means God discovered new wisdom.
This sets up the refutation.
2
Analyze the analogy
See how Sanusi compares abrogation to God's other actions in creation, like changing health or wealth.
If those don't imply badā', neither does abrogation.
3
Consider the doctor example
Apply the reasoning to a real-life scenario: a doctor changes medicine according to the patient's condition.
This makes the argument intuitive.
4
Grasp the conclusion
Understand that God's actions are beyond question and that our belief is that He does what He wills.
This is the final theological stance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والرد على مَنْ أَحالَ النسخ للبَداءِ أَنْ يُقال : ما تعني بالبَداءِ ؟ إِنْ عنيتَ أنَّ الله تعالى ظهرَ لهُ مِنَ الحكمة ما كان خافياً عليهِ عندَ شرع الحكم الأوَّلِ ولذلك نسخه .. فلا نُسلّمُ لزوم ذلكَ في النسخ ; فإِنَّهُ لَوِ استلزم تصرُّفُهُ في أفعالِ عبادِهِ ; بمنع ما أطلقه في وقت , وإطلاقِ ما منعَهُ في وقتِ البَداء .. لاستلزمَهُ تصرُّفَهُ فيهم بأفعالِهِ ; مِنْ نقلهم مِنَ الصحة إلى المرضِ , ومِنَ المرضِ إلى الصحة , ومِنَ الغنى إلى الفقرِ , ومِنَ الفقرِ إلى الغنى , ومِنَ الحياةِ إلى الموتِ , وإذا لم يدل الثاني فلا يدلُّ الأَوَّلُ , كيفَ ومِنَ المعلوم أنه لا يمتنع في الحكمة أن يأمر الحكيم مريضاً باستعمال الدواء في وقتٍ , ثم ينهاه عنه في وقتِ آخر لعلمِهِ بصلاحِهِ في الحالين ؟! فمِنَ الحكمةِ نهيهم عن القتال في أول الإسلام لقلتِهم , وإيجابُهُ عليهم عندَ كثرتهم ; إذ قال الله تعالى : وَقَدِلُوا الْمُشْرِكِينَ ﴾ [التوبة : ٣٦] , هذا إذا تنزَّلْنا إلى القولِ باعتبار الصلاح والأصلح , وإلا فمُعتقَدنا أنَّ الله تعالى يفعل ما يشاء , ويحكم ما يريدُ , لَا يُسْتَلُ عَمَّا يَفْعَلُ وَهُمْ يُسْتَلُونَ ﴾ [الأنبياء : ٢٣]
And the refutation of those who deem abrogation impossible due to badā' is to say: What do you mean by badā'? If you mean that God, exalted, had wisdom that was hidden from Him at the time of the first ruling and therefore He abrogated it... then we do not accept that this is entailed in abrogation; for if His disposal of His servants' actions—by preventing what He had permitted at one time and permitting what He had prevented at another time—entailed badā', then His disposal of them through His acts—transferring them from health to sickness, from sickness to health, from wealth to poverty, from poverty to wealth, from life to death—would also entail badā'. And if the second does not indicate badā', then the first does not. How could it be, when it is known that it is not impossible in wisdom for a wise person to order a sick person to use medicine at one time, then forbid him from it at another time because of his knowledge of its benefit in both situations? So it is from wisdom to forbid them from fighting at the beginning of Islam due to their small numbers, and to make it obligatory upon them when they became numerous, since God said: 'And fight the polytheists' [al-Tawba: 36]. This is if we descend to the position of considering what is beneficial and most beneficial. Otherwise, our belief is that God does whatever He wills and judges whatever He desires; He is not questioned about what He does, but they will be questioned [al-Anbiyā': 23].
Source pages p. 577

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Jewish argument that abrogation (naskh) implies badā' (a change in God's knowledge or will). Sanusi begins by asking what they mean by badā'. If they mean that God discovered new wisdom that was previously hidden, then he denies that abrogation necessarily entails such a change. He argues by analogy: if God's actions in the world—changing people's states from health to sickness, wealth to poverty, life to death—do not imply badā', then neither does changing His commands. The analogy is that both are acts of God's disposal. He then gives an example from daily life: a wise doctor may order medicine at one time and forbid it later, knowing what is beneficial in each situation. Similarly, God forbade fighting when Muslims were weak and commanded it when they were strong, as in the Quranic verse [9:36]. He concludes that if we accept the notion of God acting for the benefit of creation, then this is consistent; but the correct belief is that God acts according to His will and is not questioned.

Key Terms
بداء (badā')
نسخ (naskh)
صلاح (benefit)
أصلح (most beneficial)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Abrogation means God changed His mind because He realized a mistake.
Correction: Sanusi shows that abrogation is like a doctor adjusting treatment for a patient's changing condition, not a sign of ignorance.

Checkpoint
What analogy does Sanusi use to refute the claim that abrogation implies badā'?
A) A king who changes laws arbitrarily.
B) A doctor who prescribes different medicines at different times for the same patient.
C) A teacher who changes lesson plans.
D) A parent who changes rules for children.

Key Takeaway
Abrogation does not imply badā' because God's change of commands is analogous to His change of physical states, which no one considers a change in His knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a command in the Shari'ah that seems difficult or that you do not understand, do you trust that Allah's wisdom is at work—just as you trust a doctor's advice even when it changes? What would it take for you to submit without demanding an explanation from Him?
?
The passage ends with: 'He is not questioned about what He does, but they will be questioned.' Where in your life do you find yourself questioning Allah's decrees—whether in your personal circumstances or in the rulings of Islam? How will you practice the silence of a servant before His Master?
?
If abrogation shows that Allah's commands are tailored to different times and situations, does that challenge any assumption you hold about the Shari'ah being rigid or outdated? How can you cultivate a deeper trust in the divine wisdom behind every ruling, even those that challenge your modern sensibilities?

Rational Judgments
beginner
6 min
Proving Recreation After Annihilation Possible
Sanusi proves that recreating substances after annihilation is possible.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Sanusi's rational proof that substances and accidents can be recreated after complete annihilation, based on the intrinsic receptivity of their essences.

Core Idea
Recreation is possible because essences remain receptive to existence even after non-existence.

Sanusi now turns to a concrete proof that God can recreate the world after its annihilation. He argues that the very essence of substances and accidents is such that it can receive both existence and non-existence, and that this capacity does not change after they cease to exist. This passage is a model of rational theology: it uses logical necessity and observable reality to establish a point of faith.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text once, then the translation. Notice the repeated phrase 'إنَّما قلنا' which signals each part of the argument.
The passage is dense. Take your time.
2
Identify the main claim
What is Sanusi trying to prove? Find the statement that 'recreation in the first sense is possible.'
That is the conclusion.
3
Follow the argument for essential acceptance
Sanusi argues that acceptance must be essential to avoid infinite regress. Understand why any external cause would lead to a chain.
4
Examine the reductio proofs
Sanusi proves that essences must accept both existence and non-existence by showing the absurdity of accepting only one.
This uses proof of temporality and observation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإنَّما قلنا : إنَّ الإعادة بالمعنى الأول ممكنة؛ لأنَّ ماهية الجواهر والأعراض تقبلُ الوجود والعدم لذاتها؛ لما عرفت أنَّ القبول لا يكون إلا نفسياً، وإلا لزم التسلسل، وذاتها لا تنقلب بعدَ عدمِها فكما قبلَتِ الوجود والعدم ابتداءً . . تقبلهما انتهاءً، وإِنَّما قلنا : إنَّها تقبل الوجود والعدم؛ لأنَّها لو لم تقبل إلا الوجود لكانت قديمة واجبة الوجودِ، وهو باطل؛ لما سبق مِنْ برهان حدوثها، ولو لم تقبل إلا العدمَ لكانَتْ مستحيلة الوجودِ، والعِيانُ يُكذِّبُهُ.
And we only said that recreation in the first sense is possible because the essence of substances and accidents accepts existence and non-existence by itself, since you know that acceptance is only essential (nafsi), otherwise it would lead to infinite regress. Their essence does not change after their non-existence, so just as they accepted existence and non-existence initially, they accept them finally. And we only said that they accept existence and non-existence because if they accepted only existence, they would be eternal and necessary of existence, which is false due to the previously established proof of their temporality. And if they accepted only non-existence, they would be impossible of existence, and observation (al-'iyan) belies that.
Source pages p. 612

Commentary
This passage is a detailed demonstration of the possibility of recreation in the first sense—recreating the identical substances after complete annihilation. The author argues that the essence (mahiyya) of substances and accidents intrinsically accepts both existence and non-existence. This acceptance must be essential (nafsi) to avoid an infinite regress. Since their essence does not change after non-existence, what they accepted initially they can accept again. Then he provides a reductio: if they accepted only existence, they would be eternal and necessary of existence, contradicting their proven temporality (huduth). If they accepted only non-existence, they would be impossible to exist, which contradicts observable reality. Thus, they must accept both, making recreation possible.

Key Terms
ماهية
الجواهر
الأعراض
الوجود
العدم
القبول النفسي
التسلسل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Annihilation changes the essence of substances, making recreation impossible.
Correction: Sanusi argues that the essence does not change after non-existence; it remains receptive to existence and non-existence.

Checkpoint
Why must the acceptance of existence and non-existence by substances be essential (nafsi)?
A) Because they are created.
B) To avoid infinite regress.
C) Because observation requires it.
D) Because of consensus.

Key Takeaway
The possibility of recreation is proven by the essences' intrinsic and unchanging capacity to receive existence and non-existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your essence (the core of who you are) remains unchanged and always receptive to existence, what does that mean for your fear of death? Can you honestly say that you live each day with the certainty that you will be recreated and held accountable?
?
The proof shows that recreation is possible because essences accept both existence and non-existence. Is your heart truly accepting of the reality of the Hereafter, or do you live as if only this world matters? What would it take for you to shift your priorities accordingly?
?
Sanusi uses logical proof to ground his belief in recreation. Do you rely on such certain proofs in your own faith, or do you merely follow what you've been taught? Are you willing to study deeply to strengthen your conviction in the afterlife?

Rational Judgments
intermediate

Proof of the Possibility of Recreating Substances
Rational proof for the possibility of recreating substances.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational argument for the possibility of recreating substances based on their essential receptivity to existence and non-existence.

Core Idea
Substances and accidents are inherently neutral – they can be or not be.

This passage from Sanusi's work presents a rational proof for the possibility of i'ada (recreation) in the first sense: bringing back substances after annihilation. The proof does not rely on scripture but on the ontological nature of substances and accidents. Sanusi argues that their very essence is receptive to both existence and non-existence, and this receptivity remains even after they cease to exist.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its literal translation to get an overall sense of the argument. Notice the key terms and the logical flow.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the main claim
The central claim is that substances and accidents are essentially receptive to both existence and non-existence. This is the foundation for proving the possibility of recreation.
3
Understand why receptivity must be essential
Sanusi argues that if receptivity were accidental, it would lead to an infinite regress. Therefore, it must be part of the essence (necessary). This is a key logical step.
Think about why an accidental quality would require another quality, and so on.
4
Examine the two conditional proofs
Sanusi provides two arguments: if substances only received existence, they would be eternal (contradicting their known temporality); if they only received non-existence, they would be impossible (contradicting observable reality).
These are proofs by contradiction.
5
Conclude the rational basis for recreation
Since substances and accidents are receptive to both, and their essence does not change after non-existence, they can be recreated. This establishes the possibility rationally.
This argument complements scriptural proofs.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإنما قلنا : إن الإعادة بالمعنى الأول ممكنة ؛ لأن ماهية الجواهر والأعراض تقبل الوجود والعدم لذاتها ؛ لما عرفت أن القبول لا يكون إلا نفسياً ، وإلا لزم التسلسل ، وذاتها لا تنقلب بعد عدمها فكما قبلت الوجود والعدم ابتداءً تقبلهما انتهاءً ، وإنما قلنا : إنها تقبل الوجود والعدم ؛ لأنها لو لم تقبل إلا الوجود لكانت قديمة واجبة الوجود ، وهو باطل ؛ لما سبق من برهان حدوثها ، ولو لم تقبل إلا العدم لكانت مستحيلة الوجود ، والعيان يكذبه
And we said: The return in the first sense is possible because the essence of substances and accidents is receptive to existence and non-existence by its own nature, since you know that receptivity is only essential, otherwise an infinite regress would follow. And their essence does not change after their non-existence, so just as they received existence and non-existence initially, they receive them finally. And we said: They receive existence and non-existence because if they received only existence, they would be eternal and necessary of existence, which is false due to the proof of their temporal origination that preceded; and if they received only non-existence, they would be impossible of existence, and direct perception disproves that.
Source pages p. 612

Commentary
This passage is an explanation supporting the possibility of the first meaning of i'ada, which is recreating substances from nothing after their annihilation. The argument is based on the nature of substances (jawhar) and accidents ('arad): they are inherently receptive to both existence and non-existence. This receptivity is essential, not accidental, otherwise it would lead to infinite regress. Since their essence does not change after non-existence, they retain this receptivity. The proof is that if they were not receptive to non-existence, they would be eternal and necessary, which contradicts their known temporality (huduth). Conversely, if they were not receptive to existence, they would be impossible, which contradicts observable reality.

Key Terms
الإعادة بالمعنى الأول
ماهية الجواهر والأعراض
القبول النفسي
الوجود والعدم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The possibility of resurrection is based solely on scripture, not reason.
Correction: This passage shows that rational arguments, based on the nature of substances, also support the possibility of recreation. Reason and revelation are in harmony.

Checkpoint
What would be the consequence if substances received only existence?
A) They would be impossible
B) They would be eternal and necessary
C) They would be temporal
D) None of the above

Key Takeaway
The possibility of recreation is rationally established by the essential receptivity of substances to both existence and non-existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have just read a rational proof that your very substance can be recreated after annihilation. Do you truly believe this, or do you harbor doubt that when you die, that is the end? What would change in how you live today if you were certain that every atom of your being will be returned to face judgment?
?
The proof argues that nothing in creation is inherently permanent—everything is receptive to both existence and non-existence. What attachments do you hold to worldly things that you know will be annihilated? How does this truth challenge your daily priorities and the way you spend your time?
?
If the essence of substances does not change after non-existence, then your identity is not lost at death. How does this affect your view of your own death? Are you preparing for that moment as if you will be exactly the same person, accountable for every action?

Rational Judgments
beginner
4 min
The Rational Possibility of Grave Events and Accepting Scriptural Apparent Meanings
Sanusi clarifies the rational permissibility of reviving parts of the dead and the rule of accepting scriptural apparent meanings.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the events of the grave are rationally possible and why we must accept the transmitted texts about them without forced interpretation.

Core Idea
Reason does not forbid what revelation affirms about the grave.

Sanusi addresses a key issue in the samiyyat: the events of punishment and questioning in the grave. Some might think these are rationally impossible, but the author explains that there is no rational impediment, and since revelation has reported them, we must accept them literally. Let's read his argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage and its literal English translation. Pay attention to the logical flow from possibility to obligation.
2
Identify the rational judgment
Find the statements about what is rationally permissible. Notice that the author begins by saying there is no rational impediment to returning life to some parts.
This sets the stage for accepting the texts.
3
Understand the obligation
After listing the rationally permissible aspects, the author concludes that since the texts have come, we must believe their apparent meaning without straining to interpret them. Reflect on why this is important.
This connects to the broader principle of accepting samiyyat.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم : أنَّهُ لا مانع في العقلِ مِنْ ردَّ الحياةِ إلى بعض أجزائِهِ . ويُجعَلَ لَهُ مِنَ العقل والفهم ما يفهم به ويجيب ، ويدركه الملكان منه ، وإن لم نسمع نحنُ كلامهم ، وكذا يجوزُ أنْ يسمع كلامَ مَنْ سلَّمَ عليه ، وكلُّ ذلك جائز ، وقد وردَ السمعُ بهِ
فوجب اعتقاد ظاهره ، ولا حاجة إلى تكلُّفِ تأويله ، والله تعالى على كلِّ شيءٍ قدير .
And know: There is no rational impediment to returning life to some of his parts. And to give him understanding and comprehension by which he understands and answers, and the two angels perceive him from him, even if we do not hear their speech. Likewise, it is permissible that he hears the speech of one who greets him. All of that is permissible, and the transmitted reports have come with it. So it is obligatory to believe its apparent meaning, and there is no need to burden oneself with interpretation. And God the Exalted is powerful over everything.
Source pages p. 621

Commentary
This passage is a doctrinal clarification by the author (likely Sanusi or his commentator) regarding the mechanism of punishment in the grave. He begins by stating that there is no rational objection (لا مانع في العقل) to God reviving only some parts of the dead person (e.g., the part that hears or speaks) and giving him enough intellect and understanding to comprehend and answer the questions of the two angels. Although we humans do not hear the angels' speech, the dead can perceive them. Similarly, it is possible for the dead to hear the greeting of someone who passes by their grave. All these are rationally possible, and since they are attested in transmitted texts (السمع), we must believe in their literal meaning (ظاهره) without recourse to metaphorical interpretation (تأويل). The passage concludes by affirming God's absolute power over all things, which underlies the possibility of such events. The author is countering those who deny these phenomena on rational grounds.

Key Terms
لا مانع في العقل
رد الحياة
السمع
ظاهر
تأويل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that rational impossibility prevents the dead from sensing or being questioned.
Correction: The author clarifies that reason does not forbid it; rather, such events are rationally possible, and revelation confirms them.

Checkpoint
According to this passage, what is the ruling on accepting the transmitted reports about the dead hearing and answering?
A) They should be interpreted metaphorically.
B) They should be believed in their apparent meaning without unnecessary interpretation.
C) They are rationally impossible and therefore rejected.
D) Only scholars may believe them.

Key Takeaway
The grave events are rationally possible and must be believed in their apparent sense without forced interpretation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever dismissed a clear teaching from the Quran or Sunnah because it seemed rationally impossible to you? What was that teaching, and how does this lesson call you to repent from that arrogance?
?
What comfort does the literal belief in grave events—such as the questioning by angels and the dead hearing greetings—bring to your own fear of death? If you find it difficult to accept, what does that reveal about your reliance on reason over revelation?
?
In what area of your life do you still demand a rational explanation before submitting to Allah's command? How will you practice taking the apparent meaning of a text this week without forcing your own interpretation?
